### [1] [H-Probes: Extracting Hierarchical Structures From Latent Representations of Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00847)
*Cutter Dawes,Aryan Sharma,Angelos Ioannis Lagos,Shivam Raval*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models represent hierarchical structure in their internal activations, using linear probes (H-probes) to extract depth and pairwise distance information and show that this structure is low-dimensional, causally important, and present even in real-world reasoning traces.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While large language models perform well on tasks that require hierarchical reasoning, it is unclear how such hierarchical information is encoded geometrically in their latent representations. The authors aim to fill this gap by analyzing whether and how hierarchy (like tree depth and node distances) is explicitly represented in model activations, moving beyond surface-level syntax or concept representations.

Method: The authors introduce H-probes, a set of linear probes designed to recover hierarchical properties—specifically node depth and pairwise tree distance—from hidden representations of language models. They evaluate these probes on synthetic tree traversal tasks, perform ablations to identify and remove hierarchy-related subspaces, analyze dimensionality and causal importance for task performance, and test probe generalization both within the training distribution and to out-of-domain settings. They also apply similar analyses to real-world hierarchical data such as mathematical reasoning traces.

Result: On synthetic tree traversal tasks, H-probes successfully identify subspaces in model activations that encode hierarchical depth and pairwise distance. These subspaces are found to be low-dimensional, and ablating them significantly degrades task performance, indicating causal importance. The probes generalize well within the training domain and show some degree of transfer out-of-domain. In real-world reasoning contexts like math problem-solving traces, they find analogous but weaker hierarchical structure in the representations.

Conclusion: Large language models encode hierarchical information—such as depth and relational distance—within specific, low-dimensional subspaces of their latent representations, and these subspaces are causally linked to their strong performance on hierarchical reasoning tasks. This hierarchical encoding extends beyond syntactic or conceptual levels to the structure of the reasoning process itself, suggesting that LLMs internally build and manipulate abstract hierarchical representations when solving complex problems.

Abstract: Representing and navigating hierarchy is a fundamental primitive of reasoning. Large language models have demonstrated proficiency in a wide variety of tasks requiring hierarchical reasoning, but there exists limited analysis on how the models geometrically represent the necessary latent constructions for such thinking. To this end, we develop \textit{H-probes}, a collection of linear probes that extract hierarchical structure, specifically depth and pairwise distance, from latent representations. In synthetic tree traversal tasks, the H-probes robustly find the subspaces containing hierarchical structure necessary to complete the tasks; furthermore, in comprehensive ablation experiments, we show that these hierarchy-containing subspaces are low-dimensional, causally important for high task performance, and generalize within- and out-of-domain. Furthermore, we find analogous, though weaker, hierarchical structure in real-world hierarchical contexts such as mathematical reasoning traces. These results demonstrate that models represent hierarchy not only at the level of syntax and concepts, but at deeper levels of abstraction -- including the reasoning process itself.

</details>


### [2] [Model Organisms Are Leaky: Perplexity Differencing Often Reveals Finetuning Objectives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00994)
*Mohammed Abu Baker,Luca Baroni,Dan Wilhelm*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a simple perplexity-gap method to reveal what a fine-tuned “model organism” has been trained to do, by exploiting how it overgeneralizes its fine-tuned behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Finetuning large language models can introduce hidden, harmful, or misaligned behaviors that are difficult to detect, especially when we lack access to model internals or the original checkpoint. Researchers use specially fine-tuned “model organisms” to study such risks, but identifying their hidden objectives is challenging. The paper aims to develop a black-box, scalable way to infer these finetuning objectives.

Method: 1) Sample many short random prefills (prompts) from general text corpora. 2) For each prefill, generate completions from the finetuned model. 3) Compute the perplexity gap (difference in token-level likelihood) between a reference model and the finetuned model on these completions. 4) Rank completions by decreasing perplexity gap; high-gap completions are those the finetuned model strongly prefers relative to the reference. 5) Inspect the top-ranked completions, which tend to contain explicit expressions of the fine-tuned behavior. The approach works with various reference models, even if they are not the exact pre-finetuning checkpoint, and can be applied to API-gated models that return token logprobs.

Result: Across 76 model organisms ranging from 0.5B to 70B parameters—including backdoored models, models fine-tuned on synthetic documents to internalize false facts, adversarially trained models with hidden concerning behaviors, and models with emergent misalignment—the method generally surfaces completions that clearly reveal the finetuning objective among the top-ranked outputs. Models trained via synthetic document finetuning or to emit exact phrases are especially vulnerable to detection. The method remains effective even when using a different, trusted model family as the reference instead of the exact base checkpoint.

Conclusion: Perplexity-gap ranking of random-prompt completions is a simple, black-box technique that can reliably expose the fine-tuning objectives of a wide variety of model organisms. Because it relies only on next-token probabilities and does not require knowledge of model internals or base checkpoints, it is practical for auditing API-gated or proprietary models for hidden finetuned behaviors. This demonstrates both a useful auditing tool and a systematic overgeneralization vulnerability in fine-tuned LLMs.

Abstract: Finetuning can significantly modify the behavior of large language models, including introducing harmful or unsafe behaviors. To study these risks, researchers develop model organisms: models finetuned to exhibit specific known behaviors for controlled experimentation. Identifying these behaviors remains challenging. We show that a simple perplexity-based method can surface finetuning objectives from model organisms by leveraging their tendency to overgeneralize their finetuned behaviors beyond the intended context. First, we generate diverse completions from the finetuned model using short random prefills drawn from general corpora. Second, we rank completions by decreasing perplexity gap between reference and finetuned models. The top-ranked completions often reveal the finetuning objectives, without requiring model internals or prior assumptions about the behavior. We evaluate this on a diverse set of model organisms (N=76, 0.5 to 70B parameters), including backdoored models, models finetuned to internalize false facts via synthetic document finetuning, adversarially trained models with hidden concerning behaviors, and models exhibiting emergent misalignment. For the vast majority of model organisms tested, the method surfaces completions revealing finetuning objectives within the top-ranked results, with models trained via synthetic document finetuning or to produce exact phrases being particularly susceptible. We further show that the technique can be effective even without access to the exact pre-finetuning checkpoint: trusted reference models from different families can serve as effective substitutes. As the method requires only next-token probabilities from the finetuned model, it is compatible with API-gated models that expose token logprobs.

</details>


### [3] [Can AI Debias the News? LLM Interventions Improve Cross-Partisan Receptivity but LLMs Overestimate Their Own Effectiveness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01006)
*Faisal Feroz,Jonas R. Kunst*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether LLMs can debias partisan news headlines to increase cross-partisan trust.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Partisan news undermines trust between political groups; LLMs might scale debiasing but their real impact and their ability to self-evaluate are unknown.

Method: Two preregistered experiments compared human and LLM (silicon) responses to liberal news headlines that were either subtly lexically debiased (Study 1) or substantively reframed to reduce ideological framing (Study 2). Outcomes included perceived trustworthiness, completeness, and willingness to engage, and moderation analyses compared who the LLM predicted would respond to debiasing versus who actually did.

Result: Lexical debiasing in Study 1 had no effect on humans but produced strong effects in LLM-simulated participants. In Study 2, substantive reframing increased conservatives' trust and willingness to engage with liberal news without backfiring among liberals; LLMs showed qualitatively similar but quantitatively inflated effects and mispredicted the psychological profile of those most responsive to debiasing.

Conclusion: LLM-based debiasing can enhance cross-partisan receptivity when it changes ideological framing rather than just tone, but current LLMs are not reliable judges of the size of their effects or of who will be influenced, so human oversight is necessary.

Abstract: Partisan news media erode cross-partisan trust, but large language models (LLMs) offer a potential means of debiasing such content at scale. Across two pre-registered experiments, we tested whether LLM-generated debiasing of liberal news headlines could improve conservative readers' trust-relevant judgments. Study 1 found that subtle lexical debiasing (replacing emotive words with more moderate synonyms) had no effect on any outcome. Study 2 found that a more substantive reframing intervention significantly increased conservatives' perceived trustworthiness, completeness, and willingness to engage with liberal news headlines, without producing a backfire effect among a sample of liberals. In Study 1, the intervention produced robust effects among LLM-simulated silicon participants, whereas it had no impact on human readers. In Study 2, the intervention's effects among silicon participants aligned directionally with human responses but were significantly larger in magnitude for some outcomes. Moderation analyses revealed that the model's implicit theory of who responds to debiasing diverged from the psychological profile that actually predicted human responsiveness. These findings demonstrate that LLM-based debiasing can improve cross-partisan receptivity when targeting ideological framing rather than surface-level language, but that current models lack both the quantitative accuracy and qualitative psychological fidelity to evaluate their own interventions without human oversight.

</details>


### [4] [CLEAR: Revealing How Noise and Ambiguity Degrade Reliability in LLMs for Medicine](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01011)
*Kevin H. Guo,Chao Yan,Avinash Baidya,Katherine Brown,Xiang Goa,Juming Xiong,Zhijun Yin,Bradley A. Malin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes CLEAR, a framework to stress-test medical LLM benchmarks under ambiguity and uncertainty, showing that more realistic answer spaces reveal reliability and humility failures that standard benchmarks hide.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current medical LLM benchmarks use simplified, exam-like questions with clear single correct answers and no realistic ambiguity, which fails to capture how models behave in real clinical settings where multiple plausible options, uncertain ground truth, and abstention are common.

Method: The authors design the CLEAR framework, which systematically perturbs medical multiple-choice benchmarks along three axes: (1) vary the number of plausible answer options, (2) manipulate whether there is a known ground-truth option versus an abstention option, and (3) change the semantic framing of options, especially abstention choices (e.g., assertive "None of the above" vs. uncertain "I don't know"). They apply these perturbations to three existing medical benchmarks and evaluate 17 LLMs under these modified settings.

Result: Across benchmarks and models, performance degrades as more plausible choices are added; models become less able both to select the correct answer and to appropriately abstain when options are incorrect. When abstention is framed as uncertainty admission ("I don't know") instead of assertive rejection, models are even less cautious, and merely including an "I don't know" option increases the rate of incorrect answer selection. The authors define and quantify a "humility deficit"—the gap between correctly identifying the right answer and correctly abstaining from wrong ones—which they show increases with model scale.

Conclusion: Standard medical benchmarks overestimate LLM reliability by ignoring ambiguity, uncertainty, and realistic decision spaces. The CLEAR framework exposes a humility deficit that worsens in larger models, indicating that simply scaling models is insufficient to ensure safe and reliable medical reasoning; evaluation frameworks must explicitly test models' ability to recognize uncertainty and abstain appropriately.

Abstract: Medical large language model (LLM) evaluations rely on simplified, exam-style benchmarks that rarely reflect the ambiguity of real-world medical inquiries. We introduce the CLinical Evaluation of Ambiguity and Reliability (CLEAR) framework, which assesses how decision-space presentation, ambiguity, and uncertainty affect LLMs' reasoning on medical benchmarks. CLEAR systematically perturbs (1) the number of plausible answer options, (2) the presence of a ground truth or abstention option, and (3) the semantic framing of answer options. Applying CLEAR on three benchmarks evaluated across 17 LLMs reveals three notable limitations of existing evaluation methods. First, increasing the number of plausible answers degrades a model's ability to identify the correct answer and abstain against incorrect ones. Second, this lack of caution intensifies as the framing of abstention shifts from assertive rejection like "None of the Above" to uncertainty admission like "I don't know" (IDK). Notably, just including IDK in the answer space increases incorrect answer selections. Lastly, we formalize the performance gap between identifying the correct answer and abstaining from incorrect ones as the humility deficit, which worsens with model scale. Our findings reveal limitations in standard medical benchmarks and underscore that scaling alone does not resolve LLM reliability issues.

</details>


### [5] [Psychologically Potent, Computationally Invisible: LLMs Generate Social-Comparison Triggers They Fail to Detect](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01017)
*Hua Zhao,Jiapei Gu,Michelle Mingyue Gu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents XHS-SCoRE, a reader-grounded benchmark for detecting whether Xiaohongshu posts elicit upward, downward, or neutral social comparison, and shows that while models can learn this signal in-domain, prompt-based LLM classifiers struggle to robustly detect it despite being able to generate impactful content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Social comparison on platforms like Xiaohongshu affects users’ perceived social standing and emotions, but the relational signal of "who is better or worse off" is not the same as sentiment and is hard to detect automatically. Existing LLMs are fluent generators yet may miss subtle, socially meaningful cues, so a dedicated benchmark is needed to evaluate and diagnose their capacity to recognize reader-centered social comparison.


Method: The authors construct XHS-SCoRE, a benchmark of text-only Xiaohongshu posts labeled from a first-person reader perspective as eliciting upward, downward, or neutral social comparison. They evaluate both prompted LLM classifiers and supervised Chinese encoder-based models on this dataset, analyze their error patterns and failure modes, and run a controlled pilot where LLMs generate Xiaohongshu-style posts to test their effect on readers’ perceived standing and comparison-related affect relative to models’ detection performance.


Result: Results show that supervised models can learn the comparison signal in-domain, but prompt-based LLM classifiers are not robust: they often neutralize posts that clearly trigger comparison and show model-specific directional biases in predicting upward vs. downward comparison. Despite this, LLM-generated Xiaohongshu-style posts measurably shift readers’ perceived standing and comparison-related emotions, revealing a gap between generation capability and reliable detection of the same relational construct.


Conclusion: XHS-SCoRE provides a new, reader-grounded benchmark for detecting social comparison in Xiaohongshu posts and demonstrates that socially meaningful relational cues can be behaviorally potent yet only partially accessible to prompt-based LLM inference. The work offers both data and a diagnostic framework for studying when and why LLMs fail to robustly detect relational signals like social comparison, even as they fluently generate content that strongly influences such perceptions.

Abstract: We introduce Xiaohongshu Social Comparison Reader Elicitation (XHS-SCoRE), a reader-grounded benchmark for detecting if a text-only Xiaohongshu (RedNote) post elicits UPWARD, DOWNWARD, or NEUTRAL/no clear social comparison from a first-person reader perspective. The task targets a socially meaningful relational signal that is behaviorally real yet not reducible to sentiment. Across prompted LLM classifiers and supervised Chinese encoder baselines, we find a consistent mismatch between generation fluency and reliable detection ability: the signal is textually learnable in-domain, but not robustly accessible to prompt-based classification. Prompted LLM classifiers exhibit stable, interpretable failure modes, especially neutralization of comparison-triggering posts and model-specific directional skew. A controlled pilot further shows that LLM-generated Xiaohongshu-style posts can shift perceived standing and comparison-related affect even when prompt-based detection of the same construct remains fragile. XHS-SCoRE contributes both a benchmark for reader-grounded comparison detection and a diagnostic framework for studying when socially meaningful relational cues remain only partially visible to prompt-based inference.

</details>


### [6] [A Theoretical Game of Attacks via Compositional Skills](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01034)
*Xinbo Wu,Huan Zhang,Abhishek Umrawal,Lav R. Varshney*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds a game-theoretic framework for adversarial prompting against aligned LLMs, derives theoretically optimal attacks and defenses, and shows that the attack side has inherent advantages; a practical version of the optimal attack outperforms prior methods empirically.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs become more powerful, ensuring their safe deployment is critical, but existing alignment and safety strategies can be bypassed with carefully crafted adversarial prompts. There is a need for a principled, theoretical understanding of the interaction between attackers and defenders in order to understand the limits of current defenses and design stronger, more robust strategies.

Method: The authors formalize the interaction between an adversarial prompter (attacker) and the model’s safety mechanisms (defender) as a game-theoretic setup. Within this framework, they derive a theoretical best-response strategy for the attacker—showing its connections to many known adversarial prompting techniques—and analyze the strategic game to characterize equilibria. From this analysis, they prove certain structural advantages for the attacker and, symmetrically, derive a provably optimal defense strategy. They then instantiate a practical approximate version of the theoretically optimal attack and test it empirically.

Result: Theoretically, the paper identifies that the attacker has inherent advantages in the formulated game and provides closed-form or characterized best-response strategies for both attacker and defender. Empirically, a practical implementation of the theoretically motivated attack is shown to outperform existing adversarial prompting methods across various LLMs and benchmarks, indicating that current defenses are more vulnerable than what prior attacks suggested.

Conclusion: There are fundamental, game-theoretic limitations to current LLM safety defenses, with the attacker enjoying structural advantages in the adversarial prompting game. However, the same theoretical framework also yields an optimal defense strategy in principle. The practical instantiation of the optimal attack achieving stronger empirical performance underscores the urgency of adopting more principled, theoretically grounded defenses for aligned LLMs.

Abstract: As large language models grow increasingly capable, concerns about their safe deployment have intensified. While numerous alignment strategies aim to restrict harmful behavior, these defenses can still be circumvented through carefully designed adversarial prompts. In this work, we introduce a theoretical framework that formalizes a game between an attacker and a defender. Within this framework, we design a theoretical best-response attack strategy and show that it is closely related to many existing adversarial prompting methods. We further analyze the resulting game, characterize its equilibria, and reveal inherent advantages for the attacker. Drawing on our theoretical analysis, we also derive a provably optimal defense strategy. Empirically, we evaluate a practical instantiation of the theoretically optimal attack and observe stronger performance relative to existing adversarial prompting approaches in diverse settings encompassing different LLMs and benchmarks.

</details>


### [7] [Compared to What? Baselines and Metrics for Counterfactual Prompting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01048)
*Zihao Yang,Mosh Levy,Yoav Goldberg,Byron C. Wallace*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that standard counterfactual prompting tests (e.g., flipping patient gender) can misattribute effects to the targeted factor because they ignore baseline sensitivity to harmless wording changes. It proposes a statistical framework that compares the effect of targeted edits to that of paraphrases, showing many claimed effects vanish once general sensitivity is controlled for, though real directional bias can still be detected.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Researchers use counterfactual prompting—changing one factor in an input and measuring model output changes—to study bias, robustness, and causal reasoning. These analyses typically assume that any output change is due to the targeted factor (e.g., gender). The authors argue this is unjustified because any edit also changes surface form, and models may simply be sensitive to benign wording differences. There is a need for a principled way to distinguish true sensitivity to the factor of interest from generic instability to paraphrasing.

Method: The authors conceptualize each counterfactual edit as a compound treatment: the intended variable (e.g., gender) plus incidental surface-form changes. They measure prediction flip rates on MedQA when changing patient gender and when paraphrasing inputs, then statistically compare them. They generalize this into a framework that, for any target intervention, collects: (1) outcomes under targeted edits, (2) outcomes under meaning-preserving paraphrases, and then uses statistical tests to see if the effect of the target exceeds that of paraphrasing. They apply this to MedPerturb (demographics and stylistic cues) and to an occupational biography classification task. They evaluate different effect metrics: aggregate metrics, per-sample distributional metrics, and regression-based analyses, assessing their statistical power and what kinds of effects they reveal.

Result: On MedQA, changing patient gender leads to a 14.9% prediction flip rate, but paraphrasing alone causes a similar 14.1% flip rate; these are statistically indistinguishable, so there is no evidence of special sensitivity to gender. Reanalyzing MedPerturb with the new framework, previously reported sensitivities to demographics and style mostly vanish, with only 5 of 120 tests remaining significant once general sensitivity is controlled for. On the occupational biography task, however, the framework reveals clearly significant directional gender bias, demonstrating that it can still detect real, even small, directional effects. The comparison of metrics shows that per-sample distributional metrics have far greater statistical power than simple aggregate metrics, and regression methods are particularly effective at capturing effect direction and magnitude.

Conclusion: Naïve counterfactual prompting cannot reliably attribute model behavior changes to specific targeted factors because it ignores general model sensitivity to surface-form variation, violating treatment variation irrelevance. A robust evaluation must compare targeted interventions against a baseline of meaning-preserving paraphrasing and use appropriate statistical testing. When this is done, many previously claimed sensitivities (e.g., to patient demographics in MedPerturb) largely disappear, while genuine directional biases can still be detected. Per-sample metrics and regression-based analyses are notably more informative and powerful than aggregate metrics for this purpose.

Abstract: Counterfactual prompting (i.e., perturbing a single factor and measuring output change) is widely used to evaluate things like LLM bias and CoT faithfulness. But in this work we argue that observed effects cannot be attributed to the targeted factor without accounting for baseline ``meaning-preserving'' modifications to text that establish general model sensitivity. This is because every counterfactual edit is a compound treatment that bundles the variable of interest with incidental surface-form variation; this violates treatment variation irrelevance. We observe prediction flip rates on MedQA of 14.9% when we surgically change patient gender. However, this is statistically indistinguishable from the flip rates induced by simply paraphrasing inputs (14.1%). In this case, it would therefore be unwarranted to conclude that the LLM is especially sensitive to patient gender. To account for this and robustly measure the effects of targeted interventions, we propose a framework in which we compare (via statistical testing) differences observed under target interventions to those induced by paraphrasing inputs. We then use this framework to revisit a analysis done on the MedPerturb dataset, which reported evidence of model sensitivity to patient demographics and stylistic cues. We find that these effects largely dissipate when we account for general model sensitivity, with only 5 of 120 tests reaching statistical significance. Applying the same framework to occupational biography classification, we detect clearly significant directional gender bias, showing that the framework identifies real directional effects even when they are small. We evaluate a range of metrics -- aggregate, per-sample distributional, and regression -- and find that per-sample metrics are dramatically more powerful than aggregate metrics and regression powerfully and uniquely characterizes effect direction and magnitude.

</details>


### [8] [A Systematic Exploration of Text Decomposition and Budget Distribution in Differentially Private Text Obfuscation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01065)
*Stephen Meisenbacher,Angelo Kleinert,Florian Matthes*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper systematically studies how different ways of splitting text into chunks and distributing a fixed differential privacy budget over those chunks affect the quality of differentially private text obfuscation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Differentially private text obfuscation aims to make texts indistinguishable from their originals under DP guarantees, but most prior work focuses on word-level perturbation or does not deeply analyze how to allocate a global privacy budget across parts of a full document. There is a need to understand how design choices in text decomposition and budget distribution influence the empirical privacy-utility trade-off for realistic document-level text privatization.

Method: The authors conduct a systematic empirical evaluation of multiple strategies for decomposing texts into smaller units (chunks) and multiple strategies for allocating a fixed global epsilon privacy budget across those units. They combine different chunking schemes with different epsilon allocation policies, apply DP text obfuscation under these settings, and compare the resulting outputs in terms of performance and trade-offs under comparable overall privacy budgets.

Result: The experiments show that the choice of text decomposition and privacy budget distribution strategy has a substantial impact on the outcomes of DP text obfuscation: even when the total privacy budget epsilon is the same, different combinations of chunking and allocation schemes produce significantly different empirical results.

Conclusion: Careful design and optimization of text chunking and privacy budget allocation is crucial for effective differentially private text obfuscation at the document level. The findings provide evidence that practical privacy-utility trade-offs can be improved by deliberately optimizing these aspects of DP obfuscation procedures, rather than treating budget distribution as a secondary detail.

Abstract: The goal of differentially private text obfuscation is to obfuscate, or "perturb", input texts with Differential Privacy (DP) guarantees, such that the private output texts are quantifiably indistinguishable from the originals. While perturbation at the word level is intuitive, meaningful text privatization happens on complete documents. Recent research has laid the groundwork for reasoning about privacy budget distribution, namely, how an overall $\varepsilon$ budget can be sensibly distributed among the component pieces of a text. We perform a systematic evaluation of multiple text decomposition and budget distribution techniques in the context of DP text obfuscation, testing how different methods for chunking texts can be combined with techniques for allocating $\varepsilon$ to these chunks. Our experiments reveal that such design choices are very important, as even with comparable privacy budgets, significantly different results can occur based on which methods are chosen. In this, we provide credible evidence of the feasibility of maximizing empirical trade-offs by optimizing DP obfuscation procedures.

</details>


### [9] [Controlled Paraphrase Geometry in Sentence Embedding Space: Local Manifold Modeling and Latent Probing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01073)
*Leonid Bedratyuk*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes the local geometric structure of sentence embedding spaces for tightly controlled paraphrase-like sentence variants, modeling them with low-degree polynomial surfaces and probing these fitted manifolds using synthetic points.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how small, paraphrase-like semantic variations are organized in sentence embedding spaces and whether this local structure can be explicitly captured and analyzed using simple geometric models, rather than treating embeddings as opaque vectors.

Method: They collect controlled sets of semantically close sentences and examine their embeddings as local “clouds” in representation space. They fit affine, quadratic, and cubic models (carriers) to these local clouds and perform PCA-based dimensionality reduction to define a local coordinate system. On top of the fitted surfaces, they define a surface-based latent probing procedure that generates synthetic latent points constrained to lie near the fitted carrier. They then evaluate these synthetic points against multiple criteria: geometric consistency with the fitted surface, preservation of local neighborhood structure, agreement with the empirical distribution of observed points, and stability of second-order (Hessian-based) shape descriptors and fitted-model coefficients. They also test how using these synthetic points affects downstream classification tasks.

Result: Nonlinear (quadratic and cubic) local models fit the embedding clouds of semantically close sentences better than simple affine models, capturing curvature and higher-order structure. The surface-based generation procedure yields synthetic points that strongly respect the learned local geometry, as evidenced by good surface consistency, stable Hessian-based shape descriptors, and consistent model coefficients. However, these geometrically valid synthetic points do not systematically improve performance in downstream classification tasks.

Conclusion: Local sentence embedding geometry around clusters of semantically close sentences is better described by nonlinear polynomial surfaces than by purely affine subspaces, and these structures can be used for principled geometry-aware latent probing and analysis. Nonetheless, faithfully modeling local geometry does not guarantee better discriminative performance, underscoring a distinction between geometric validity and task utility. The authors also provide CoPaGE-300K, a large, controlled dataset of paraphrase-like sentence variants with annotations and embeddings to support further study of local embedding geometry.

Abstract: The paper studies the local geometry of embedding clouds induced by \emph{controlled local classes of semantically close sentences}. The central question is how controlled paraphrase-like semantic variation is organized in sentence embedding space and whether this local structure can be explicitly modeled by low-degree fitted carriers.
  We introduce a local geometric modeling scheme based on affine, quadratic, and cubic fitted models. We also use a surface-based latent probing procedure that constructs synthetic latent points in a reduced local PCA space with respect to the fitted carrier. The procedure is intended as an offline method for representation-space analysis, local manifold modeling, and geometry-aware latent probing.
  Generated latent points are evaluated using criteria that measure consistency with the fitted surface, preservation of neighborhood structure, agreement with the empirical distribution, stability of Hessian-based second-order shape descriptors, and stability of fitted-model coefficients. Experiments on controlled sets of semantically close sentences show that nonlinear local models describe embedding clouds more accurately than affine models. Surface-based generation provides strong fitted-geometry fidelity, including surface consistency, Hessian-based shape consistency, and coefficient consistency.
  Downstream experiments show that geometric validity of synthetic latent points does not automatically translate into improved classification performance. The results support explicit local geometric modeling of sentence embedding space and highlight the need to distinguish geometric validity from discriminative utility. As a resource contribution, we introduce \textbf{CoPaGE-300K}, a controlled template-based dataset of semantically close sentence variants with slot-level annotations and precomputed sentence embeddings.

</details>


### [10] [Teaching LLMs Brazilian Healthcare: Injecting Knowledge from Official Clinical Guidelines](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01077)
*Hugo Abonizio,Filipe Rocha Lopes,Roberto Lotufo,Rodrigo Nogueira*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper adapts Qwen2.5-14B to Brazilian clinical guidelines using synthetic data and RL, and builds new Portuguese clinical benchmarks where the adapted model beats larger frontier LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Brazil’s public health system depends on official clinical guidelines, but existing LLMs perform poorly on this specialized, Brazilian-Portuguese content and no benchmark exists to evaluate guideline-grounded recall. The authors want a strong, open, and locally aligned clinical model plus high-quality evaluation sets for SUS protocols.

Method: They collect 178 Brazilian official clinical guidelines (~5.4M tokens) and use four different LLMs to generate ~70M tokens of synthetic training data in three styles: guideline rephrasings, wiki-like explanatory articles, and QA pairs. They perform continual pre-training of Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct on this corpus, then apply Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) for reinforcement learning. They also construct two benchmarks: HealthBench-BR with 1,780 balanced true/false clinical statements, and PCDT-QA with 890 open-ended clinical questions evaluated by an LLM judge.

Result: The adapted 14B-parameter model reaches 83.9% accuracy on HealthBench-BR and 85.4% on PCDT-QA, surpassing GPT-5.2, Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 3.1 Pro, and Google AI Overview’s web-grounded RAG. Ablation studies indicate that using multiple generator models and the RL phase (GRPO) are key contributors to performance gains.

Conclusion: Domain adaptation of a mid-sized open model with diverse synthetic data and RL can surpass much larger frontier models on Brazilian clinical guideline tasks. The authors provide open datasets, benchmarks, and model weights, establishing a reproducible foundation for clinical NLP in Brazilian Portuguese and enabling better SUS guideline-grounded AI tools.

Abstract: Brazil's Unified Health System (SUS) relies on official clinical guidelines that define diagnostic criteria, treatments, dosages, and monitoring procedures for over 200 million citizens. Yet current LLMs perform poorly on this guideline-specific knowledge, and no benchmark evaluates clinical recall grounded in Brazilian Portuguese protocols. We address this gap by adapting Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct to the Brazilian clinical domain. From 178 official guidelines (~5.4M tokens), we generate ~70M tokens of synthetic data in three formats -- rephrases, wiki-style articles, and question-answer pairs -- using four generator LLMs. We then apply continual pre-training followed by Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO). We introduce HealthBench-BR, with 1,780 balanced true/false clinical assertions, and PCDT-QA, with 890 open-ended clinical questions scored by an LLM judge. Our best model achieves 83.9% on HealthBench-BR and 85.4% on PCDT-QA, outperforming GPT-5.2, Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 3.1 Pro, and Google AI Overview's web-grounded RAG despite having only 14B parameters. Ablations show that generator diversity and reinforcement learning are critical to these gains. We release all datasets, benchmarks, and model weights to support reproducible clinical NLP research for Brazilian Portuguese. Code, data, and model weights are available at https://github.com/hugoabonizio/clinical-protocols-br

</details>


### [11] [Interpretable Difficulty-Aware Knowledge Tracing in Tutor-Student Dialogues](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01097)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build an LLM-based, interpretable, difficulty-aware knowledge tracing framework for tutoring dialogues and show it beats baselines while staying aligned with cognitive theory.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Current dialogue-based knowledge tracing methods neglect explicit question difficulty and depend on black-box LLM embeddings, which hurts both prediction accuracy and interpretability.

Method: They design a conversational knowledge tracing framework on top of LLMs that, at every tutor–student turn, explicitly models (1) the student’s ability and (2) the difficulty of the tutor’s task. The model uses the current question text and the upcoming tutor task to estimate the student’s knowledge state and next-turn difficulty, and integrates Item Response Theory to convert LLM outputs into interpretable parameters for student ability and item difficulty.

Result: On two tutor–student dialogue datasets, their framework quantitatively outperforms existing knowledge tracing baselines and, in qualitative analyses, produces explanations and parameter estimates that align with cognitive learning theory.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling student ability and task difficulty within an LLM-based conversational KT framework, grounded in Item Response Theory, yields more accurate and interpretable predictions of student performance in tutoring dialogues than prior latent, difficulty-agnostic approaches.

Abstract: Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have led to the development of AI-powered tutoring systems that provide interactive support via dialogue. To enable these tutoring systems to provide personalized support, it is essential to assess student performance at each turn, motivating knowledge tracing (KT) in dialogue settings. However, existing dialogue-based KT approaches often ignore question difficulty modeling and rely on opaque latent representations from LLMs, hindering accurate and interpretable prediction. In this work, we propose an interpretable difficulty-aware conversational KT framework built upon LLMs, which explicitly models students' abilities and the difficulty of tutor-posed tasks at each turn. The framework incorporates the original textual question and the next tutor-posed task to estimate the student's knowledge state and the difficulty of the upcoming turn. Furthermore, it integrates Item Response Theory to map LLM's outputs into student ability and question difficulty parameters, enabling interpretable prediction of student performance grounded in cognitive theories of learning. We evaluate the framework on two tutor-student dialogue datasets. Both quantitative and qualitative results show that our framework outperforms existing KT baselines, meanwhile generating interpretable outputs consistent with cognitive theory.

### [12] [Quantifying and Predicting Disagreement in Graded Human Ratings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01168)
*Leixin Zhang,Çağrı Çöltekin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how much human annotators disagree when labeling inappropriate language and whether this disagreement (and a new opposition metric) can be predicted from text alone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human annotations for inappropriate language (offensive, hate, toxic) often show disagreement, but the degree and nature of that variation across items is not uniform or well understood. Current models typically ignore this variability, assuming a single ground truth label. There is a need to characterize and predict when people will diverge, including cases of strong opposing views, to better model subjectivity and uncertainty in harmful language detection.

Method: The authors analyze graded human ratings on inappropriate language tasks (offensive, hate, toxic). They quantify annotation variation using variance and introduce a new metric, the Opposition Index, to capture how strongly annotators’ opinions are split on an item. They then train models to predict (a) the variance of annotations directly from textual features and (b) the full annotation distribution from which variance can be derived, and compare these approaches. They also study how well models can predict items with high Opposition Index values.

Result: There is a moderate positive correlation between model-estimated and observed annotation variance, indicating that disagreement is partially predictable from text. Direct prediction of variance and indirect estimation via predicted annotation distributions perform similarly on variance prediction. For opposition prediction, instances with high Opposition Index (strongly split annotator opinions) are harder for models to predict and the models tend to underestimate their opposition level.

Conclusion: Disagreement among annotators in inappropriate language tasks is not random; its degree is somewhat predictable from textual features. Both direct and distribution-based modeling approaches can approximate annotation variance similarly. However, highly contentious items with strong opposing perspectives remain challenging and are systematically underestimated by models. The proposed Opposition Index provides a useful tool to study and potentially incorporate perspective conflict into future annotation and modeling frameworks.

Abstract: It is increasingly recognized that human annotators do not always agree, and such disagreement is inherent in many annotation tasks. However, not all instances in a given task elicit the same degree of opinion divergence. In this paper, we investigate annotation variation patterns in graded human ratings for inappropriate languages, including offensive language, hate speech, and toxic language perception. We examine whether the degree of annotation disagreement can be predicted from textual features. We further propose the Opposition Index, a metric that quantifies perspective opposition among annotators on a given item, and investigate the predictability of instances with potentially opposing human opinions. Our results show a moderate positive correlation between estimated and observed annotation variance. We find that two approaches achieve comparable performance in variance prediction: directly predicting the variance value and estimating it from predicted annotation distributions. Our results on opposition perspective prediction show that items with high opposition index values are more difficult to predict and are often underestimated by models.

### [13] [Compute Optimal Tokenization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01188)
*Tomasz Limisiewicz,Artidoro Pagnoni,Srini Iyer,Mike Lewis,Sachin Mehta,Alisa Liu,Margaret Li,Gargi Ghosh,Luke Zettlemoyer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how token information granularity, controlled via compression rate (bytes per token), impacts scaling laws of language models, finding that compute-optimal model size scales with data measured in bytes rather than tokens and that optimal compression rate is lower than standard BPE and decreases with compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing scaling laws optimize model and data sizes assuming a fixed tokenization and measure data in tokens, but the effect of the token definition itself (via compression rate) on these laws is not well understood. The authors aim to clarify how token granularity influences compute-optimal trade-offs and whether common BPE tokenizers are optimal for scaling.

Method: They introduce and train 988 BLT (latent tokenized) language models between 50M and 7B parameters, where the compression rate (average bytes of text per token) can be controlled. This lets them sweep a wide range of compression rates, including beyond the typical ~4.57 bytes/token of standard BPEs. They then analyze performance, scaling curves, and compute-optimal configurations across model sizes, compression rates, tokenization schemes (latent vs. subword), and multiple languages.

Result: They find that, under compute-optimal scaling, the number of model parameters grows in proportion to the amount of data measured in bytes, not tokens, contrary to common interpretations of prior scaling laws. They also show that the compression rate that yields the best compute efficiency is not the one induced by standard BPE tokenizers and that this optimal compression rate decreases as available compute increases. These patterns hold across different tokenization paradigms and languages.

Conclusion: Data size should be conceptualized and optimized in terms of raw bytes rather than token counts when applying scaling laws, and tokenization should be tuned for compression rate rather than assumed fixed (e.g., BPE). As compute budgets grow, using finer-grained tokenizations (lower compression rates) becomes more optimal. These insights provide practical guidance for choosing or designing tokenizers to achieve better compute efficiency in language model training across languages and tokenization methods.

Abstract: Scaling laws enable the optimal selection of data amount and language model size, yet the impact of the data unit, the token, on this relationship remains underexplored. In this work, we systematically investigate how the information granularity of tokens, controlled by the compression rate (i.e., average bytes of text per token), affects scaling trends. We train 988 latent tokenized models (BLT) ranging from 50M to 7B parameters that enable setting the desired compression rate. This flexibility allows us to study the role of compression rate well beyond 4.57 bytes per token obtained with a popular BPE tokenizer. Our experiments reveal that in compute-optimal configurations, model parameter counts scale proportionally to data size measured in bytes, not in tokens as commonly perceived (Kaplan et al., 2020; Hoffmann et al., 2022). Furthermore, we discover that the optimal compression rate differs from the one obtained with BPE and decreases with compute. These findings generalize to both latent and subword tokenization, as well as to languages other than English, guiding language model developers on tokenization scheme selection for maximal compute efficiency.

</details>


### [14] [SRA: Span Representation Alignment for Large Language Model Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01205)
*Quoc Phong Dao,Hoang Son Nguyen,Pham Khanh Chi,Tung Nguyen,Linh Ngo Van,Nguyen Thi Ngoc Diep,Trung Le*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose SRA, a span-based, physically-inspired framework for cross-tokenizer knowledge distillation that outperforms prior CTKD methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing cross-tokenizer knowledge distillation methods rely on brittle token-level alignment, which struggles when tokenizers differ significantly. The authors want a more robust, tokenizer-agnostic representation for aligning teacher and student models during distillation.

Method: They introduce SRA, which shifts alignment from tokens to spans. Each span is modeled as a cluster of particles, with its state represented by a center of mass (CoM): an attention-weighted average over token representations. They use attention-derived weights to focus on salient spans, add a geometric regularizer to maintain structural relationships in the representation space, and perform distillation using aligned span logits between teacher and student.

Result: In cross-architecture, cross-tokenizer distillation experiments, SRA consistently and significantly outperforms state-of-the-art CTKD baselines.

Conclusion: Physically-grounded, span-based representation alignment (using centers of mass, geometric regularization, and span logit distillation) provides a more robust and effective approach to cross-tokenizer knowledge distillation than token-level alignment alone.

Abstract: Cross-Tokenizer Knowledge Distillation (CTKD) enables knowledge transfer between a large language model and a smaller student, even when they employ different tokenizers. While existing approaches mainly focus on token-level alignment strategies, which are often brittle and sensitive to discrepancies between tokenizers, we argue that the method of aggregating tokens into more robust representations before distillation is of equal importance. In this paper, we introduce \textbf{SRA} (\textbf{S}pan \textbf{R}epresentation \textbf{A}lignment for Large Language Model Distillation), a novel framework that reframes CTKD through the physical lens of Multi-Particle Dynamical Systems. SRA shifts the fundamental unit of alignment from tokens to robust, tokenizer-agnostic spans. We model each span as a cluster of particles and represent its state by its Center of Mass (CoM) - an attention-weighted average that captures rich semantic information. We leverage the concept of span centers of mass with attention-derived weighting to prioritize the most salient spans. In addition, we employ a geometric regularizer to preserve the structural integrity of the representation space and introduce aligned span logit distillation to enhance knowledge transfer across models. In challenging cross-architecture distillation experiments, SRA consistently and significantly outperforms state-of-the-art CTKD baselines, validating our physically-grounded approach.

### [15] [Lost in the Tower of Babel: The Adverse Effects of Incidental Multilingualism in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01224)
*Anjishnu Mukherjee,Chutong Meng,Antonios Anastasopoulos*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper criticizes current large language models for having only incidental, uneven multilingual abilities and proposes a new agenda of “multilingualism by design.”

  <summary>Details</summary>

This is especially problematic when LLMs are used in real-world, agentic settings where consistent performance across languages is critical. The authors want to systematically examine and expose these shortcomings and motivate the need for principled multilingual design.

Method: The authors conduct an empirical study focused on two practical questions: (1) which languages LLMs claim to support (self-reported support), and (2) which languages they actually respond in when given multilingual prompts. They also design a simple language-change attack to probe and reveal failures and hidden assumptions about language in LLM-based systems.

Result: The study finds mismatches between languages that models say they support and the languages they actually use in responses, demonstrating brittle, unequal multilingual behavior. The language-change attack further exposes systematic failures and implicit assumptions in how LLMs and LLM-based systems handle language choice and transitions.

Conclusion: Incidental multilingualism in current LLMs leads to unfair, brittle, and opaque behavior across languages. The authors argue that the field should move to “multilingualism by design,” making equitable multilingual performance, cultural grounding, and cross-lingual behavioral understanding explicit, first-class goals throughout data collection, model training, evaluation, and deployment.

Abstract: This paper argues that contemporary multilingual NLP has converged on a fragile and misleading paradigm of incidental multilingualism. Today's LLMs appear multilingual largely because they are trained on massive, uneven web corpora, not because multilingual or multicultural competence has been treated as a core design objective. We contend that this paradigm systematically produces unequal, brittle, and opaque behavior across languages, with severe consequences in real-world and agentic deployments where models must reason, plan, and act across multiple linguistic contexts. We report a focused empirical study of two practical questions: which languages models self-report as supported and which languages they actually respond in across multilingual prompts. We additionally demonstrate how even a simple language-change attack can surface these failures and expose hidden assumptions about language in LLM-based systems. To address this, we call for a shift toward multilingualism by design: a research agenda that treats equitable multilingual performance, cultural grounding, and cross-lingual behavioral understanding as first-class goals in all aspects of the model pipeline.

### [16] [GIFT: Guided Fine-Tuning and Transfer for Enhancing Instruction-Tuned Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01256)
*Zhiwen Ruan,Yichao Du,Jianjie Zheng,Longyue Wang,Yun Chen,Peng Li,Jinsong Su,Yang Liu,Guanhua Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GIFT is a guided fine-tuning framework that uses an instruction-tuned model’s confidence to train adapters on a base model, then merges them back to get task-specialized yet generally capable models, outperforming standard fine-tuning and transfer baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing task adaptation methods for instruction-tuned LMs typically learn task-specific updates on a base model and merge them into the instruction-tuned model, but they treat the instruction-tuned model as a passive target, not leveraging it to guide learning. This can limit task performance and preservation of instruction-following capabilities.

Method: Introduce GIFT (Guided Fine-Tuning and Transfer): (1) start from a pretrained base language model plus an instruction-tuned counterpart; (2) fine-tune a low-rank adapter on the base model, but use confidence signals from the instruction-tuned model as guidance during training (e.g., to reweight or shape the learning signal); (3) after training, merge the learned adapter parameters into the instruction-tuned model, producing a task-specialized instruction follower. Evaluate on math and knowledge-intensive tasks across several model families and sizes, and compare against direct fine-tuning and other transfer-based methods.

Result: Across mathematical and knowledge-intensive benchmarks, and for multiple model families and scales, GIFT achieves higher task performance than direct fine-tuning and representative transfer-based baselines, while also demonstrating better generalization properties and more favorable scaling with test-time compute.

Conclusion: Guiding task adaptation using an instruction-tuned model’s confidence signals during adapter training, then merging those adapters into the instruction-tuned model, is an effective and efficient way to obtain task-specialized models that retain general instruction-following behavior and outperform existing adaptation approaches.

Abstract: A promising paradigm for adapting instruction-tuned language models is to learn task-specific updates on a pretrained base model and subsequently merge them into the instruction-tuned model. However, existing approaches typically treat the instruction-tuned model as a passive target that is only involved at the final merging stage, without guiding the training process. We propose GIFT (Guided Fine-Tuning and Transfer), a simple and efficient framework that incorporates guidance from the instruction model into task adaptation. GIFT fine-tunes a low-rank adapter on the pretrained base model using confidence signals derived from the instruction-tuned model. The learned adapter is then merged into the instruction-tuned model, yielding task-specialized models that preserve general instruction-following behavior. We evaluate GIFT on mathematical and knowledge-intensive benchmarks across multiple model families and scales. Results show that GIFT consistently outperforms direct fine-tuning and representative transfer-based baselines, while maintaining robust generalization and favorable test-time scaling behavior.

*Ahmed Alfey Sani,Kazi Akib Zaoad,Shefayat E Shams Adib,Md Abdul Muqtadir,Ajwad Abrar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tackles Bangla fake news detection in low-resource settings by using LLM-based data augmentation to balance imbalanced datasets, improving F1 performance and releasing synthetic data for the community.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bangla, like many under-resourced languages, suffers from limited and imbalanced labeled datasets for fake news detection, which constrains the performance and robustness of classifiers. Existing work has focused mainly on high-resource languages, and LLM-based augmentation for Bangla fake news has barely been explored. There is a need to understand whether modern instruction-tuned LLMs can generate useful synthetic Bangla news that alleviates data scarcity and imbalance while preserving label quality, thereby supporting more reliable misinformation detection.

Method: The authors design a systematic LLM-driven augmentation framework. They use the instruction-tuned Gemma 3 27B IT model to generate synthetic Bangla news articles, targeting primarily the minority (fake) class. Generated samples are passed through semantic filtering and controlled subsampling to maintain label consistency and content diversity. They compare zero-shot versus few-shot prompting, explore different augmentation ratios, and test random versus similarity-based strategies for selecting which original minority samples to augment.

Result: The experiments reveal that augmenting only the minority (fake news) class is more effective than augmenting both classes, and that higher augmentation rates combined with random subsampling produce the best gains. Specifically, the Fake News F1 score improves from 0.85 on the original imbalanced data to 0.88 with their augmentation strategy. They also create and release a synthetic dataset of 4,545 Bangla fake news samples plus the associated codebase to facilitate reproducibility and further work.

Conclusion: The study concludes that carefully designed LLM-based augmentation can meaningfully improve fake news detection in low-resource languages like Bangla, even when starting from small and imbalanced datasets. Their framework—using an instruction-tuned LLM, semantic filtering, and targeted augmentation of the minority class—offers a practical approach for enhancing classifier performance and can serve as a foundation for broader multilingual misinformation research. Public release of the synthetic data and implementation is intended to spur further advances in this area.

Abstract: The growing spread of misinformation in digital media highlights the need for reliable fake news detection systems, yet progress in under-resourced languages such as Bangla is limited by small and imbalanced datasets. This study investigates whether Large Language Model (LLM) based augmentation can effectively address this limitation and improve Bangla fake news classification. Existing datasets remain valuable but highly imbalanced, limiting model performance, and LLM based augmentation for Bangla has been scarcely explored. To fill this gap, we propose a systematic augmentation framework that generates synthetic Bangla news articles using the instruction tuned Gemma 3 27B IT model, supported by semantic filtering and controlled subsampling to preserve label consistency and diversity. We compare zero shot and few shot prompting, evaluate multiple augmentation rates, and examine random versus similarity-based selection strategies. Our experiments show that augmenting only the minority class with a high augmentation rate and random subsampling yields the strongest gains, raising the Fake News F1 score from 0.85 to 0.88. To support reproducibility and further research in this low-resource domain, we publicly release 4,545 synthetically generated Bangla fake news samples along with our full implementation. These findings demonstrate that well-designed LLM-driven augmentation can significantly improve fake news detection in low resource settings and provide a practical foundation for advancing multilingual misinformation research.

### [18] [Beyond Semantic Relevance: Counterfactual Risk Minimization for Robust Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01302)
*Peiyang Liu,Qiang Yan,Ziqiang Cui,Di Liang,Xi Wang,Wei Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies a failure mode in standard RAG—over-focusing on semantic relevance leads to reinforcing users’ biased or incorrect assumptions—and proposes CoRM-RAG, a causally motivated training and scoring framework that prioritizes decision safety and robustness over mere similarity, yielding more reliable, bias-resilient retrieval and better abstention in adversarial decision-making tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most RAG systems optimize for semantic similarity between query and documents, assuming that more relevant-looking evidence is more useful. In real decision-making scenarios, user queries can embed cognitive biases (false premises, confirmation bias, etc.). In these cases, maximizing relevance often surfaces evidence that confirms the user’s bias, worsening hallucinations and unsafe decisions. The authors formalize this mismatch as the Relevance-Robustness Gap and aim to develop a retrieval framework that is robust to biased, adversarial queries and aligned with decision safety rather than just topical relevance.

Method: They propose CoRM-RAG (Counterfactual Risk Minimization for RAG). The core idea is to use causal intervention-inspired training: (1) define a Cognitive Perturbation Protocol that systematically perturbs user queries to simulate cognitive biases and adversarial conditions; (2) during training, evaluate how different documents affect the downstream model’s correctness under these perturbations; (3) distill this information into a lightweight Evidence Critic—a scoring module that, given a query-document pair, predicts the document’s ability to guide the model toward correct answers even when the query is biased. This critic replaces or augments standard similarity-based scoring to re-rank or filter retrieved documents according to robustness/decision safety rather than pure semantic similarity.

Result: On decision-making benchmarks specifically designed or adapted to include adversarial or bias-infused queries, CoRM-RAG outperforms strong dense retrievers and LLM-based rerankers. The gains are most pronounced in adversarial settings where standard relevance-based methods tend to be sycophantic and amplify hallucinations. Additionally, CoRM-RAG’s robustness scores correlate well with actual error risk, enabling more effective risk-aware abstention (i.e., choosing not to answer when evidence is insufficient or conflicting).

Conclusion: The paper concludes that conventional relevance-optimized RAG is fundamentally misaligned with robust, safe decision-making in the presence of biased user queries. By framing retrieval as counterfactual risk minimization and explicitly training against cognitively perturbed queries, CoRM-RAG closes much of the Relevance-Robustness Gap. The introduced Evidence Critic provides a practical, lightweight way to favor documents that can correct or withstand query biases, improving both answer quality and the system’s ability to abstain when needed. The framework offers a principled path to more trustworthy, bias-resilient RAG systems.

Abstract: Standard Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems predominantly rely on semantic relevance as a proxy for utility. However, this assumption collapses in realistic decision-making scenarios where user queries are laden with cognitive biases, such as false premises or confirmation bias. In such cases, maximizing relevance paradoxically promotes the retrieval of sycophantic evidence that reinforces hallucinations, a critical failure we term the ``Relevance-Robustness Gap''. To bridge this gap, we propose CoRM-RAG (Counterfactual Risk Minimization for RAG), a framework that aligns retrieval with decision safety rather than mere similarity. Grounded in causal intervention, we introduce a Cognitive Perturbation Protocol to simulate user biases during training, which is then distilled into a lightweight Evidence Critic. This scoring module learns to identify documents that possess sufficient evidential strength to steer the model toward correctness despite adversarial query perturbations. Extensive experiments on decision-making benchmarks demonstrate that CoRM-RAG significantly outperforms strong dense retrievers and LLM-based rerankers in adversarial settings, while enabling effective risk-aware abstention through reliable robustness scoring. Our code is available at https://github.com/PeiYangLiu/CoRM-RAG.git.

</details>

### [22] [Creating and Evaluating Figurative Language Dataset for Sindhi](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01323)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors collect raw Sindhi text from blogs, social media, and literary sources, then preprocess it into a corpus. Two native speakers annotate the data using Doccano for figurative language classification, achieving an inter-annotator agreement of 0.81. They then establish baseline classification results with 5-fold and 10-fold cross-validation, and evaluate several multilingual transformer-based models (mBERT, XLM-RoBERTa, XLM-RoBERTa-XL) as well as SetFit for few-shot fine-tuning of sentence transformers.

Result: The annotated SiNFluD dataset is produced with substantial inter-annotator agreement (0.81). Baseline performance metrics are obtained using cross-validation. Among the evaluated models, the pretrained XLM-RoBERTa-XL achieves the highest performance on Sindhi figurative language classification, outperforming mBERT, XLM-RoBERTa, and SetFit-based approaches.

Conclusion: SiNFluD provides a valuable new benchmark for Sindhi figurative language classification and demonstrates that large multilingual transformers, particularly XLM-RoBERTa-XL, are effective for this task. The dataset and baseline results can serve as a foundation for future work in figurative language understanding for low-resource languages.

Abstract: In this article, we introduce SiNFluD, a novel benchmark dataset for Sindhi figurative language classification. We first collect raw text from various blogs, social media platforms, and literary sources, and subsequently prepare the corpus for annotation. Two native annotators label the data using the Doccano text annotation tool, achieving an inter-annotator agreement of 0.81. We then establish baseline results using 5-fold and 10-fold cross-validation. Finally, we evaluate mBERT, XLM-RoBERTa, and XLM-RoBERTa-XL models, along with SetFit for few-shot fine-tuning of sentence transformers. Among these, the pretrained XLM-RoBERTa-XL achieves the best performance.

### [25] [LLM Output Detectability and Task Performance Can be Jointly Optimized](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01350)

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Watermarking is a leading method to make LLM outputs detectable, but it biases token distributions in a way that often degrades performance on real tasks. There is a need for a method that preserves or improves downstream quality while still making machine-generated text reliably detectable, and that is efficient to train and robust across settings and attacks.

Method: They fine-tune LLMs with reinforcement learning using two reward signals: (1) a detector that estimates the likelihood that a text is machine-generated, encouraging outputs that are easier to classify as such, and (2) a task-specific evaluator that scores downstream quality (e.g., QA accuracy, summarization quality, essay writing quality). The PUPPET framework jointly optimizes these rewards, using relatively small datasets and modest compute, and is evaluated across multiple tasks, model families, and sizes, including robustness checks against paraphrasing attacks.

Result: Across long-form QA, summarization, and essay writing, PUPPET-trained models reach detectability levels comparable to watermark-based methods while delivering better downstream performance. The optimization converges with only a few thousand training samples and 1–2 GPU hours. The improvements generalize to out-of-domain tasks, different LLM architectures and scales, and maintain detectability even after adversarial paraphrasing of the generated text.

Conclusion: PUPPET demonstrates that it is possible to jointly optimize for detectability and task quality in LLM outputs, overcoming the performance drawbacks of traditional watermarking. The approach is computationally efficient, generalizes well across tasks and model families, and is resilient to paraphrasing attacks, making it a practical alternative or complement to watermarking-based detection schemes.

Abstract: Detecting machine-generated text is essential for transparency and accountability when deploying large language models (LLMs). Among detection approaches, watermarking is a statistically reliable method by design -- it embeds detectable signals into LLM outputs by biasing their token distributions. However, it has been reported that watermarked LLMs often perform worse on downstream tasks. We propose PUPPET, a framework that fine-tunes an LLM via reinforcement learning to generate text that is both more detectable and better performing on downstream tasks. We use two reward functions: a detector that outputs a machine-class likelihood and an evaluator that measures a task-specific metric. Experiments on long-form QA, summarization, and essay writing show that LLMs trained with PUPPET achieve high detectability competitive with watermarking methods while outperforming them on downstream tasks. The analysis shows that this optimization can be performed efficiently with only a few thousand samples in 1--2 GPU hours. Moreover, these gains are consistent across out-of-domain tasks, different LLM families, and model sizes, and are even robust to paraphrasing attacks.

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: 1) Benchmarking: Create VOLTBench, a heterogeneous-task benchmark that quantifies length volatility in long-form generation across tasks and models. 2) Probing: Analyze internal attention patterns (attention traces) to find shared mechanisms that lead to volatile output lengths. 3) Mitigation: Propose GLoBo (Stable Generation via Logits Boosting), a decoding-time algorithm that adjusts token logits to improve length accuracy and stability, requiring no retraining.

Result: On VOLTBench, mainstream LLMs are shown to have severe instability in long-form output length. Applying GLoBo increases mean output length by 148% and cuts length volatility by 69%, while preserving high generation quality.

Conclusion: Output length volatility is a pervasive, previously under-studied problem in long-form LLM generation. A new benchmark (VOLTBench) makes this measurable, attention-trace analysis reveals internal causes, and the GLoBo decoding strategy effectively stabilizes and lengthens generations without retraining, providing more reliable long-form outputs.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) excel at long-context understanding but exhibit significant limitations in long-form generation. Existing studies primarily focus on single-generation quality, generally overlooking the volatility of the output. This volatility not only leads to significant computational costs but also severely impacts the models' reliable application. To address this gap, our work unfolds in three stages: benchmarking, probing, and mitigation. We first propose the VOlatility in Long-form Text Benchmark (VOLTBench), a novel heterogeneous-task benchmark designed to systematically quantify the length volatility of long-form generation. Subsequently, by analyzing attention traces, we conduct an in-depth probe to identify several common internal patterns that cause this volatility. Finally, to mitigate long-form output volatility, we propose Stable Generation via Logits Boosting (GLoBo), a lightweight decoding-stage optimization strategy, designed to significantly enhance both the length accuracy and stability of long-form generation without additional training. Extensive experiments on VOLTBench provide the first systematic confirmation of severe long-form output instability in mainstream models and validate that our proposed method successfully improves the mean output length of the base model by 148% and reduces the length volatility by 69%, while maintaining high generation quality.

### [27] [Embedding-based In-Context Prompt Training for Enhancing LLMs as Text Encoders](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01372)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: EPIC introduces an embedding-based in-context prompt training strategy where the usual textual demonstrations are replaced with their precomputed continuous embeddings. During contrastive training, the LLM receives both text inputs and embedded demonstrations in its context and is trained so that semantically related text pairs are aligned in the embedding space. Result: Models trained with EPIC achieve new state-of-the-art performance on the MTEB benchmark among systems trained only on publicly available retrieval data. The approach maintains or improves embedding performance both when in-context prompts are used and when they are omitted at inference. Ablation studies show that removing or modifying EPIC’s key components degrades performance, confirming their contribution.

Conclusion: Embedding-based in-context prompt training can transfer the benefits of ICL into the model’s embedding function while avoiding the token overhead of text demonstrations. By letting LLMs consume continuous demonstration embeddings as context, EPIC yields highly effective, computationally efficient embedding models that generalize well even without prompts, establishing a new performance frontier on standard benchmarks.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have been widely explored for embedding generation. While recent studies show that in-context learning (ICL) effectively enhances the representational capability of LLMs by prepending a few task-related demonstrations, it causes substantial token overhead due to the increased sequence length. In this work, we propose EPIC, a novel embedding-based in-context prompt training strategy that leverages ICL to generate high-quality embeddings while reducing computational burden during both training and inference. This approach replaces discrete text demonstrations with their corresponding continuous embeddings, which not only encourages the LLM to align semantically-related text pairs during contrastive learning, but also requires the model to interpret demonstration embeddings as part of the in-context prompt. Consequently, EPIC-trained models achieve excellent embedding performance both with or without in-context prompts at inference time. Comprehensive experiments demonstrate that our method establishes new state-of-the-art results on the MTEB benchmark, surpassing frontier models trained solely on publicly available retrieval data. Extensive ablation studies further validate the effectiveness and necessity of our mechanism.

### [28] [Focus on the Core: Empowering Diffusion Large Language Models by Self-Contrast](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01373)
