### [55] [RMGAP: Benchmarking the Generalization of Reward Models across Diverse Preferences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01831)
*Yangyang Zhou,Yi-Chen Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces RMGAP, a benchmark to test how well reward models in RLHF generalize to diverse user preferences, revealing current models perform poorly.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reward model benchmarks assume a single universal preference, so they cannot assess whether reward models can adapt to different, potentially conflicting user preferences for the same task. Since real users have diverse requirements and ways of expressing them, there is a need to rigorously evaluate the generalizability of reward models across varied domains and preference phrasings.

Method: The authors build RMGAP, a benchmark of 1,097 instances across Chat, Writing, Reasoning, and Safety. For each original prompt, they generate four candidate responses with distinct linguistic profiles. They then design new, more specific prompts by contrasting these candidates and constructing scenarios where exactly one response is uniquely appropriate, thus encoding different user preferences. To capture variation in how preferences are expressed, they extend each prompt with two paraphrased versions that preserve the same underlying preference but change the phrasing. They evaluate 24 state-of-the-art reward models on this benchmark using Best-of-N accuracy to measure how often the model selects the uniquely appropriate response.

Result: Across 24 state-of-the-art reward models, performance on RMGAP is low: the best model reaches only 49.27% Best-of-N accuracy in selecting the uniquely appropriate response among the candidates. This indicates that current reward models struggle to robustly capture and generalize to diverse user preferences, even when the preference is clearly encoded in the prompt.

Conclusion: RMGAP exposes significant weaknesses in the generalization ability of current reward models used in RLHF. Benchmarks assuming a universal preference over responses are insufficient to evaluate alignment with real-world, heterogeneous user preferences. The authors argue that improving reward models requires explicitly testing and training for generalization across varied domains, linguistic profiles, and paraphrased expressions of preference, and they release RMGAP to catalyze such research.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback has become the standard paradigm for language model alignment, where reward models directly determine alignment effectiveness. In this work, we focus on how to evaluate the generalizability of reward models. By "generalizability", we mean the ability of RMs to correctly rank responses to align with diverse user preferences. However, existing reward model benchmarks are typically designed around a universal preference, failing to assess this generalization. To address this critical gap, we introduce RMGAP, a benchmark comprising 1,097 instances across Chat, Writing, Reasoning, and Safety domains. Since different users exhibit diverse preferences for the same task, we first generate four distinct responses with different linguistic profiles for each collected prompt. However, the original prompt set lacks the specificity to convey different preferences. We therefore construct tailored prompts by contrasting these candidates and designing scenarios in which one response becomes the uniquely appropriate choice. Moreover, we observe that users often express the same preference using different phrasings, and thus extend each prompt with two paraphrased variants. Our evaluation of 24 state-of-the-art RMs reveals their substantial limitations: even the best RM achieves only 49.27% Best-of-N accuracy, highlighting considerable room for improvement in reward model generalization. Related data and code are available at https://github.com/nanzhi84/RMGAP.

### [56] [The Cylindrical Representation Hypothesis for Language Model Steering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01844)
*Lang Gao,Jinghui Zhang,Wei Liu,Fengxian Ji,Chenxi Wang,Zirui Song,Akash Ghosh,Youssef Mohamed,Preslav Nakov,Xiuying Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new geometric hypothesis, the Cylindrical Representation Hypothesis (CRH), to explain why steering large language models via linear directions is unstable, showing that concept representations form a cylinder-like structure with an axis and a surrounding plane that introduces intrinsic uncertainty in steering outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Steering LLMs with linear directions is popular but often leads to unstable and unpredictable effects. Existing explanations rely on the Linear Representation Hypothesis, which assumes orthogonal, non-overlapping concept directions. This assumption does not hold in real model representations and cannot explain the empirically observed variability in steering, motivating a more realistic theory of how concepts are encoded and controlled.

Method: The authors relax the orthogonality assumption of the Linear Representation Hypothesis while keeping linearity, and mathematically analyze how overlapping concept contributions structure the representation space. They formalize this into the Cylindrical Representation Hypothesis, where each concept is represented by a central axis plus a surrounding normal plane. They then design experiments on real LLMs to test for the presence of this cylindrical structure, estimate axes and planes from difference vectors, and study how different directions within the plane affect steering sensitivity and variability.

Result: They find evidence that concept representations in LLMs indeed exhibit a cylindrical geometry: a main axis that distinguishes presence vs absence of a concept and a normal plane modulating how easily the concept is activated. They show that while the normal plane can be stably recovered from difference vectors, only certain sectors in that plane are highly sensitive for activating the concept, and these sectors cannot be reliably predicted from standard steering directions. This sector-level uncertainty aligns with and quantitatively explains the observed instability and fluctuation in steering outcomes.

Conclusion: The Cylindrical Representation Hypothesis offers a more accurate and practical framework than the standard Linear Representation Hypothesis for understanding and predicting the behavior of steering in language models. It explains why steering often behaves unpredictably even with seemingly correct directions, by attributing this to intrinsic uncertainty in which sectors of the concept’s normal plane are sensitive. This framework can guide the design of more robust steering methods and interpretation tools for real-world LLM control.

Abstract: Steering is a widely used technique for controlling large language models, yet its effects are often unstable and hard to predict. Existing theoretical accounts are largely based on the Linear Representation Hypothesis (LRH). While LRH assumes that concepts can be orthogonalized for lossless control, this idealized mapping fails in real representations and cannot account for the observed unpredictability of steering. By relaxing LRH's orthogonality assumption while preserving linear representations, we show that overlapping concept contributions naturally yield a sample-specific axis-orthogonal structure. We formalize this as the Cylindrical Representation Hypothesis (CRH). In CRH, a central axis captures the main difference between concept absence and presence and drives concept generation. A surrounding normal plane controls steering sensitivity by determining how easily the axis can activate the target concept. Within this plane, only specific sensitive sectors strongly facilitate concept activation, while other sectors can suppress or delay it. While the surrounding normal plane can be reliably identified from difference vectors, the sensitive sector cannot, introducing intrinsic uncertainty at the sector level. This uncertainty provides a principled explanation for why steering outcomes often fluctuate even when using well-aligned directions. Our experiments verify the existence of the cylindrical structure and demonstrate that CRH provides a valid and practical way to interpret model steering behavior in real settings: https://github.com/mbzuai-nlp/CRH.

</details>


### [57] [Do Large Language Models Plan Answer Positions? Position Bias in Multiple-Choice Question Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01846)
*Xuemei Tang,Xufeng Duan,Zhenguang G. Cai*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They empirically analyze answer-position distributions across 10 LLMs and 5 VLMs on three MCQ generation tasks, run probing experiments on hidden representations in question stems to see if they encode the future answer position, and then use activation steering to modify internal activations to shift positional preferences.

Result: They find strong, structured position biases that are similar within model families; probe models can predict the correct answer position from stem representations, indicating implicit planning; and activation steering can partially control and significantly shift the distribution of answer positions.

Conclusion: LLMs implicitly plan the position of correct answers when generating MCQs, leading to systematic biases. Activation steering provides a practical way to study and partially control these positional preferences, which is crucial for reliable MCQ construction and evaluation.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used to generate multiple-choice questions (MCQs), where correct answers should ideally be uniformly distributed across options. However, we observe that LLMs exhibit systematic position biases during generation. Through extensive experiments with 10 LLMs and 5 vision-language models (VLMs) on three MCQ generation tasks, we show that these biases are structured, with similar patterns emerging within model families. To investigate the underlying mechanisms, we conduct probing experiments and find that hidden representations in the question stem encode predictive signals of the correct answer position, suggesting that answer position may be implicitly planned during generation. Building on this insight, we apply activation steering to manipulate internal representations and influence answer position. Our results show that steering can partially control positional preferences and substantially shift answer position distributions. Our findings provide a practical framework for studying implicit positional planning in LLMs and highlight the importance of controllable generation for reliable MCQ construction and evaluation.

### [58] [Spatiotemporal Hidden-State Dynamics as a Signature of Internal Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01853)
*Kotaro Furuya,Takahito Tanimura*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces StALT, a training-free metric that captures spatiotemporal patterns in hidden-state transitions of large reasoning models and uses it to distinguish correct from incorrect reasoning trajectories.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While large reasoning models produce long solution traces, it is unclear whether these traces correspond to genuine internal reasoning or just verbosity. Existing hidden-state analyses are too coarse and overlook fine-grained token-by-layer structure. The authors aim to find reliable, training-free internal signals that reflect whether and how models are actually reasoning, and that can predict correctness without labels.

Method: The authors analyze hidden-state transitions across both decoding steps (tokens) and layers in large reasoning models. They characterize a pattern where successful reasoning trajectories have broad temporal dynamics but concentrated layer-wise activity. They formalize this via Spatiotemporal Amplitude of Latent Transition (StALT), which measures temporal changes between adjacent tokens weighted by layer saliency within each token. They compute StALT across models and benchmarks, compare its ability to separate correct vs. incorrect trajectories against output- and length-based baselines, and perform interventions that vary reasoning demand to see how StALT responds.

Result: StALT consistently separates correct from incorrect trajectories in reasoning-heavy tasks and is competitive with strong label-free baselines based on outputs and length. Successful trajectories show strong spatiotemporal amplitude with specific temporal and layer-localized patterns in LRMs, while this structure is weaker in non-reasoning models and knowledge-focused domains. Intervention experiments show StALT increases or decreases systematically when reasoning demands are raised or lowered.

Conclusion: Large reasoning models exhibit distinctive and measurable hidden-state dynamics associated with successful reasoning. The proposed StALT statistic provides a practical, training-free probe of these internal computations, going beyond output-based evaluation and supporting the view that LRMs perform substantive internal reasoning rather than mere verbose generation.

Abstract: Large reasoning models (LRMs) generate extended solutions, yet it remains unclear whether these traces reflect substantive internal computation or merely verbosity and overthinking. Although recent hidden-state analyses suggest that internal representations carry correctness-related signals, their coarse aggregations may obscure the token and layer structure underlying reasoning computation. We investigate hidden-state transitions across decoding steps and layers, and identify a distinct spatiotemporal pattern in LRMs: successful trajectories exhibit broad temporal dynamics with localized layer-wise concentration, while this structure is weaker in non-reasoning models and knowledge-heavy domains. We formalize this characteristic as Spatiotemporal Amplitude of Latent Transition (StALT), a training-free trajectory statistic that summarizes temporal changes between adjacent tokens weighted by within-token layer saliency. Across diverse models and benchmarks, StALT reliably separates correct from incorrect trajectories in reasoning-intensive regimes, providing a competitive label-free correctness signal alongside strong output-space and length-based baselines. Intervention analyses further show that this spatiotemporal amplitude responds systematically to manipulations that increase or reduce the demand for internal reasoning, supporting its association with latent reasoning dynamics in LRMs. These findings provide empirical evidence that LRMs exhibit measurable hidden-state dynamics and offer a practical probe for understanding internal computation beyond output-based evaluation.

Additionally, state-of-the-art LRMs often comprise hundreds of billions of parameters and require several seconds per inference, even on advanced multi-GPU systems. These characteristics limit their practicality for deployment in conventional computing environments. Meanwhile, NLP research on multilingual LLMs continues to prioritize high-resource languages. However, these models exhibit limited performance in under-resourced languages, primarily due to insufficient language- and culture-specific training data. ### [60] [StressEval: Failure-Driven Dynamic Benchmarking for Knowledge-Intensive Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01939)
*Yongrui Chen,Yangyang Ma,Xiaoying Huang,Shenyu Zhang,Huajun Chen,Haofen Wang,Guilin Qi*

Main category: cs.CL

  <summary>Details</summary>

Dynamic benchmarks help but often become too hard, less answerable, and harder to control. There is a need for an evaluation method that remains fresh and challenging while preserving answerability and providing interpretable, controllable difficulty signals for more actionable model improvement.

Method: StressEval is a three-stage, failure-driven data synthesis framework. (1) It first creates a semi-structured difficulty card for instances where models fail, explicitly identifying the failed reasoning step and its root cause. (2) It then performs dual-perspective instance synthesis, generating new test items that simultaneously target model knowledge gaps and reasoning breakdowns, while preserving the core difficulty factors uncovered in the card. (3) Finally, it applies a gating mechanism to filter out instances that are not grounded or are ambiguous, ensuring quality and answerability. Using several knowledge-intensive reasoning datasets as seeds, StressEval is applied to construct a new benchmark suite named Dynamic OneEval.

Result: When applied to multiple seed datasets, StressEval produces Dynamic OneEval, a focused and challenging dynamic benchmark. Evaluations of several state-of-the-art LLMs on Dynamic OneEval show substantially larger performance drops compared to the original benchmarks, indicating that the new benchmark is more challenging and sensitive to model weaknesses. At the same time, Dynamic OneEval maintains explicit difficulty factors linked to specific failure modes.

Conclusion: StressEval effectively transforms observed LLM failures into high-quality, dynamic evaluation instances that are both challenging and controllable. The resulting Dynamic OneEval benchmark exposes weaknesses in state-of-the-art LLMs more strongly than existing benchmarks while preserving explicit difficulty annotations, making evaluations more informative and enabling more targeted, actionable iteration on model and system design.

Abstract: Static benchmarks for LLMs are increasingly compromised by contamination and overfitting especially on knowledge intensive reasoning tasks While recent dynamic benchmarks can alleviate staleness they often increase difficulty at the expense of answerability and controllability In this paper we propose StressEval a failure driven data synthesis framework that turns observed model failures into dynamic challenging and controllable test instances StressEval consists of three stages first it constructs a semi structured difficulty card that identifies the failed reasoning step and its root cause second it applies a dual perspective instance synthesis method that targets both knowledge gaps and reasoning breakdowns while preserving the underlying difficulty factors and third it applies a gating mechanism to retain only grounded unambiguous instances Seeding from multiple knowledge intensive reasoning datasets we employ StressEval to build Dynamic OneEval a focused suite of challenging dynamic benchmark Across several state of the art LLMs Dynamic OneEval yields substantially larger performance drops than the original benchmarks while retaining explicit difficulty factors enabling more actionable iteration

Method: They treat the β coefficient in SwiGLU feed-forward blocks as a meta-signal and activate it through a meta-gating mechanism. They compare against finetuning and meta-learning baselines on multiple condition types and examine generalization to unseen tasks and condition settings.

It shows better robustness to corpus heterogeneity and maintains performance without catastrophic forgetting. The method also demonstrates reasonable zero- or few-shot generalization to new tasks, condition types, or instructions not seen during training.

Conclusion: Condition-dependent control of LLMs can be effectively realized by treating SwiGLU β as a meta-gate generated by a hypernetwork, avoiding the cost and instability of full finetuning or traditional meta-learning. This approach yields better adaptation and generalization under heterogeneous and changing conditions, suggesting that modest structural changes to existing architectures can unlock powerful meta-learning-like behavior in a scalable way.

Abstract: Conventional LLMs may suffer from corpus heterogeneity and subtle condition changes. While finetuning can create the catastrophe forgetting issue, application of meta-learning on LLMs is also limited due to its complexity and scalability. In this paper, we activate the meta-signal of $β$ within the SwiGLU blocks, resulting in a meta-gating mechanism that adaptively adjusts the nonlinearity of FFN. A hypernetwork is employed which dynamically produces $β$ on textual conditions, providing meta-controllability on LLMs. By testing on different condition types such as task, domain, persona, and style, our method outperforms finetuning and meta-learning baselines, and can generalize reasonably on unseen tasks, condition types, or instructions. ### [63] [Counting as a minimal probe of language model reliability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02028)
*Tianxiang Dai,Jonathan Fan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a simple, content-free benchmark—Stable Counting Capacity—to test whether large language models can reliably follow a basic procedural rule (counting symbols).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models perform well on tasks like math reasoning, coding, and document analysis, it is unclear whether this reflects genuine, general logical competence or merely sophisticated pattern matching and memorized procedures. Existing benchmarks are confounded by knowledge, semantics, and tokenization issues, making it hard to isolate pure rule-following ability. The authors want a clean way to assess whether models can stably apply a simple algorithmic rule across long contexts.

Method: They design an assay called Stable Counting Capacity, where models are asked to count repeated, structureless symbols (e.g., a long sequence of the same token) until the model’s answers start to fail. The task is stripped of semantics and domain knowledge to focus purely on procedural reliability. They test more than 100 model variants, systematically increasing sequence length and observing where counting breaks, and also explore whether extra test-time compute (e.g., chain-of-thought or more sampling) improves reliability.

Result: Across all tested models, the length over which counting remains accurate (the stable counting capacity) is much shorter than the models’ nominal context windows. Model behavior does not match that of an unbounded logical counter or a stable learned counting algorithm. Instead, accuracy patterns suggest that models rely on a limited set of internal count-like states—akin to using a small number of fingers—beyond which performance abruptly deteriorates into near-random guessing, even when given more time or reasoning steps at inference.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current large language models’ impressive performance on complex benchmarks should not be mistaken for robust, general rule-following ability. Their counting behavior indicates they depend on a finite, fragile internal mechanism rather than an open-ended algorithmic capability. Once this mechanism is saturated, they fail catastrophically rather than gracefully degrading, implying that fluent task performance can mask serious limits in procedural reliability and logical generalization.

Abstract: Large language models perform strongly on benchmarks in mathematical reasoning, coding and document analysis, suggesting a broad ability to follow instructions. However, it remains unclear whether such success reflects general logical competence, repeated application of learned procedures, or pattern matching that mimics rule execution. We investigate this question by introducing Stable Counting Capacity, an assay in which models count repeated symbols until failure. The assay removes knowledge dependencies, semantics and ambiguity from evaluation, avoids lexical and tokenization confounds, and provides a direct measure of procedural reliability beyond standard knowledge-based benchmarks. Here we show, across more than 100 model variants, that stable counting capacity remains far below advertised context limits. Model behavior is consistent neither with open-ended logic nor with stable application of a learned rule, but instead with use of a finite set of count-like internal states, analogous to counting on fingers. Once this resource is exhausted, the appearance of rule following disappears and exact execution collapses into guessing, even with additional test-time compute. These findings show that fluent performance in current language models does not guarantee general, reliable rule following.

</details>


### [64] [A Multimodal Dataset for Visually Grounded Ambiguity in Machine Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02035)
*Jingheng Pan,Xintong Wang,Longyue Wang,Liang Ding,Weihua Luo,Chris Biemann*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VIDA, a curated dataset and new evaluation metrics to better test and improve how multimodal machine translation models use images to resolve linguistic ambiguities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal machine translation benchmarks for ambiguity resolution suffer from noisy data, limited alignment with realistic translation scenarios, and narrow ambiguity types. As a result, they provide weak evidence that models genuinely exploit visual context rather than spurious patterns. There is a need for cleaner, more targeted data and evaluation methods that explicitly test whether visual information is necessary and actually used to resolve ambiguous expressions in open-ended translation.

Method: The authors construct VIDA, a dataset of 2,500 translation instances where each example contains an explicitly annotated ambiguous span in the source sentence that cannot be correctly interpreted without the accompanying image. They then design Disambiguation-Centric Metrics that rely on an LLM-as-a-judge classifier, which checks at the span level whether the ambiguous source expression has been correctly disambiguated in the model’s translation. They evaluate two state-of-the-art large vision-language models under three regimes: standard (vanilla) inference, supervised fine-tuning (SFT) on relevant data, and a proposed chain-of-thought SFT (CoT-SFT) that encourages models to explicitly reason through the disambiguation process.

Result: Supervised fine-tuning improves overall translation quality compared with vanilla inference across the evaluated models. However, CoT-SFT leads to more consistent and pronounced improvements specifically in disambiguation performance, as measured by the new span-level metrics. These gains are particularly strong on out-of-distribution subsets of VIDA, suggesting that the chain-of-thought approach helps models generalize their ambiguity resolution capabilities beyond the training distribution.

Conclusion: VIDA and the proposed Disambiguation-Centric Metrics provide a more reliable way to assess whether multimodal translation systems truly use visual context for ambiguity resolution. Chain-of-thought supervised fine-tuning appears especially effective for strengthening models’ ability to handle diverse and out-of-distribution ambiguity types, offering a promising direction for developing more robust and visually grounded multimodal machine translation systems.

Abstract: Ambiguity resolution is a key challenge in multimodal machine translation (MMT), where models must genuinely leverage visual input to map an ambiguous expression to its intended meaning. Although prior work has proposed disambiguation-oriented benchmarks that provide supportive evidence for the role of vision, we observe substantial issues in data quality and a mismatch with translation scenarios. Moreover, existing ambiguity-oriented evaluations are not well suited to broader ambiguity types in open-ended translation. To address these limitations, we present VIDA (Visually-Dependent Ambiguity), a dataset of 2,500 carefully curated instances in which resolving an annotated ambiguous source span requires visual evidence. We further propose Disambiguation-Centric Metrics that use an LLM-as-a-judge classifier to verify whether annotated ambiguous expressions are resolved correctly at the span level. Experiments with two state-of-the-art Large Vision Language Models under vanilla inference, supervised fine-tuning (SFT), and our chain-of-thought SFT (CoT-SFT) show that while SFT improves overall translation quality, CoT-SFT yields more consistent gains in disambiguation accuracy, especially on out-of-distribution subsets, indicating a stronger generalization for resolving diverse ambiguity types.

### [65] [What Single-Prompt Accuracy Misses: A Multi-Variant Reliability Audit of Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02038)
*Ranit Karmakar,Jayita Chatterjee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard single-prompt accuracy benchmarks can hide important reliability issues in language models, and that conclusions about model reliability depend strongly on the evaluation pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Researchers and practitioners usually assess language models using single-prompt accuracy, assuming it adequately reflects reliability. However, this approach may overlook critical issues such as calibration, robustness to prompt changes, and failures in evaluator setups, especially for smaller open-weight models that many people rely on.

Method: The authors evaluate 15 open-weight language models, focusing their main reliability analyses on 10 instruction-tuned models. They test five classification and reasoning benchmarks, each under five different prompt variants. For each combination of model, dataset, and prompt variant, they compute multiple metrics: accuracy, token-probability calibration (ECE), verbal-confidence calibration, verbal parse rate, and the spread of performance under prompt perturbations. They also compare different calibration definitions and examine failures caused by evaluator logic, such as first-character grading for chain-of-thought outputs, and attempt repair procedures to distinguish evaluator-side from model-side failures.

Result: They find that evaluation design greatly affects measured reliability. Changing the ECE definition from raw to label-set-normalized shifts calibration scores by an average absolute 0.149 per cell. Using chain-of-thought prompts with a simplistic first-character evaluator on ARC-Challenge produces apparent accuracy drops of 72–88% for all main models; two repair methods recover roughly all the lost performance, revealing evaluator, not model, failure. They also show that verbal confidence often exceeds both actual accuracy and probability-based confidence (overconfidence) on MMLU-Pro, and that parseability of verbal confidence can fail completely for particular model–prompt combinations. Finally, they find that prompt robustness (performance spread across prompt variants) does not correlate reliably with model size, with correlations between -0.244 and 0.474.

Conclusion: The reliability of small (and more broadly, open-weight) language models cannot be judged solely by single-prompt accuracy or without specifying the evaluation pipeline. Calibration definitions, evaluator logic, verbal-output parseability, and robustness to prompt changes materially shape reliability conclusions and should be explicitly reported in benchmarking and reliability claims.

Abstract: Single-prompt accuracy is the dominant way to benchmark language models, but it can miss reliability failures that matter. We evaluate a 15-model open-weight corpus, with the main reliability analyses focused on 10 instruct models across five classification and reasoning benchmarks under five prompt variants each, measuring accuracy, token-probability calibration, verbal-confidence calibration, verbal parse rate, and prompt-perturbation spread for every (model x dataset x variant) cell. We find three broad results. First, evaluation design can materially change the conclusion. Switching Expected Calibration Error (ECE) token from a raw to a label-set-normalised definition changes per-cell calibration by a mean absolute 0.149. More strikingly, pairing a chain-of-thought prompt with a first-character evaluator on ARC-Challenge reduces apparent accuracy by 72-88% across all five primary models; two independent repair procedures recover 93.8% and 102.7% of the lost performance, indicating an evaluator-side rather than model-side failure. Second, confidence signals are fragile. On MMLU-Pro, every primary model verbally reports confidence substantially above both its accuracy and its token-probability confidence on the same rows, and verbal parse rate can collapse for a single model on a single prompt variant. Third, prompt robustness does not track parameter count reliably. Across 10 instruct models, the correlation between model size and prompt-perturbation spread ranges from -0.244 to 0.474 across benchmarks. Taken together, these results show that reliability conclusions for small language models depend not only on the model being evaluated, but also on the evaluation pipeline used to measure it. We argue that calibration definitions, evaluator logic, verbal parseability, and prompt robustness should be reported explicitly when making reliability claims.

</details>


### [66] [Methods, Data, and Conceptual Change: Reflections from Two Quantitative Diachronic Case Studies](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02052)
*Catherine Wong,Bach Phan-Tat,Susan Fitzmaurice*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper reflects on how quantitative methods in historical linguistics depend on and are constrained by dataset properties, using two English corpora and methods as case studies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and make explicit how different quantitative, frequency-based methods for studying semantic change are shaped by the structure and assumptions of the corpora they are applied to, and to clarify what kinds of diachronic interpretations are methodologically warranted.

Method: The authors conduct a comparative methodological reflection using two worked examples: (1) quad-based concept modelling applied to Early Modern English discourse in the EEBO-TCP corpus (c. 1470s–1690s; 765M words), and (2) SynFlow analysis applied to scientific writing in the Royal Society Corpus 6.0.4 (1750–1799; a 78.6M-token corpus). They compare how each method operationalises ‘concepts’, what data assumptions are made, and what kinds of semantic changes each can detect.

Result: They find that the two quantitative approaches, though both lexical and frequency-based, are constrained in different ways by their underlying datasets and by how they define and model concepts. These constraints affect the types of semantic change that are visible and the robustness of diachronic interpretations, revealing methodological limits and mismatches between methods and data properties.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that reflective comparison of quantitative methods and datasets is crucial: it exposes the limits of purely lexical, frequency-based approaches and shows that dataset structure significantly conditions what semantic changes can be reliably detected. Researchers must therefore align methods with corpus properties and be cautious in interpreting diachronic semantic patterns.

Abstract: This discussion paper reflects on how quantitative approaches to historical linguistics interact with dataset properties. Drawing on two worked examples, we examine English data using quad-based concept modelling of Early Modern English discourse in EEBO-TCP (c. 1470s-1690s; 765M words) alongside SynFlow analysis of scientific writing in Royal Society Corpus 6.0.4 (1750-1799; drawn from a 78.6M-token open corpus). Through parallel comparison, the paper explores how each approach operationalises concepts, the data assumptions they entail, and the diachronic interpretations they support. We argue that comparative methodological reflection clarifies the limits of purely lexical, frequency-based approaches and highlights how dataset structure shapes the kinds of semantic change that quantitative methods can reliably detect.

### [67] [Pair2Score: Pairwise-to-Absolute Transfer for LLM-Based Essay Scoring](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02069)
*İbrahim Rıza Hallaç,Hasan Oğul*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Pair2Score is a two-stage framework that converts pairwise comparison learning into absolute scoring using parameter-efficient LLaMA adaptation, improving automated essay scoring for certain traits.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Absolute scoring is often required in applications like Automated Essay Scoring, but learning absolute predictions directly can be challenging, whereas pairwise comparisons are easier to learn from. The paper aims to leverage the simplicity of pairwise learning while still producing accurate absolute scores.

Method: The method, Pair2Score, uses a two-stage learning framework. Stage 1 trains a directional Siamese ranker on pairwise comparisons that are derived from existing absolute trait labels. Stage 2 then trains an absolute score predictor using LLaMA with parameter-efficient adaptation, employing different transfer strategies (such as warm-start and embedding-fusion) to transfer knowledge from the pairwise model to the absolute predictor.

Result: On rubric-aligned AES traits (grammar, vocabulary, syntax) evaluated under a five-fold protocol, the best transfer variant of Pair2Score improves quadratic weighted kappa compared to an absolute-only baseline across all three traits. However, not all transfer configurations are beneficial, and some extended pairwise training settings can even hurt performance compared to a shorter one-epoch pairwise stage.

Conclusion: Pair2Score demonstrates that pairwise comparison learning can effectively enhance absolute scoring models when combined with appropriate transfer strategies. The success of this approach depends sensitively on the configuration of the transfer stage rather than simply adding a pairwise stage, with shorter pairwise training sometimes offering more reliable benefits than extended training.

Abstract: Many scoring applications require absolute predictions, while pairwise comparisons can provide a simpler learning objective. We present Pair2Score, a two-stage learning framework that transfers pairwise comparisons into absolute scoring with parameter-efficient LLaMA adaptation. Stage 1 trains a directional Siamese ranker on pairwise comparisons derived from absolute trait labels; Stage 2 trains an absolute predictor using configurable transfer strategies (warm-start and embedding-fusion variants). We evaluate on rubric-aligned Automated Essay Scoring (AES) traits (grammar, vocabulary, syntax) under a five-fold protocol that co-rotates held-out fold and random seed. At the trait level, the best-performing transfer variant improves quadratic weighted kappa (QWK) over an absolute-only baseline for all three traits. However, not all transfer configurations help: a one-epoch pairwise stage transfers more reliably than extended pairwise training, and transfer configuration -- not just the inclusion of a pairwise stage -- determines whether downstream scoring benefits.

*Garvin Kruthof*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Benchmark to test if LLMs properly propagate local factual edits through all dependent claims in scientific-style manuscripts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

LLM-based manuscript editors may correctly edit the local fact but fail to update these dependent claims, threatening scientific reliability. Method: They build EditPropBench: synthetic ML/NLP-style manuscripts with a controlled fact graph. For each instance, they specify a targeted factual edit, all sentences that must change because they depend on that fact, and sentences that must stay unchanged. They define several editing protocols (including harder implicit/free-form ones), adversarial metric probes, stress-test variants, and a metric suite focused on Edit-Ripple Adherence (ERA) to quantify how well edits are propagated without over-editing. They evaluate five LLM editing systems and deterministic substitution baselines across the benchmark strata.

Result: On the hardest implicit/free-form setting, LLM editors achieve ERA between 0.148 and 0.705; even the best model fails to perform around 30% of the necessary cascade edits. In a mixed-stratum stress-test that includes easier substitution cases, LLMs still outperform deterministic substitution baselines, retaining a positive advantage overall. An empirical audit of recent arXiv cs.CL benchmark/dataset papers finds that 37.2% include qualitative claims whose correctness depends on such factual details, highlighting real-world relevance.

Conclusion: EditPropBench demonstrates that current LLM editors can fix many implicit consequences of factual changes but are still unreliable at fully propagating edits through all dependent claims. The benchmark and ERA metric provide a structured way to study and improve cascade-aware scientific editing. For now, dependable scientific revision still requires explicit cascade-aware checking beyond what LLM editors provide.

Abstract: Local factual edits in scientific manuscripts often create non-local revision obligations. If a dataset changes from 215 to 80 documents, claims such as 'medium-scale' or 'a few hundred items' may also become stale, even though they do not repeat the edited number. We introduce EditPropBench, a benchmark for measuring whether LLM editors propagate factual edits through dependent manuscript claims. Each item contains an ML/NLP-style synthetic manuscript, a targeted edit, and a controlled fact graph with sentence-level labels for direct targets, required downstream updates, and protected unrelated text. EditPropBench provides a controlled manuscript-level benchmark with sentence-level dependency supervision, three editing protocols, adversarial metric probes, stress-test variants, and a metric suite centered on Edit-Ripple Adherence (ERA). On the hard implicit/free-form stratum, five LLM editing systems span ERA 0.148--0.705; even the strongest misses roughly 30% of required cascade updates. A mixed-stratum stress test shows that LLMs retain a positive advantage over deterministic substitution baselines when easy substitution-solvable cases are included. Finally, an audit of recent arXiv cs.CL benchmark and dataset papers finds fact-dependent qualitative claims in 37.2% of papers. EditPropBench shows that current LLM editors can repair many implicit consequences of factual edits, but reliable scientific revision still requires cascade-aware checking.

Abstract: In this paper, we present our system for SemEval-2026 Task 6 (CLARITY) on response clarity and evasion detection in question-answer pairs from U.S. presidential interviews, comparing fine-tuned encoders with prompt-based LLMs. Our LLM ensemble achieves 80 macro-F1 on the 3-class Task 1 (9th/41) and 59 on the 9-class Task 2 (3rd/33). Across 8 transformer encoders optimized through a four-stage pipeline, partial encoder layer unfreezing outperforms full fine-tuning by a wide margin. Combining English and multilingual encoders further improves ensemble performance over either family alone, despite multilingual models being individually weaker. Prompt-based LLMs, without any task-specific parameter updates, outperform fine-tuned encoders, particularly on minority classes; among open-weight LLMs, parameter count does not predict performance. Enriched input, concatenating the full interviewer turn, improves LLM performance but not that of encoders, an effect that persists with Longformer's extended context window, suggesting the divergence is not attributable to sequence-length capacity alone in our settings. The Clear Reply/Ambivalent boundary remains the dominant failure mode, mirroring the disagreement among human annotators. Our code, prompts, model configurations, and results are publicly available.

Method: ARGUS is a three-stage framework: (1) Policy Seeding builds an initial understanding of new regulations; (2) Adversarial Label Rectification uses a multi-agent "Prosecutor-Defender-Umpire" setup to reconcile inconsistencies between old labels and new mandates; (3) Latent Knowledge Discovery conducts tripartite dialectical discussions to surface subtle gray-area violations. It integrates RAG-augmented policy knowledge and Chain-of-Thought reasoning as dynamic rewards in a reinforcement learning setup to align the system’s reasoning with new policies.

Result: Experiments on industrial and public datasets show that ARGUS outperforms standard fine-tuning approaches, learning updated policy behavior more effectively and requiring only minimal high-quality labeled data.

Conclusion: ARGUS offers a robust, reinforcement-learning-based framework for adaptive policy governance in online advertising, effectively handling non-stationary regulations, resolving label conflicts, and capturing nuanced violations while reducing dependence on large amounts of new labeled data.

In this paper, we propose ARGUS, a policy-adaptive governance system that enables evolving reinforcement through multi-agent adversarial umpiring. ARGUS addresses the sparsity of new policy data by employing a three-stage framework: (1) Policy Seeding for initial perception; (2) Adversarial Label Rectification, which utilizes a ``Prosecutor-Defender-Umpire'' architecture to resolve conflicts between stale labels and new mandates; and (3) Latent Knowledge Discovery, which employs a tripartite dialectical discussion to unearth sophisticated, ``gray-area'' violations. By leveraging RAG-enhanced policy knowledge and Chain-of-Thought synthesis as dynamic rewards for reinforcement learning, ARGUS synchronizes its reasoning pathways with evolving regulations. Extensive experiments on both industrial and public datasets demonstrate that ARGUS significantly outperforms traditional fine-tuning baselines, achieving superior policy-adaptive learning with minimal gold data.

### [71] [An Information-theoretic Propagation Denoising and Fusion Framework for Fake News Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02259)
*Mengyang Chen,Lingwei Wei,Wei Zhou,Songlin Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes InfoPDF, an information-theoretic framework that denoises and fuses real and LLM-generated synthetic propagation data to improve fake news detection, addressing the unreliability of synthetic signals via mutual-information-based learning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fake news detection methods increasingly exploit news propagation patterns on social platforms. However, real propagation data are often incomplete (missing user interactions), which weakens detection robustness. Recent works try to fill these gaps by using large language models to role-play users and generate synthetic propagation traces. Yet these synthetic signals are noisy and unreliable; naively merging them with real data introduces bias and can even hurt performance. There is a need for a principled way to exploit the complementary information in synthetic propagation while mitigating its noise and uncertainty.

Method: The authors propose InfoPDF, an information-theoretic propagation denoising and fusion framework. First, they use LLMs to generate attribute-specific synthetic propagation graphs (e.g., reflecting different user attributes or behaviors). Each synthetic propagation graph is then modeled as a probabilistic latent distribution instead of a deterministic graph. InfoPDF performs reliability-aware adaptive fusion between real propagation and these latent synthetic distributions. During training, they design a mutual-information-based objective that: (1) compresses propagation representations to remove noise, (2) enforces consistency between representations derived from real and synthetic propagation, and (3) preserves sufficiency of the learned representations for downstream tasks: fake news classification and attribute prediction. The framework thus jointly denoises and fuses multiple propagation sources via information-theoretic constraints.

Result: On three real-world fake news datasets, InfoPDF outperforms existing baselines across diverse fake news detection settings. The framework yields higher detection accuracy and robustness compared to methods that either ignore synthetic propagation or naively incorporate it. Additional analyses show that InfoPDF can estimate attribute-level reliabilities of synthetic propagation, down-weighting noisy attributes and emphasizing informative ones, and that the resulting representations are more discriminative in separating fake and real news in the learned embedding space.

Conclusion: InfoPDF provides a principled, information-theoretic approach to leveraging unreliable LLM-generated propagation data for fake news detection. By modeling synthetic propagation as probabilistic latent variables, adaptively fusing them with real data, and optimizing a mutual-information-based objective that enforces compression, consistency, and task sufficiency, the method effectively denoises and exploits synthetic propagation. This leads to consistently better detection performance and more discriminative, reliability-aware propagation representations, suggesting a promising direction for integrating generative models with propagation-based detection systems.

Abstract: Incomplete propagation data significantly hinders robust fake news detection. Recent approaches leverage large language models to simulate missing user interactions via role-playing, thereby enriching propagation with synthetic signals. However, such propagation data is intrinsically unreliable, and directly fusing it can lead to biased representations, leading to limited detection performance. In this paper, we alleviate the unreliability of synthetic propagation from the mutual information perspective and propose a novel information-theoretic propagation denoising and fusion (InfoPDF) framework to learn effective representations from both real and synthetic propagation. Specifically, we first generate attribute-specific synthetic propagation using large language models. Then we model each synthetic propagation graph as a probabilistic latent distribution to guide reliability-aware adaptive fusion with real propagation. During training, we design a mutual information-based objective to learn compressed and task-sufficient propagation representations. It jointly suppresses noisy signals across attribute-specific synthetic propagation, maintains consistency between real and synthetic propagation representations, and ensures task sufficiency for fake news detection and attribute prediction. Experiments on three real-world datasets show that InfoPDF consistently achieves superior performance across various fake news detection tasks. Further analysis demonstrates that InfoPDF can estimate attribute-level reliabilities and learn more discriminative propagation representations.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors built and validated a large parallel Tajik–Persian corpus (328k sentence pairs, with a stratified 40k subset) from diverse textual sources, then evaluated six model classes: rule-based baseline, LSTM+attention, character Transformer, G2P Transformer, pre-trained multilingual models (mBART, mT5 with LoRA), and byte-level ByT5, comparing them with chrF++ metrics in both directions.

Result: ByT5 achieved the best performance by a wide margin (chrF++ 87.4 Tajik→Persian, 80.1 reverse). The G2P Transformer outperformed mBART (72.3 vs. 62.2 chrF++) despite limited data, while mT5 with subword tokenization failed badly (chrF++ < 18.5).

Conclusion: For Tajik–Persian transliteration, byte- and character-level architectures clearly outperform multilingual Seq2Seq models based on subword tokenization, highlighting the importance of operating at finer granularity for cross-script transliteration tasks.

Abstract: This paper presents the first comprehensive comparative analysis of modern machine learning architectures for transliteration between Tajik (Cyrillic script) and Persian (Arabic script). A key contribution is the creation and validation of a unique parallel corpus aggregated from multiple heterogeneous sources, including crowdsourced projects, lexicographic pairs, parallel texts of "Shahnameh", diplomatic articles, texts of "Masnavi-i Ma'navi", official terminology lists, and transliterated correspondences. The initial dataset comprised 328,253 sentence pairs; a representative subset of 40,000 pairs was formed using stratified random sampling.
  The experiment compared six classes of models: rule-based baseline, LSTM with attention, character-level Transformer, G2P Transformer (trained from scratch), pre-trained multilingual models (mBART, mT5 with LoRA), and byte-level ByT5. Results demonstrate the overwhelming superiority of ByT5 (chrF++ 87.4 for Tajik to Farsi, 80.1 for reverse). The G2P Transformer significantly outperformed mBART (72.3 vs. 62.2 chrF++) despite limited data. Models using subword tokenization (mT5) failed completely (chrF++ less than 18.5).
  The findings demonstrate that for accurate transliteration of the Tajik-Farsi pair, architectures operating at the byte or character level are unequivocally more effective than traditional multilingual Seq2Seq models relying on subword tokenization.

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CECoR is a reasoning-aware framework for factual error correction that decomposes multi-hop claims into steps, synthesizes training data via controlled perturbations, and trains with a two-stage SFT+RL approach to improve multi-hop and single-hop factual correction robustness.

  <summary>Details</summary>

They struggle on multi-hop cases that require compositional reasoning over several pieces of evidence. This difficulty is intensified by the scarcity of paired training data and by the challenge of pinpointing where, within a complex reasoning chain, the semantic error lies. The paper aims to address these key obstacles so that factual correction systems can handle more realistic, multi-step reasoning scenarios.

Method: The authors propose CECoR, a reasoning-aware framework built around a Decomposition and Injection paradigm. First, CECoR decomposes complex multi-hop claims into explicit, interpretable reasoning steps. Then it injects controlled perturbations into these steps to synthesize high-quality training pairs for factual error correction. Training is performed in two stages: supervised fine-tuning on the synthesized data and then reinforcement learning to further optimize factual accuracy and robustness. This approach encourages the model to understand and correct errors at the level of reasoning steps instead of treating the entire claim as indivisible.

Result: In experimental evaluations on multi-hop factual error correction benchmarks, CECoR achieves strong performance, surpassing both distantly supervised approaches and few-shot large language model baselines. The framework also transfers well to single-hop correction tasks and maintains stability when the supporting evidence is noisy, indicating robust generalization and practical applicability beyond the specific multi-hop benchmarks.

Conclusion: CECoR demonstrates that explicitly modeling reasoning structure and synthesizing targeted training data via decomposition and perturbation can significantly improve factual error correction, especially for multi-hop claims. The two-stage SFT+RL training further enhances accuracy and robustness. Overall, the framework provides a versatile and effective solution for real-world factual correction scenarios that involve complex, compositional reasoning over multiple evidence sources.

Abstract: Factual Error Correction (FEC) aims to revise inaccurate text into statements that are factually consistent with external evidence. Although recent methods perform well on single-hop correction, they often treat claims as atomic units and struggle with multi-hop cases that require compositional reasoning across multiple evidence sources. This challenge is further amplified by limited paired data and difficulties in locating semantic errors within complex reasoning chains. We present CECoR (Compositional Error Correction via Reasoning-aware Synthesis), a reasoning-aware framework that introduces a Decomposition and Injection paradigm for compositional error correction. CECoR decomposes multi-hop claims into interpretable reasoning steps and injects controlled perturbations to synthesize high-quality training pairs. A two-stage learning strategy combining supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning improves factual accuracy and robustness. Comprehensive evaluations show that CECoR achieves strong performance on multi-hop benchmarks, outperforming both distantly supervised methods and few-shot LLM baselines. It also generalizes effectively to single-hop correction and remains stable under noisy evidence, demonstrating its versatility for real-world factual correction.

At the technological level, research paradigms have evolved from rule-based systems and traditional machine learning to large language models (LLMs). At the theoretical level, argumentation theory and legal dogmatics provide important references for modeling argumentation structures. However, despite ongoing progress, the overall development of legal argument mining remains relatively slow. Building on a systematic review of existing research, this study conducts an in-depth analysis and finds that this is due not only to data scarcity or technical limitations, but more fundamentally to the lack of a structured representational approach that reconciles theoretical expressiveness with computational feasibility. ### [76] [Decoding-Time Debiasing via Process Reward Models: From Controlled Fill-in to Open-Ended Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02348)
*Muneeb Ur Raheem Khan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a decoding-time debiasing framework for large language models that uses a separate reward model to steer generation toward fair and fluent outputs, without modifying model weights.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models inherit and propagate social biases from their training data, reinforcing harmful stereotypes across dimensions like gender, race, religion, disability, age, and socioeconomic status. Existing mitigation methods such as retraining on curated data or RLHF-style fine-tuning are costly, require access to model weights, and can harm general performance. There is a need for a cheaper, more flexible, and non-invasive way to reduce bias that works even with closed-source or frozen models.

Method: The authors treat bias mitigation as a structured search problem at decoding time. A separate Process Reward Model (PRM) evaluates candidate continuations for both fairness (low bias) and fluency. They design three decoding-time schemes: (1) Best-of-N, which generates multiple candidates and selects the one with the best PRM score; (2) Sequential critique-and-revise, where the PRM iteratively critiques and revises candidates to reduce bias; and (3) Constitutional self-audit, where the model performs self-critique guided by fairness principles. They test these schemes on four LLMs (GPT-4o-mini, Llama 3.2 3B, Gemma 3 4B, Qwen 2.5 3B) using a 200-prompt bilingual benchmark (English and Urdu) spanning eight types of social bias. They also extend the approach to open-ended generation, debiasing each token in real time and adding a Bias Guard mechanism that triggers PRM evaluation only for potentially biased tokens to control computational overhead. They formalize an overhead metric that separates generator cost from judge (PRM) cost.

Result: Sequential critique-and-revise yields the strongest debiasing, improving average bias scores by up to +0.40 compared to baseline generations, while maintaining or sometimes enhancing fluency. All three decoding schemes can be extended to open-ended token-by-token debiasing, with the Bias Guard keeping runtime overhead around 2x for well-calibrated models. The new overhead metric shows that Best-of-N decoding imposes negligible extra cost on the generator itself when implemented natively, with most cost attributable to the PRM. Experiments across the four models demonstrate that the framework is effective and scales with model capability, with GPT-4o-mini serving as a strong high-performance anchor and the smaller open-weight models revealing remaining limitations in bias control at small scale.

Conclusion: Decoding-time debiasing using a separate Process Reward Model is a practical, model-agnostic way to mitigate social bias in LLM outputs without retraining or fine-tuning. Structured search schemes like sequential critique-and-revise can substantially reduce bias while preserving fluency, and can be applied both to short-form and open-ended generation. With mechanisms like Bias Guard and a clear accounting of generator versus judge cost, the approach offers a scalable, relatively inexpensive path to fairer LLM behavior that works across both proprietary and open-weight models, though smaller models still show non-trivial limitations.

Abstract: Large language models pick up social biases from the data they are trained on and carry those biases into downstream applications, often reinforcing stereotypes around gender, race, religion, disability, age, and socioeconomic status. The standard fixes (retraining on curated data or fine-tuning with human feedback) are expensive, need access to model weights, and risk degrading the model on other tasks. In this paper we take a different route: we debias the model at decoding time, treating bias mitigation as a structured search over candidate tokens without ever touching model weights. A separate Process Reward Model (PRM) acts as a judge, scoring each candidate for both fairness and fluency. We design three schemes of increasing sophistication (Best-of-N selection, Sequential critique-and-revise, and Constitutional self-audit) and evaluate them on four models (GPT-4o-mini, Llama 3.2 3B, Gemma 3 4B, Qwen 2.5 3B) across a 200-prompt bilingual benchmark in English and Urdu covering eight bias categories. Sequential debiasing proves the most effective, raising mean bias scores by up to +0.40 over baseline while preserving (and sometimes improving) fluency. We then extend all three schemes to open-ended generation, where each token is debiased on the fly, and introduce a lightweight Bias Guard gate that fires only on potentially biased words, keeping overhead near 2x for well-calibrated models. A formal overhead metric that separates generator cost from judge cost reveals that Best-of-N is effectively free on the generator side in a native implementation. GPT-4o-mini, included as a strong proprietary anchor, confirms that the framework scales with model capability; the three open-weight models show where current small-scale LLMs still struggle.

</details>

The multi-layer evaluation provides fine-grained diagnostics on where models fail: code execution, API misuse, or chemically incorrect behavior. The benchmark functions as a robust testbed that surfaces gaps between general coding ability and domain-specific molecular reasoning.

Conclusion: MolViBench fills a key evaluation gap by providing the first specialized benchmark and evaluation framework for Molecular Vibe Coding. It enables rigorous, fine-grained assessment of LLMs’ ability to generate executable, chemically correct code across realistic drug discovery workflows, and offers a practical foundation for improving and comparing AI systems in molecular and drug design applications.

Abstract: Molecular Vibe Coding, a paradigm where chemists interact with LLMs to generate executable programs for molecular tasks, has emerged as a flexible alternative to chemical agents with predefined tools, enabling chemists to express arbitrarily complex, customized workflows. Unlike general coding tasks, molecular coding imposes a distinctive challenge that LLMs should jointly equip programming, molecular understanding, and domain-specific reasoning capabilities. General code generation benchmarks such as HumanEval and SWE-bench require no chemistry knowledge, while chemistry-focused benchmarks such as S^2-Bench and ChemCoTBench evaluate knowledge recall or property prediction rather than executable code generation. To bridge this gap, we introduce MolViBench, the first benchmark tailored for Molecular Vibe Coding. We systematically evaluate 9 frontier coding LLMs and compare three real-world Molecular Vibe Coding paradigms, providing a practical and fine-grained testbed for diagnosing LLMs' coding capabilities in AI-accelerated molecular discovery.

### [78] [InfoLaw: Information Scaling Laws for Large Language Models with Quality-Weighted Mixture Data and Repetition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02364)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing scaling laws fail to generalize across different data mixtures and repetition patterns, especially in data-limited or overtrained settings, making it difficult to design optimal high/low-quality data recipes. Upweighting high-quality data can improve performance but may cause harmful repetition and degradation when data is scarce, so a more data-aware, generalizable scaling framework is needed.

Method: The authors conceptualize pretraining as information accumulation where data quality controls information density and repetition yields scale-dependent diminishing returns. They run controlled LLM pretraining experiments over datasets that differ in total scale, quality distributions, and repetition levels, collecting resulting losses. They then fit an information-based scaling model—InfoLaw—that maps consumed tokens, model size, mixture weights, and repetition into an effective information measure that predicts final loss; they validate it on unseen mixtures and larger scales.

Result: InfoLaw achieves very accurate loss prediction for unseen data recipes and at larger training scales (up to 7B parameters and 425B tokens), with mean absolute error of 0.15% and maximum error of 0.96% in loss. It reliably extrapolates across different overtraining levels and mixture recipes, unlike standard scaling laws, and is therefore usable to evaluate tradeoffs like upweighting high-quality data vs. inducing repetition under a fixed compute budget.

Conclusion: The proposed InfoLaw framework successfully extends scaling laws to be data- and repetition-aware, enabling robust prediction of LLM performance across data mixtures, scales, and overtraining regimes. This allows practitioners to efficiently search and optimize data recipes and compute allocations, particularly in data-limited settings where naive upweighting of high-quality data might otherwise hurt performance.

Abstract: Upweighting high-quality data in LLM pretraining often improves performance, but in datalimited regimes, especially under overtraining, stronger upweighting increases repetition and can degrade performance. However, standard scaling laws do not reliably extrapolate across mixture recipes or under repetitions, making the selection for optimal data recipes at scaling underdetermined. To solve this, we introduce InfoLaw (Information Scaling Laws), a data-aware scaling framework that predicts loss from consumed tokens, model size, data mixture weights, and repetition. The key idea is to model pretraining as information accumulation, where quality controls information density and repetition induces scaledependent diminishing returns. We first collect the model performance after training on datasets that vary in scale, quality distribution, and repetition level. Then we build up the modeling for information so that information accurately predicts those model performance. InfoLaw predicts performance on unseen data recipes and larger scale runs (up to 7B, 425B tokens) with 0.15% mean and 0.96% max absolute error in loss, and it extrapolates reliably across overtraining levels, enabling efficient data-recipe selection under varying compute budgets.

Result: LLM-based workflows outperform embedding-based baselines in both retrieving correct prior-art passages for individual features and identifying which claim features are novel. Conclusion: Fine-grained, feature-level modeling of patent novelty better matches the real examination process and yields more accurate and robust novelty assessments than coarse claim-level classifiers. The FiNE-Patents dataset and accompanying code provide a foundation for future research on transparent, granular patent analysis and on LLM-based workflows for legal-technical reasoning tasks.

The process involves intricate matching between specific features of a patent claim and passages in the prior art. While prior work has approached novelty prediction primarily as a binary classification task at the claim level, we argue that this formulation is susceptible to spurious correlations and lacks the granularity required for practical application. In this work, we introduce FiNE-Patents (Fine-grained Novelty Examination of Patents), a novel dataset comprising 3,658 first patent claims annotated with fine-grained, feature-level prior art references extracted from European Search Opinion (ESOP) documents. We implement and evaluate LLM-based workflows that decompose claims into features, analyze each feature against prior art, and finally derive a claim-level novelty prediction. Our experiments demonstrate that these workflows outperform embedding-based baselines on passage retrieval and novel feature identification. Furthermore, we show that unlike trained classifiers, LLMs are robust against spurious correlations present in the claim-level novelty classification task. We release the dataset and code to foster further research into transparent and granular patent analysis.

Result: Using argument components and their checkworthiness/hatefulness annotations as features, the models achieve promising performance, with F1 scores up to 96% for predicting the hatefulness of full messages, showing that argumentative structure is highly informative for this task.

Conclusion: Argument structure, enriched with checkworthiness and hatefulness annotations at the component level, offers an effective way to infer the hatefulness of entire messages. This supports the idea that argumentation analysis can serve as a bridge between different facets of information disorder and is a promising direction for future research on detecting and countering hateful content online.

Abstract: Information disorder is a challenging phenomenon that affects society at large. This phenomenon entails the diffusion of misleading, misinforming, and hateful content online. In different contexts, one aspect of the problem may prevail, but overall, this is a broad problem that requires comprehensive solutions. While each dimension of the problem (hate speech, disinformation, misinformation, etc.) requires in-depth analysis, in this paper, we look into the possibility of argument structure to provide relevant information to link these different areas of the problem. In particular, we focus on the WSF-ARG+ dataset, which consists of white supremacy forum messages annotated in terms of argument structure (premises and conclusion). There, we leverage the checkworthiness and hatefulness annotations of the argument components to obtain insights into the hatefulness of the whole message. Our results show promising insights (up to 96% F1), indicating the possibility of extending this direction in the future to tackle hateful content identification and information disorder countering.

### [84] [ATLAS: Article Tracking, Linking, and Analysis of Swedish Encyclopedias](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02466)

Method: They design a multi-step pipeline that, given raw OCR text, (1) detects and extracts headwords and entry boundaries, (2) classifies headwords into categories, (3) aligns corresponding entries across different editions of the encyclopedia, and (4) links entries to Wikidata items. Often, however, this process does not go beyond an optical character recognition, leaving all the underlying structure unexploited. In addition, many encyclopedias had multiple editions reflecting the evolution of knowledge. The lack of structure in the raw text makes it difficult to track changes across these editions. In this work, we built a pipeline to restore the text structure, where we extract the headwords and identify entries; categorize the entities; match entries across editions; and link entries to a Wikidata item. We applied this pipeline to the four major editions of \textit{Nordisk familjebok}, an authoritative Swedish encyclopedia published between 1876 and 1951. We could extract the headwords with an F1 score of 97.8\% and we obtained an F1 score of 93.4\% on the headword classification. On a small-scale evaluation, we reached a 93\% precision on the cross-edition matching, 85\% precision and 16.5\% recall on the Wikidata linking. This shows that an automated approach to digitized historical knowledge is possible. This should facilitate the preservation of general knowledge and the understanding of knowledge transmission. ### [85] [Accurate Legal Reasoning at Scale: Neuro-Symbolic Offloading and Structural Auditability for Robust Legal Adjudication](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02472)
*Stanisław Sójka,Witold Kowalczyk*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a neuro-symbolic system that converts complex legal clauses into an executable graph language (DACL), enabling cheap, deterministic, and auditable legal reasoning that outperforms large reasoning models in consistency and cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Legal texts contain computational clauses whose correct interpretation requires precise, complex reasoning. Current frontier Large Reasoning Models can read and paraphrase these clauses but make subtle reasoning mistakes, are non-deterministic, expensive to run at scale, and provide poor audit trails—problems that make them unsuitable for production legal adjudication. The authors want a way to get LLM-level understanding once, then rely on cheap, reliable, and auditable execution thereafter.

Method: They introduce "Amortized Intelligence": use an LLM a single time to translate legal clauses into Deterministic Autonomous Contract Language (DACL), a typed graph-based intermediate representation. After this compilation step, adjudication of cases is performed by deterministically executing the DACL graph, which yields a visually inspectable trace. They benchmark a DACL-based agent against strong runtime LRM baselines (e.g., GPT-5.2, Gemini 3 Pro) on legal reasoning tasks and measure consistency, reasoning robustness, and compute costs.

Result: The DACL-based agent exhibits near-perfect consistency across adjudications and avoids the sharp performance drop-off ("reasoning cliff") seen in probabilistic LRMs as cases get more complex. It also satisfies legal auditability requirements via its explicit execution traces. In high-volume workflows, using DACL for execution reduces compute costs by more than 90% compared to repeatedly querying LRMs at runtime.

Conclusion: Compiling legal clauses once into a deterministic, graph-based contract language (DACL) and then executing that representation is a practical alternative to continuous reliance on LRMs. This amortized intelligence paradigm offers high reasoning reliability, strong auditability, and large cost savings, suggesting a promising direction for deploying AI in real-world legal adjudication systems.

Abstract: Legal texts often contain computational legal clauses--provisions whose understanding requires complex logic. While frontier Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) can describe such clauses, building production-ready systems is limited by reasoning errors and the high cost of inference. We propose Amortized Intelligence, a neuro-symbolic approach where we use an LLM once to translate a legal text into Deterministic Autonomous Contract Language (DACL): a typed graph intermediate representation. Adjudication then relies on deterministic graph executions with a visually auditable trace. In comparison against runtime LRM baselines (including GPT-5.2 and Gemini 3 Pro), our DACL-based Agent achieves near-perfect consistency and mitigates the "reasoning cliff" observed in probabilistic models. The system reduces compute costs by over 90% in high-volume workflows while satisfying the strict auditability requirements of legal adjudication.

Abstract: Most hallucination evaluations focus on English, leaving it unclear whether findings transfer to lower-resource languages. We investigate faithfulness hallucinations, defined as model-generated content that is fluent and plausible but diverges from the provided input or is internally inconsistent. Leveraging the multilingual MultiWikiQA dataset, we utilize the LettuceDetect framework to create synthetic hallucination datasets for 306 languages, from which we train token-level hallucination classifiers for 30 European languages. In this work, we present evaluations of model hallucinations on a selection of languages: English, Danish, German, and Icelandic. Using these classifiers, we evaluate the hallucination rates for Qwen3-0.6B, Qwen3-14B, Gemma-3-12B-IT, cogito-v1-preview-qwen-32B, and cogito-v1-preview-llama-70B. Our classifiers reveal notably higher hallucination rates for Qwen3-0.6B (up to 60\% of answers containing at least one hallucination, peaking in Icelandic) and generally lower rates for larger models, with cogito-v1-preview-qwen-32B and cogito-v1-preview-llama-70B performing best on most languages. Hallucination rates are consistently higher for lower-resource languages, particularly Icelandic.

Abstract: Semantic Role Labeling (SRL) provides an explicit representation of predicate-argument structure, capturing linguistically grounded relations such as who did what to whom. Traditionally, SRL systems have often relied on AllenNLP; however, the framework entered maintenance mode in December 2022, limiting compatibility with evolving encoder architectures and modern inference requirements. We revisit structured SRL modeling, introducing a modernized encoder-based framework that preserves explicit predicate-argument structure while enabling inference 10 times faster. Using BERT-base, the model attains comparable predictive performance, and RoBERTa and DeBERTa further improve F1 performance within the same framework. We adopt a dependency-informed diagnostic methodology to characterize span-level inconsistencies and conduct a representation-level analysis of LLM behavior under dependency-informed structural signals. Results indicate that dependency cues primarily improve structural stability. Finally, we illustrate how the framework's explicit predicate-argument structure can support multilingual SRL projection as a downstream application.
