### [88] [Benchmarking Retrieval Strategies for Biomedical Retrieval-Augmented Generation: A Controlled Empirical Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02520)
*Devi Prasad Bal,Subhashree Puhan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper systematically compares five retrieval strategies for RAG in biomedical QA and finds that Cross-Encoder Reranking performs best, though dense retrieval is nearly as strong, and all RAG setups greatly outperform no-context generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although RAG is widely used to ground LLM outputs, there has been no controlled, multi-metric comparison of retrieval strategies specifically in the high-stakes biomedical domain. Practitioners lack clear guidance on which retrieval method offers the best trade-offs in accuracy, faithfulness, and relevance when everything else in the RAG stack is held constant.

Method: The authors construct a controlled RAG pipeline for biomedical question answering in which only the retrieval component is varied. They compare five retrieval strategies: Dense Vector Search, Hybrid BM25 + Dense retrieval, Cross-Encoder Reranking, Multi-Query Expansion, and Maximal Marginal Relevance. The generation model (GPT-4o-mini), embeddings (OpenAI text-embedding-3-small), and vector store (ChromaDB) are fixed. Evaluation is performed on 250 question-answer pairs from a curated BioASQ subset (rag-mini-bioasq), using four DeepEval metrics—contextual precision, contextual recall, faithfulness, and answer relevancy—each with 95% confidence intervals. A no-context ablation (no retrieval) serves as a baseline lower bound.

Result: Cross-Encoder Reranking yields the best overall composite metric (0.827) and the highest contextual precision (0.852), demonstrating that modeling fine-grained query-document interactions improves retrieval quality. Dense retrieval alone is very competitive, with a composite score of 0.822, just 0.005 below the best method. Multi-Query Expansion, despite being designed for higher recall, attains the lowest contextual precision (0.671), implying that naive query diversification leads to noisy, less useful contexts. MMR emphasizes diversity at the cost of answer relevancy. All RAG configurations substantially outperform the no-context baseline in answer relevancy (0.658–0.701 vs. 0.287), confirming retrieval’s strong practical benefit.

Conclusion: In biomedical QA RAG systems, more sophisticated retrieval methods that explicitly model query-document interactions, such as Cross-Encoder Reranking, can deliver measurable gains in contextual precision and overall performance, though strong dense retrieval baselines remain highly competitive. Recall-oriented or diversity-focused techniques like naive Multi-Query Expansion and MMR must be carefully tuned to avoid degrading precision and answer relevancy. Overall, incorporating any of the tested retrieval strategies significantly improves answer quality compared with no retrieval, and the publicly released pipeline and code enable further benchmarking and extension.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) offers a well-established path to grounding large language model (LLM) outputs in external knowledge, yet the question of which retrieval strategy works best in a high-stakes domain such as biomedicine has not received the controlled, multi-metric treatment it deserves. This paper presents a systematic empirical comparison of five retrieval strategies -- Dense Vector Search, Hybrid BM25 + Dense retrieval, Cross-Encoder Reranking, Multi-Query Expansion, and Maximal Marginal Relevance (MMR) -- within a biomedical question-answering RAG pipeline. All strategies share a fixed generation model (GPT-4o-mini), a common vector store (ChromaDB), and OpenAI's text-embedding-3-small embeddings, ensuring that observed differences are attributable to retrieval alone. Evaluation is conducted on 250 question-answer pairs drawn from a preprocessed subset of the BioASQ benchmark (rag-mini-bioasq) using four DeepEval metrics: contextual precision, contextual recall, faithfulness, and answer relevancy, each reported with 95% confidence intervals. A no-context ablation is included as a lower bound. Cross-Encoder Reranking achieves the best composite score (0.827) and highest contextual precision (0.852), confirming that query-document interaction yields measurable retrieval gains. Multi-Query Expansion, despite its recall-oriented design, produces the weakest contextual precision (0.671), suggesting naive query diversification introduces retrieval noise. MMR sacrifices answer relevancy for diversity, while the Dense baseline (composite 0.822) falls within 0.005 points of the top strategy. All RAG conditions dramatically outperform the no-context ablation on answer relevancy (0.658-0.701 vs. 0.287), confirming the practical value of retrieval. The full pipeline, hyperparameters, and evaluation code are publicly available.

Abstract: We present our shared task on evaluating the adaptability of LLMs and NLP systems across multiple languages and cultures. As the task is designed strictly for evaluation, participants were not permitted to use the data for training, fine-tuning, few-shot learning, or any other form of model modification. Our task includes two tracks: (a) Short-Answer Questions (SAQ) and (b) Multiple-Choice Questions (MCQ). Participants were required to predict labels and were allowed to submit any NLP system and adopt diverse modelling strategies, provided that the benchmark was used solely for evaluation. The task attracted more than 140 registered participants, and we received final submissions from 62 teams, along with 19 system description papers. We report the results and present an analysis of the best-performing systems and the most commonly adopted approaches.   <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to understand which architectures are practically better when annotated data is limited, a common situation for many languages.

Morphological complexity is found to be a significant secondary predictor of transformers' relative disadvantage after controlling for corpus size.

Conclusion: For low-resource languages, particularly with small treebanks and higher morphological complexity, the Biaffine LSTM is currently a more suitable choice for dependency parsing tools. Abstract: Transformer-based models achieve state-of-the-art dependency parsing for high-resource languages, yet their advantage over simpler architectures in low-resource settings remains poorly understood. We find that the Biaffine LSTM consistently outperforms transformer models in low-resource regimes, with transformers recovering their advantage as training data increases. The crossover falls within a resource range typical of treebanks for under-resourced languages. ### [91] [Beating the Style Detector: Three Hours of Agentic Research on the AI-Text Arms Race](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02620)
*Andreas Maier,Moritz Zaiss,Siming Bayer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that modern agentic-research tooling can fully reproduce and extend an empirical NLP study on personal-style post-editing, and uses the same setup to study AI-text detection and evasion.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Empirical NLP studies are hard to reproduce and extend, often taking weeks of human effort. At the same time, LLMs are increasingly used for style mimicking and raise concerns around AI-text detection and evasion. The authors want to test whether an agentic research framework can rapidly and reliably reproduce a complex ACL paper, quantify how well frontier LLMs can mimic personal writing style, and examine how easily such systems can evade AI-text detectors.

Method: Using the original data from an ACL 2026 paper on personal-style post-editing, the authors use an agentic research harness where the human plays only a reviewer-in-the-loop role. They re-run all original experiments and add three new ones. They evaluate GPT-5.5 and Claude Opus 4.7 on 324 paired style-mimicry tasks, measure perceived and embedding-based self-similarity, and compare to a same-author ceiling and human post-edits. Then they reframe the dataset as an AI-text detection problem: they train leave-authors-out linear SVM detectors on LUAR-MUD embeddings across approaches, analyze detection performance and confounds (e.g., length), and run an adversarial feedback loop where an Opus agent iteratively adapts (T=20 iterations) against a frozen detector to reduce its detectability.

Result: The agentic pipeline accurately reproduces all seven preregistered hypotheses of the original study, including the key correlation between perceived and embedding-based self-similarity (r=+0.244, p<1e-8, n=648). Under a leakage-free held-out protocol, GPT-5.5 and Claude Opus 4.7 close 71–75% of the style gap to a same-author upper bound on 324 tasks, compared to only 24% for human post-editing, and they outperform human post-editing on about 80% of tasks. As AI-text detectors, leave-authors-out linear SVMs on LUAR-MUD embeddings achieve AUCs of 0.93–1.00; diagnostics indicate that detection of GPT-5.5 mainly reflects text length, whereas detection of Opus reflects a true stylistic signal. In adversarial testing, an Opus-based agent with 20 rounds of feedback can flip 2 of 5 held-out mimics into the human side of the classifier and reduce all margins by an order of magnitude, substantially lowering its detection probability.

Conclusion: Agentic-research frameworks can now fully reproduce and extend complex empirical NLP studies with minimal human intervention. Frontier LLMs like GPT-5.5 and Claude Opus 4.7 are highly effective at personal-style mimicry, often surpassing human post-editing relative to a same-author ceiling. Standard embedding-based detectors can achieve very high AUC, but their performance may partially rely on simple confounds such as length and can be weakened by targeted adversarial optimization. With moderate, structured feedback against a known detector, a strong LLM can meaningfully reduce its own AI-text detection likelihood. The authors release all data, code, detectors, diagnostics, and adversarial trajectories to support further research and reproducibility.

Abstract: Reproducing an empirical NLP study used to take weeks. Given the released data and a modern agentic-research harness, we redo every experiment of a recent ACL\,2026 study on personal-style post-editing of LLM drafts -- and add three new ones -- with the human investigator acting only as a reviewer-in-the-loop. We reproduce all seven preregistered hypotheses and recover the paper's headline correlation between perceived self-similarity and embedding-measured self-similarity to three decimal places ($r{=}{+}0.244$, $p{<}10^{-8}$, $n{=}648$). Under a leakage-free held-out protocol, GPT-5.5 and Claude\,Opus\,4.7 close $71$--$75\,\%$ of the style gap to the same-author ceiling on $324$ paired tasks, against $24\,\%$ for the human post-edit, and beat the human post-edit on $\sim$$80\,\%$ of tasks. We then frame the same data as an AI-text detection arms race. A leave-authors-out linear SVM on LUAR-MUD embeddings reaches AUC $0.93$--$1.00$ across approaches; six diagnostics show that GPT-5.5 detection is mostly a length confound while Opus detection is a genuine stylistic signature. Given $T{=}20$ feedback iterations against the frozen detector, an Opus agent flips two of five held-out test mimics to the human half-space and shrinks every margin by an order of magnitude. With moderate effort against a known detector, a frontier LLM can already efficiently lower its own AI-detection probability. All code, $648$ mimic drafts, trained detectors, diagnostics, and adversarial trajectories are released.

### [92] [Synthetic Users, Real Differences: an Evaluation Framework for User Simulation in Multi-Turn Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02624)
*Yu Lu Liu,Hyokun Yun,Tanya Roosta,Ziang Xiao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents realsim, a framework to evaluate how realistic user simulations are compared to real user-chatbot dialogues across multiple dimensions and domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: User simulations are increasingly used to evaluate AI chatbots instead of collecting costly real user interactions, but current methods for checking how realistic these simulations are are coarse, mostly per-dialogue, and ignore distributional properties across many dialogues and aspects of interaction. There is a need for a more fine-grained, multi-dimensional, corpus-level assessment of realism.

Method: The authors propose realsim, an evaluation framework that compares real and simulated dialogues from a distributional perspective along 8 carefully defined dimensions that cover communicative functions, user states, and surface-level properties of user messages. They instantiate the framework using a curated dataset of 1,000 multi-turn, task-oriented, real user-chatbot dialogues spanning 16 application domains, and use this setup to analyze how well simulated users match real ones across these dimensions and domains.

Result: Applying realsim reveals that simulated users systematically fail to capture many types of communication friction present in real interactions, such as misunderstandings, hesitations, or corrections, leading to simulations that are smoother than reality. The analysis also shows substantial variability in simulation realism across different application domains.

Conclusion: Evaluations based on current user simulators may be overly optimistic because these simulators under-represent real-world communication challenges. The findings suggest the importance of distributional, multi-dimensional realism checks such as those provided by realsim, and indicate that building domain-specific user simulators may be necessary to achieve realistic evaluation across varied chatbot applications.

Abstract: There is growing interest in exploring user simulation as an alternative to gathering and scoring real user-chatbot interactions for AI chatbot evaluation. For this purpose, it is important to ensure the realism of the simulation, i.e., the extent to which simulated dialogues reflect real dialogues users have with chatbots. Most existing methods evaluating simulation realism produce coarse quality signal and remain solely at the level of individual dialogues. To support more rigorous evaluation in this area, we propose realsim, an evaluation framework that enables practitioners to take a distributional view of real vs. simulated dialogues along 8 dimensions, covering attributes related to the communicative functions of the interaction, user states, and the surface form of user messages. We then instantiate the framework with a curated dataset of 1K multi-turn task-focused real user-chatbot dialogues that cover 16 domains of chatbot applications. Overall, we find that simulated users tend to struggle at capturing communication frictions that real users introduce to interactions, which could make evaluations based on such simulations overly optimistic. We also observe variability in performance across different domains, which may indicate a need for domain-specific user simulators.

*Lorella Viola*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a multi-layer network framework to better detect and track emerging, low-frequency online narratives related to information disorder in Italian HPV vaccine discourse over 14 years on X.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing computational analyses of online narratives use conservative network constructions and community detection that miss sparse but important early signals of information disorder, making it hard to see how problematic narratives emerge, are reframed, and spread across coalitions over time.

Method: The authors build hashtag co-occurrence networks from 14 years of Italian X posts about the HPV vaccine and design a dual-layer framework. First, they apply conservative community detection to identify robust, stable core discourse coalitions. Second, they add a coverage layer that projects low-frequency, fringe hashtags into these core coalitions using weighted connectivity. They use manually labeled skeptical and conspiratorial seed tweets to evaluate how well the method recovers long-tail problematic hashtags while preserving clear coalition structure.

Result: The core layer reveals a stable, prevention-oriented backbone in the discourse, alongside increasingly distinct skepticism coalitions. Adding the coverage layer substantially improves the detection and recovery of rare, problematic hashtags linked to skepticism and conspiracy, without breaking the interpretability of the coalition map.

Conclusion: The proposed core-plus-coverage framework effectively captures both stable discourse coalitions and emerging, low-frequency signals of information disorder. It allows researchers to map where and how polarized and problematic narratives about the HPV vaccine are reframed and amplified over time, offering a generalizable methodology for studying the structural maturation of information disorder in online discourse.

Abstract: Understanding how online narratives travel through coalitions is critical for identifying information disorder, yet computational analyses often rely on conservative network constructions that erase initially sparse but salient signals. This paper proposes a novel multi-layer framework that captures low-frequency signals of emerging information disorder allowing for locating where online discourse is reframed and amplified over time. The use case is 14 years of Italian discourse on X regarding the Human Papillomavirus (HPV) vaccine across three pivotal epochs (2010-2024). Utilizing hashtag co-occurrence networks, we introduce a dual-layer approach. We first identify robust core discourse coalitions through conservative community detection, revealing a stable prevention-oriented backbone contrasted with increasingly separable skepticism coalitions. We then introduce a coverage layer and project fringe hashtags into core coalitions based on weighted connectivity. Using a manually labelled set of skeptical and conspiratorial seed tweets, we demonstrate that this core-coverage projection significantly improves the recovery of long-tail, problematic hashtags while preserving an interpretable coalition structure. Our findings characterize the structural maturation of polarized narratives and provide a methodology for mapping how discourse is reframed and amplified by information disorder over time.

</details>


### [94] [ContextualJailbreak: Evolutionary Red-Teaming via Simulated Conversational Priming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02647)
*Mario Rodríguez Béjar,Francisco J. Cortés-Delgado,S. Braghin,Jose L. Hernández-Ramos*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ContextualJailbreak, an evolutionary, multi-turn, contextual red-teaming framework that optimizes jailbreak dialogues against LLMs using graded harm feedback and semantic mutation operators, achieving very high attack success rates and highlighting cross-model robustness differences.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are vulnerable to jailbreak attacks, especially via multi-turn contextual priming that current automated red-teaming tools poorly capture because they focus on single-turn prompts and static optimization. There is a need for a systematic, optimization-based way to explore and exploit the multi-turn priming space that better reflects realistic conversational attacks and uncovers weaknesses in advanced models.

Method: The authors propose ContextualJailbreak, a black-box evolutionary search method over simulated multi-turn dialogues. It uses a two-level judge that assigns 0–5 harm scores to model responses, using these graded scores as fitness signals rather than discarding partially harmful outputs. The search mutates dialogues with five semantic operators—roleplay, scenario, expand, troubleshooting, and mechanistic (the last two being novel)—to iteratively evolve more effective jailbreak conversations.

Result: On 50 HarmBench behaviors, ContextualJailbreak attains 100% attack success on several strong open models (gpt-oss:20B, qwen3-8B, llama3.1:70B) and 90% on gpt-oss:120B, outperforming four baseline methods by 31–96 percentage points. The most harmful prompts found for gpt-oss:120B transfer well to several leading closed models, with high success rates on gpt-4o-mini, gpt-5, and gemini-3-flash, but substantially lower transfer to claude-opus-4-7 and claude-sonnet-4-6.

Conclusion: ContextualJailbreak demonstrates that evolutionary search over semantically structured multi-turn priming, guided by graded harm scores, is a highly effective way to jailbreak LLMs, far surpassing single-turn and simpler multi-turn attacks. Its transfer results reveal large differences in alignment robustness across providers, suggesting that some safety architectures are more resilient to sophisticated contextual attacks than others.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) remain vulnerable to jailbreak attacks that bypass safety alignment and elicit harmful responses. A growing body of work shows that contextual priming, where earlier turns covertly bias later replies, constitutes a powerful attack surface, with hand-crafted multi-turn scaffolds consistently outperforming single-turn manipulations on capable models.
  However, automated optimization-based red-teaming has remained largely limited to the single-turn setting, iterating over static prompts and lacking the ability to reason about which forms of conversational priming induce compliance. While recent multi-turn, search-based approaches have begun to bridge this gap, the mutator design space underlying effective primed dialogues remains largely unexplored.
  We present ContextualJailbreak, a black-box red-teaming strategy that performs evolutionary search over a simulated multi-turn primed dialogue. The strategy leverages a graded 0-5 harm score from a two-level judge as an in-loop signal, enabling partially harmful responses to guide the search process rather than being discarded.
  Search is driven by five semantically defined mutation operators: roleplay, scenario, expand, troubleshooting, and mechanistic, of which the last two are novel contributions of this work.
  Across 50 representative HarmBench behaviors, ContextualJailbreak achieves an ASR of 100% on gpt-oss:20B, 100% on qwen3-8B, 100% on llama3.1:70B, and 90% on gpt-oss:120B, outperforming four single- and multi-turn baselines by 31-96 percentage points on average.
  The 40 maximally harmful attacks discovered against gpt-oss:120B transfer without adaptation to closed frontier models, achieving 90.0% on gpt-4o-mini, 70.0% on gpt-5, and 70.0% on gemini-3-flash, but only 17.5% on claude-opus-4-7 and 15.0% on claude-sonnet-4-6, revealing a pronounced provider-level asymmetry in alignment robustness.

</details>


### [95] [Fuzzy Fingerprinting Encoder Pre-trained Language Models for Emotion Recognition in Conversations: Human Assessment and Validity Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02665)
*Patrícia Pereira,Helena Moniz,Joao Paulo Carvalho*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents an interpretable emotion recognition in conversations method that augments pre-trained language models with class-specific fuzzy fingerprints to reduce bias toward neutral emotions and provide insight into model decisions, achieving state-of-the-art performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Emotion recognition models based on pre-trained language models achieve strong performance but behave like black boxes, offering little interpretability. This lack of transparency is especially harmful on imbalanced datasets, where emotions are often misclassified as the majority neutral class. The authors aim to develop a method that both maintains high performance and reveals why particular emotion predictions are made, while mitigating overclassification into the neutral class.

Method: The authors combine standard pre-trained language models with Fuzzy Fingerprints (FFPs). For each emotion class, they compute class-specific prototypes by aggregating and pooling conversational context-dependent embeddings from the PLM across training instances, ranking the activations and converting them into fuzzy sets that capture typical latent activation patterns. At inference, an input utterance is passed through the PLM, its activation pattern is transformed into a fuzzy fingerprint, and this fingerprint is compared to the class prototypes via a fuzzy similarity function that aggregates the intersections of fuzzy sets. The class with the highest fuzzy similarity determines the prediction and provides a prototype-based explanation of the decision.

Result: Integrating Fuzzy Fingerprints into the ERC pipeline reduces the tendency to overpredict the majority neutral class, improving recognition of minority emotions. Quantitative experiments show performance at or near state-of-the-art levels, and human evaluations confirm that the FFP-based predictions and their associated explanations align well with human judgments about emotions in conversations.

Conclusion: The proposed PLM+FFP approach offers a more interpretable framework for emotion recognition in conversations, providing class-specific prototypes that elucidate the model’s reasoning while maintaining state-of-the-art performance. By mitigating overclassification into the neutral class and aligning predictions with human perception, the method narrows the gap between deep neural inference and human-understandable explanations in ERC tasks.

Abstract: In Emotion Recognition in Conversations (ERC), model decisions should align with nuanced human perception and ideally provide insights on the classification process. Standard encoder pre-trained language models (PLMs) are the state-of-the-art at these tasks but offer little insight into why a certain prediction is made. This is especially problematic in imbalanced datasets, where most utterances are labeled as neutral, making these models frequently misclassify minority emotions as the majority neutral class. To tackle this issue, we introduced a novel, interpretable approach to ERC by combining PLMs with Fuzzy Fingerprints (FFPs). FFP provide class-specific prototypes that reflect the characteristic class activation patterns in the PLM's latent space. They are derived by ranking and fuzzifying the activations of the pooled conversational context-dependent embeddings across training instances for each emotion. At inference time, each input utterance is similarly fuzzy fingerprinted and matched to the emotion prototypes using a fuzzy similarity function based on the aggregation of the intersection of the fuzzy sets that define each FFP. Experimental results show that FFP integration reduces overclassification into the neutral class and human evaluation further supports the adequacy of FFP predictions. Our proposed method thus bridges the gap between deep neural inference and human perception, performing at state-of-the-art level while simultaneously offering valuable insights into the classification procedure.

</details>

### [98] [FlexSQL: Flexible Exploration and Execution Make Better Text-to-SQL Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02815)
*Quang Hieu Pham,Yang He,Ping Nie,Canwen Xu,Davood Rafiei,Yuepeng Wang,Xi Ye,Jocelyn Qiaochu Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FlexSQL is a text-to-SQL agent that flexibly interacts with databases during reasoning, exploring schemas, data, and verification queries to correct errors and improve performance on complex analytical benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing text-to-SQL systems for large analytical databases rely on rigid, one-shot schema retrieval and limited post-hoc database access, making them brittle to early errors and poor at handling ambiguous queries and complex schemas. There is a need for an approach that can dynamically explore and verify against the database throughout the reasoning process.

Method: The authors design FlexSQL, a text-to-SQL agent that (1) can dynamically explore schema structure and inspect data values, (2) runs verification queries at arbitrary points in its reasoning, (3) generates multiple alternative execution plans to cover different query interpretations, (4) chooses between implementing each plan in SQL or Python depending on task requirements, and (5) employs a two-tiered repair mechanism that can fix both low-level code errors and higher-level plan errors via backtracking and revision.

Result: On the Spider2-Snow benchmark and using the gpt-oss-120b model, FlexSQL achieves a 65.4% score, outperforming strong open-source baselines that rely on larger, stronger models such as gpt-o3 and DeepSeek-R1. When incorporated as skills inside a general-purpose coding agent (Claude Code), FlexSQL yields more than 10% relative improvement on Spider2-Snow compared to the base agent. Ablation-style analysis indicates that both the flexible exploration of the database and the flexible choice of execution modality contribute meaningfully to performance gains.

Conclusion: Flexible database interaction—through dynamic schema and data exploration, multiple execution plans, mixed SQL/Python implementation, and multi-level repair—is an effective design principle for text-to-SQL agents operating over complex analytical databases. FlexSQL demonstrates that such flexibility can deliver strong performance even with smaller models and can be integrated as modular skills into broader coding agents to further improve text-to-SQL capabilities.

Abstract: Text-to-SQL over large analytical databases requires navigating complex schemas, resolving ambiguous queries, and grounding decisions in actual data. Most current systems follow a fixed pipeline where schema elements are retrieved once upfront and the database is only revisited for post-hoc repair, limiting recovery from early mistakes. We present FlexSQL, a text-to-SQL agent whose core design principle is flexible database interaction: the agent can explore schema structure, inspect data values, and run verification queries at any point during reasoning. FlexSQL generates diverse execution plans to cover multiple query interpretations, implements each plan in either SQL or Python depending on the task, and uses a two-tiered repair mechanism that can backtrack from code-level errors to plan-level revisions. On Spider2-Snow, using gpt-oss-120b, FlexSQL achieves a 65.4\% score, outperforming strong open-source baselines that use stronger, larger models such as gpt-o3 and DeepSeek-R1. When integrated into a general-purpose coding agent (as skills in Claude Code), our approach yields over 10\% relative improvement on Spider2-Snow. Further analysis shows that flexible exploration and flexible execution jointly contribute to the effectiveness of our approach, highlighting flexibility as a key design principle. Our code is available at: https://github.com/StringNLPLAB/FlexSQL

</details>

### [100] [Understanding Emergent Misalignment via Feature Superposition Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00842)
*Gouki Minegishi,Hiroki Furuta,Takeshi Kojima,Yusuke Iwasawa,Yutaka Matsuo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explains emergent misalignment in LLMs using geometry of feature superposition and proposes a geometry-aware data filtering method to reduce misalignment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Emergent misalignment—where harmless-seeming, narrow-task fine-tuning causes new harmful behaviors—is empirically observed but poorly understood. The authors want a principled explanation of why this happens and a practical way to mitigate it.

Method: They propose a geometric explanation based on feature superposition, deriving at the gradient level how fine-tuning a target feature also increases similar, nearby harmful features in representation space. Using sparse autoencoders on several LLMs, they extract features linked to misalignment-inducing data and harmful behaviors, compare geometric proximity between feature sets, test across domains, and design a geometry-aware filtering scheme that removes training samples closest to toxic features.

Result: They find that features associated with misalignment-inducing data lie geometrically closer to harmful-behavior features than features from non-inducing data, robustly across multiple models and domains. Their geometry-aware filtering of fine-tuning data, based on distance to toxic features, cuts emergent misalignment by 34.5%, outperforms random data removal, and competes with or slightly trails LLM-as-a-judge-based filtering.

Conclusion: Emergent misalignment arises naturally from feature superposition: overlapping internal representations cause fine-tuning for benign targets to co-amplify similar harmful features. Understanding this geometry enables a practical mitigation strategy via geometry-aware data filtering, offering both conceptual insight and an actionable tool for safer fine-tuning.

Abstract: Emergent misalignment, where fine-tuning on narrow, non-harmful tasks induces harmful behaviors, poses a key challenge for AI safety in LLMs. Despite growing empirical evidence, its underlying mechanism remains unclear. To uncover the reason behind this phenomenon, we propose a geometric account based on the geometry of feature superposition. Because features are encoded in overlapping representations, fine-tuning that amplifies a target feature also unintentionally strengthens nearby harmful features in accordance with their similarity. We give a simple gradient-level derivation of this effect and empirically test it in multiple LLMs (Gemma-2 2B/9B/27B, LLaMA-3.1 8B, GPT-OSS 20B). Using sparse autoencoders (SAEs), we identify features tied to misalignment-inducing data and to harmful behaviors, and show that they are geometrically closer to each other than features derived from non-inducing data. This trend generalizes across domains (e.g., health, career, legal advice). Finally, we show that a geometry-aware approach, filtering training samples closest to toxic features, reduces misalignment by 34.5%, substantially outperforming random removal and achieving comparable or slightly lower misalignment than LLM-as-a-judge-based filtering. Our study links emergent misalignment to feature superposition, providing a basis for understanding and mitigating this phenomenon.

</details>

Abstract: Clinical diagnosis requires answers that are accurate, verifiable, and explicitly grounded in official guidelines. Existing retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) systems treat all evidence equally, producing noisy context and generic answers misaligned with clinical practice. We present ClinicBot, an AI system that translates guideline recommendations into trustworthy clinical support through three key advances: (1) structured extraction of clinical guidelines into semantic units (recommendations, tables, definitions, narrative) with explicit provenance, (2) evidence prioritization that ranks content by clinical significance and guideline structure rather than textual similarity, and (3) a web-based interface that presents concise, actionable answers with verifiable evidence. This interoperability enhancement enables coordinated, distributed collaboration across automated systems and human-operated workflows, bridging multiple research centers. Using this approach, we iteratively explore the trade-off between formation time and EOL performance and identify candidate solutions approximating the Pareto front. The resulting workflow demonstrates the capability of interoperable infrastructures to facilitate data-driven optimization in battery research, and establishes a transferable framework applicable to diverse materials science and engineering optimization tasks.

### [103] [Effect-Transparent Governance for AI Workflow Architectures: Semantic Preservation, Expressive Minimality, and Decidability Boundaries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01030)
*Alan L. McCann*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper formally proves that you can impose strong structural governance on AI workflows without limiting what they can compute, by mediating all side-effectful actions through a governance operator.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a concern that adding governance or safety constraints to AI systems might reduce their computational power or expressivity. The paper aims to provide a precise, machine-checked formal foundation showing that structural governance can control side effects (like external calls or memory access) without weakening the underlying computational expressivity of the system.

Method: The authors use Interaction Trees in Rocq 8.19 (Coq) to formalize AI workflow architectures. They introduce a governance operator G that interposes on all effectful operations: memory access, external calls, and oracle/LLM queries. They build a large, fully-checked development (no admitted lemmas, 36 modules, ~12k lines, 454 theorems) to prove seven key properties about this governed architecture, covering Turing completeness, expressivity relative to oracles, decidability boundaries for governance predicates, goal preservation, minimality of primitive capabilities, subsumption of content-level filtering, and semantic transparency of governed executions.

Result: They formally prove seven main properties: (P1) the governed system remains Turing-complete; (P2) oracle-based expressivity is preserved under governance; (P3) governance predicates can remain total and Boolean-closed while still being unable to decide non-trivial semantic properties (so governance doesn’t trivially solve undecidable problems); (P4) goals of permitted executions are preserved under governance; (P5) the set of primitive capabilities (compute, memory, reasoning, external call, observability) is minimally sufficient for expressivity under governance; (P6) structural governance is strictly more powerful than pure content-level filtering; and (P7) when governance allows an execution, the governed and ungoverned semantics are observationally equivalent except for governance-specific events.

Conclusion: Governance can be treated as an orthogonal dimension to computational expressivity: by using a structural governance operator that mediates side effects, one can impose robust effect-level safety and control without degrading the internal computational power of the AI system. This provides a principled, machine-checked foundation for designing governed AI workflows that are both expressive and safely constrained at their effect boundaries.

Abstract: We present a machine-checked formalization of structurally governed AI workflow architectures and prove that effect-level governance can be imposed without reducing internal computational expressivity. Using Interaction Trees in Rocq 8.19, we define a governance operator G that mediates all effectful directives, including memory access, external calls, and oracle (LLM) queries. Our development compiles with 0 admitted lemmas and consists of 36 modules, ~12,000 lines of Rocq, and 454 theorems. We establishseven properties: (P1) governed Turing completeness, (P2) governed oracle expressivity, (P3) a decidability boundary in which governance predicates are total and closed under Boolean composition while semantic program properties remain non-trivial and undecidable by governance, (P4) goal preservation for permitted executions, (P5) expressive minimality of primitive capabilities (compute, memory, reasoning, external call, observability), (P6) subsumption asymmetry showing structural governance strictly subsumes content-level filtering, and (P7) semantic transparency: on all executions where governance permits, the governed interpretation is observationally equivalent (modulo governance-only events) to the ungoverned interpretation. Together, these results show that governance and computational expressivity are orthogonal dimensions: governance constrains the effect boundary of programs while remaining semantically transparent to internal computation.

</details>

*Alan L. McCann*

TL;DR: They build a formal, mechanized algebraic framework that precisely characterizes when and how program execution is governed, and prove that governance exactly matches what can be expressed in their primitive constructs.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To have a mathematically precise, compositional, and mechanized notion of governance in program execution that can guarantee that all composed programs respect specified governance policies, while still retaining expressiveness like Turing completeness.

Method: They define a GovernanceAlgebra with three axioms (safety, transparency, properness) over interaction trees using parameterized coinduction, show it forms a symmetric monoidal category, and design an algebraic effect system that restricts handlers to governance-preserving ones. They introduce capability-indexed composition and prove dual guarantees about capability bounds and governance safety. They further prove a coterminous boundary theorem showing equivalence between governed programs and those expressible via four primitive morphism constructors. The whole framework is mechanized in Rocq/Coq, extracted to OCaml, and validated with extensive property-based testing against a runtime interpreter.

Result: A fully mechanized, axiomatized governance algebra whose instances automatically satisfy strong properties like compositional closure, convergence, and goal preservation, together with a verified effect and capability system ensuring all safely composed programs remain governed and capability-bounded. They preserve Turing completeness within the governed fragment while excluding unmediated I/O, and empirically validate the implementation with 70k+ random tests showing equivalence between specification and interpreter.

Conclusion: Their algebraic semantics delivers an exact match between what can be expressed and what is governed, demonstrating that strong governance guarantees can coexist with high expressiveness. The parametric governance algebra can be instantiated in different systems to inherit all proven properties, and the mechanized development plus testing show the theory is both sound and practically implementable.

Abstract: We present an algebraic semantics for governed execution in which governance is axiomatized, compositional, and coterminous with expressibility. The framework, mechanized in 32 Rocq modules (~12,000 lines, 454 theorems, 0 admitted), is built on interaction trees and parameterized coinduction. A three-axiom GovernanceAlgebra record (safety, transparency, properness) induces a symmetric monoidal category with verified pentagon, triangle, and hexagon coherence, where every tensor composition preserves governance. An algebraic effect system constrains the handler algebra so that only governance-preserving handlers can be constructed in the safe fragment; programs in the empty capability set provably emit only observability directives. Capability-indexed composition bundles programs with machine-checked capability bounds, and a dual guarantee theorem establishes that within_caps and gov_safe hold simultaneously under all composition operators. The capstone result is the coterminous boundary: within our formal model, every program expressible via the four primitive morphism constructors is governed under interpretation, and every governed program is the image of such a program. Turing completeness is preserved inside governance; unmediated I/O is excluded from the governed fragment. Governance denial is modeled as safe coinductive divergence. The governance algebra is parametric: any system instantiating the three axioms inherits all derived properties, including convergence, compositional closure, and goal preservation. Extracted OCaml runs as a NIF in the BEAM runtime, with property-based testing (70,000+ random inputs, zero disagreements) confirming behavioral equivalence between the specification and the runtime interpreter.

### [107] [Towards Multi-Agent Autonomous Reasoning in Hydrodynamics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01102)
*Jinpai Zhao,Albert Cerrone,Joannes Westerink,Clint Dawson*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Single large language model agents currently handle entire scientific workflows in a single context window, but as tool specs and observational logs grow, the available context for each decision shrinks, reducing reliability. There is a need for an architecture that scales better with complex tool use, maintains accuracy, and supports auditability in scientific domains like hydrodynamics.

Method: The authors design a multi-agent system coordinated via a Layer Execution Graph (LEG). A planner agent builds query-specific execution topologies from natural-language routing heuristics rather than fixed control logic. Specialized agents, each with strict tool allowlists and domain-specific roles, execute tasks in parallel. Consolidator agents merge parallel outputs into concise layer briefs, and a reporter agent synthesizes the final answer. The runtime records detailed provenance for all tool calls. Claude Sonnet 4.6 is used as the backbone LLM for all agents. The system is evaluated on 37 hydrodynamics queries spanning six complexity levels, with benchmarks, ablations, stress tests, concurrency variations, and simulated data-source failures.

Result: On the 37-query hydrodynamics benchmark, the system reaches 93.6% factual precision with a 100% pass rate. Accuracy stays above 90% from single-threaded execution up to five parallel tracks. Under simulated failures of individual data sources, the system maintains meaningful partial answers and degrades gracefully rather than failing catastrophically. These experiments indicate improved reliability and robustness compared to single-agent, monolithic LLM workflows, particularly in the face of growing context and tool complexity.

Conclusion: Planner-guided, graph-structured multi-agent orchestration using a Layer Execution Graph can mitigate context-saturation problems inherent in single-agent LLM scientific workflows. By decomposing tasks across specialized agents, enforcing strict tool scopes, and fusing their outputs through consolidators and a reporter, the system sustains high factual precision, robustness to concurrency, and graceful degradation under data loss, suggesting a promising direction for scalable, auditable LLM-based scientific computing in hydrodynamics and potentially other domains.

Abstract: Single-agent systems (SAS) have become the default pattern for LLM-driven scientific workflows, but routing planning, tool use, and synthesis through a single context window comes with a well-known cost: as tool specifications and observational traces accumulate, the effective context available for each decision shrinks, and end-to-end reliability suffers. We present a multi-agent system (MAS) prototype for hydrodynamics in which specialized agents are coordinated through a Layer Execution Graph (LEG). A planner agent constructs query-specific execution topologies from natural-language routing heuristics that capture domain knowledge without hard-coding it as rigid control logic; specialist agents operate under strict tool allowlists and occupy complementary data-class roles. Between layers, consolidator agents fuse parallel outputs into concise briefs, and a reporter agent synthesizes the final response, while the runtime logs provenance for every tool invocation to support auditability. All benchmarks, ablations, and stress tests use Claude Sonnet~4.6 as the backbone model for both specialist and general-purpose agents. Evaluated on 37 queries spanning six complexity categories, the prototype achieves 93.6% factual precision with a 100% pass rate. Accuracy remains above 90% across runs from single-threaded to five independent parallel tracks, and under simulated loss of individual data sources the system degrades gracefully, still returning substantive partial answers. Together, these results suggest that planner-guided, graph-structured multi-agent orchestration can meaningfully alleviate the context-saturation bottlenecks that constrain monolithic single-agent architectures.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Zarankiewicz numbers are fundamental in extremal graph theory but are notoriously hard to determine exactly; many cases remain open or only have loose bounds. There is growing interest in using machine learning—especially LLMs—to assist mathematical discovery, but it is unclear whether such methods can genuinely advance cutting-edge combinatorics in a cost-effective and reproducible way. The paper aims to both push the frontiers of known Zarankiewicz numbers and to test whether an LLM-driven evolutionary approach can systematically generate strong extremal graph constructions.

Method: The authors introduce OpenEvolve, an open-source evolutionary algorithm that treats algorithmic generators of bipartite graphs as individuals in a population. An LLM is used within the evolutionary loop to propose and refine these generation algorithms, guided by a reward signal based on the size of the resulting $K_{s,t}$-free graphs relative to known bounds. The system iteratively mutates and selects better-performing algorithms, evaluates their constructions, and records extremal or near-extremal graphs. Implementation details and computational budgets are specified to make the pipeline reproducible and to quantify cost.

Result: They obtain exact values for three previously unresolved Zarankiewicz numbers: $Z(11,21,3,3)=116$, $Z(11,22,3,3)=121$, and $Z(12,22,3,3)=132$. They also provide new lower bounds for 41 additional Zarankiewicz numbers, several within one edge of the best known upper bounds, and exactly match four previously closed cases via their constructions. The paper includes the explicit extremal graph constructions and the corresponding generation algorithms identified by OpenEvolve. The total computational cost is under $30 per Zarankiewicz parameter set searched.

Conclusion: LLM-guided evolutionary search, as implemented in OpenEvolve, can produce state-of-the-art results in extremal combinatorics at modest cost. The method not only yields new exact Zarankiewicz numbers and near-optimal bounds but also produces reusable generation algorithms, making the discoveries reproducible and accessible. This suggests that similar LLM-based evolutionary frameworks could be a powerful general tool for advancing research in other areas of combinatorics and mathematical construction problems.

Abstract: The Zarankiewicz number $\textbf{Z}(m, n, s, t)$ is the maximum number of edges in a bipartite graph $G_{m, n}$ such that there is no complete $K_{s, t}$ bipartite subgraph. We determine for the first time the exact values of three Zarankiewicz numbers: $\textbf{Z}(11, 21, 3, 3)=116$, $\textbf{Z}(11, 22, 3, 3)=121$, and $\textbf{Z}(12, 22, 3, 3)=132$. We further establish lower bounds for 41 more Zarankiewicz numbers, including several that are within one edge of the best known upper bound, and we match the established value in four more closed cases. Our results are obtained using OpenEvolve, an open-source evolutionary algorithm based on Large Language Models (LLMs) that iteratively improves algorithms for generating mathematical constructions by optimizing a reward signal which we tailored for this specific problem. These findings provide new extremal graph constructions and demonstrate the potential of LLM-guided evolutionary search to contribute to mathematical research. In addition to presenting the resulting constructions, we report the generation algorithms produced, describe the relevant implementation details, and provide our computational costs. Our costs are remarkably low, at less than \$30 for each Zarankiewicz parameter combination, showing that LLM-guided evolutionary search can be an inexpensive, reproducible, and accessible tool for discovering new combinatorial constructions.

### [109] [PERSA: Reinforcement Learning for Professor-Style Personalized Feedback with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01123)
*Ravi Ranjan,Utkarsh Grover,Xiaomin Lin,Agoritsa Polyzou*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs can generate automated feedback, it is difficult to match an individual instructor’s tone and structure without degrading the model’s underlying correctness. Existing alignment methods may cause global parameter drift and hurt core capabilities when aggressively fine-tuned for style. The paper aims to provide a way to personalize educational feedback (tone, phrasing, structure) to specific professors without losing the accuracy of programming feedback.

Method: The authors introduce PERSA, a reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) pipeline for style adaptation. It first performs supervised fine-tuning on demonstrations of professor-style feedback, then trains a reward model from pairwise human preferences over different feedback samples. Using this reward model, they apply Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) to optimize the LLM’s outputs. Crucially, they use parameter-efficient fine-tuning constrained to style-bearing parts of the model (top transformer blocks and their feed-forward projections) to avoid broad changes to the model’s knowledge and reasoning abilities. PERSA is evaluated on multiple programming feedback benchmarks using metrics for style alignment and correctness.

Result: On three code-feedback benchmarks (APPS, PyFiXV, CodeReviewQA) and with two backbones (Llama-3 and Gemma-2), PERSA achieves strong transfer into a target professor’s style while maintaining or improving correctness. For instance, on APPS with Llama-3, PERSA increases the Style Alignment Score (SAC) from 34.8% to 96.2% and maintains Correctness Accuracy (CA) up to 100% on both Llama-3 and Gemma-2, outperforming baseline and alternative adaptation approaches.

Conclusion: PERSA demonstrates that carefully constrained RLHF can effectively personalize LLM feedback to match a specific instructor’s tone and structure without sacrificing diagnostic accuracy in programming feedback. By limiting updates to style-relevant parameters, it minimizes global drift and offers a practical, scalable method to deploy LLMs that “sound like” particular educators while still “knowing” the correct content, supporting more personalized and consistent automated educational feedback.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can provide automated feedback in educational settings, but aligning an LLMs style with a specific instructors tone while maintaining diagnostic correctness remains challenging. We ask how can we update an LLM for automated feedback generation to align with a target instructors style without sacrificing core knowledge? We study how Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) can adapt a transformer-based LLM to generate programming feedback that matches a professors grading voice. We introduce PERSA, an RLHF pipeline that combines supervised fine-tuning on professor demonstrations, reward modeling from pairwise preferences, and Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO), while deliberately constraining learning to style-bearing components. Motivated by analyses of transformer internals, PERSA applies parameter efficient fine-tuning. It updates only the top transformer blocks and their feed-forward projections, minimizing global parameter drift while increasing stylistic controllability. We evaluate our proposed approach on three code-feedback benchmarks (APPS, PyFiXV, and CodeReviewQA) using complementary metrics for style alignment and fidelity. Across both Llama-3 and Gemma-2 backbones, PERSA delivers the strongest professor-style transfer while retaining correctness, for example on APPS, it boosts Style Alignment Score (SAC) to 96.2% (from 34.8% for Base) with Correctness Accuracy (CA) up to 100% on Llama-3, and Gemma-2. Overall, PERSA offers a practical route to personalized educational feedback by aligning both what it says (content correctness) and, crucially, how it says it (instructor-like tone and structure).

### [110] [Iterative Finetuning is Mostly Idempotent](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01130)
*Zephaniah Roe,Jack Sanderson,Dang Nguyen,Julian Huang,Todd Nief,Aryan Shrivastava,Chenhao Tan,Ari Holtzman*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether behavioral traits in language models, like sycophancy or misalignment, grow stronger when models are repeatedly trained on their own outputs. It finds that such amplification is generally rare and fragile, mainly appearing under continual preference-based post-training, and often reduces model coherence when it occurs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As language models are iteratively trained and updated, they may increasingly rely on data generated by previous generations of models rather than humans. If those earlier models have undesirable tendencies (e.g., sycophancy, misalignment, strong personas), there is a safety concern that these traits could be amplified over generations, potentially leading to more harmful or unreliable systems. The paper aims to empirically test this risk and understand in which training setups such amplification is likely or unlikely to happen.

Method: The authors create a sequence of models where each generation is finetuned on data produced by the previous generation. They seed the initial model with a specific persona or belief (i.e., a behavioral trait) and then explore three training regimes: (1) supervised finetuning (SFT) on instruct models using synthetic instruction-following data from the predecessor; (2) synthetic document finetuning (SDF) on base models using generated documents; and (3) direct preference optimization (DPO), where each model is trained to prefer its own outputs over alternatives. For each regime and across multiple finetuning cycles, they measure whether and how much the seeded traits are amplified, remain stable, or decay, and they also track model coherence/quality.

Result: In the SFT and SDF regimes, the seeded traits usually either decay or stay roughly constant across generations, and additional finetuning cycles tend not to change the trait strength. When trait amplification does occur in these regimes, it is rare and tends to coincide with a drop in coherence or quality of the outputs. In contrast, under DPO, consistent trait amplification is observed when the same model is continually post-trained with a preference for its own outputs. However, this amplification effect disappears if models are reinitialized at each cycle rather than continually updated from the same weights. The authors also find that in non-RL-style finetuning settings, trait amplification is extremely sensitive to the amount of training data and thus unlikely to occur by accident.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that strong trait amplification across model generations is not an inevitable outcome of self-generated training data. For SFT and SDF, amplification is uncommon and fragile, with any amplification often undermining model coherence, which acts as a natural brake. The main risk arises from continual post-training with preference-based methods like DPO that favor a model’s own outputs, but this can be mitigated by limiting or restructuring that stage (e.g., via reinitialization or careful data design). Overall, the work suggests that while self-training can pose some risk of amplifying undesirable behaviors, this risk is controllable and less severe than might be feared in standard non-RL finetuning pipelines.

Abstract: If a model has some behavioral tendency, such as sycophancy or misalignment, and it is trained on its own outputs, will the tendency be amplified in the next generation of models? We study this question by training a series of models where each model is finetuned on data generated by its predecessor, and the initial model is seeded with some persona or belief. We test three settings: supervised finetuning (SFT) on instruct models, synthetic document finetuning (SDF) on base models, and direct preference optimization (DPO). In the SFT and SDF settings, traits mostly decay or remain constant so that further finetuning cycles do nothing. In rare cases when amplification occurs, it generally comes at the cost of coherence. In the DPO setting, trait amplification can reliably occur when a model is continually trained with a preference for its own outputs, but vanishes when models are reinitialized at each cycle. Overall, our results suggest that amplification most likely comes from continual post-training, and limiting this stage may be an effective defense. For non-RL finetuning, trait amplification is rare and very sensitive to data quantity, making it significantly less likely to occur accidentally. Finally, the amplification-coherence tradeoff serves as a natural deterrent against trait amplification.

</details>


### [111] [To Use AI as Dice of Possibilities with Timing Computation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01134)
*Jia Li,Vipin Kumar,Rui Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new, verb-centered AI modeling paradigm that explicitly represents time and possibilities, and shows it can discover patient trajectories and infer counterfactual timings from breast cancer EHR data without prior domain knowledge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This limits their ability to represent the open-ended nature of the future and to model how events unfold over time. The authors aim to overcome this limitation by creating a framework that more faithfully captures the dynamic, temporal grammar of human thought and reasoning, especially in domains like medicine where patient trajectories and timing of events are central.

Method: They introduce a verb-based modeling paradigm that formally defines two key concepts: timing computation (how to reason about when events occur or could occur) and possibility (how to represent alternative potential futures or counterfactual scenarios). This paradigm is then instantiated on longitudinal electronic health record (EHR) data of 3,276 breast cancer patients. Using purely data-driven methods, the framework learns patient trajectories and performs counterfactual reasoning about event timings, without using hand-crafted features or domain-specific rules.

Result: On the breast cancer longitudinal EHR dataset, the framework is able to (1) automatically discover clinically meaningful patient trajectories over time, and (2) perform counterfactual timing deduction, i.e., infer how the timing of clinical events might differ under alternative scenarios. These achievements are obtained without prior clinical knowledge encoded in the model and are claimed to be the first such results in the machine learning literature.

Conclusion: The study shows that a verb-based temporal modeling paradigm with formal notions of timing computation and possibility can overcome limitations of traditional noun-based AI. It can uncover significant temporal patterns and support counterfactual reasoning about event timing directly from raw longitudinal data. This suggests that such a framework can serve as a powerful tool for aligning AI with the dynamic, temporal structure of human thought and decision-making, particularly in complex clinical settings.

Abstract: The dominant noun-based modeling paradigm has fundamentally constrained AI development, precluding any adequate representation of the future as an open temporal dimension. This paper introduces a verb-based paradigm, together with precise definitions of \emph{timing computation} and \emph{possibility}, that enables AI to function as an effective instrument for realizing the grammar of our thought.
  Applied to longitudinal EHR data from 3,276 breast cancer patients, the framework empirically demonstrates: (1) automatic discovery of clinically significant patient trajectories, and (2) counterfactual timing deduction. Both results are purely data-driven, require no prior domain knowledge, and, to our knowledge, represent the first such demonstrations in the machine learning literature.

### [112] [A Low-Latency Fraud Detection Layer for Detecting Adversarial Interaction Patterns in LLM-Powered Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01143)
*Sheldon Yu,Yingcheng Sun,Hanqing Guo,Julian McAuley,Qianqian Tong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a lightweight, real-time fraud-detection-style layer that detects adversarial interaction patterns in LLM agents by modeling multi-turn trajectories rather than single prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents are increasingly autonomous and can be attacked via prompt injection, indirect content attacks, and multi-turn escalation. Existing defenses that focus on static prompts and guardrails miss risks that accumulate gradually across interaction sequences, creating a need for defenses that reason over full interaction trajectories with low latency.

Method: Introduce a behavioral detection layer that monitors agent interaction trajectories using structured runtime features. The system extracts 42 features capturing prompt characteristics, session dynamics, tool usage, execution context, and fraud-inspired signals, and trains lightweight models (e.g., XGBoost) to classify adversarial vs. benign sessions in real time. To train and test, the authors construct a synthetic dataset of 12,000 multi-turn agent interactions generated via parameterized templates that emulate realistic workflows and attacks.

Result: On the synthetic corpus, the XGBoost-based detector using 42 structured features attains substantial speed advantages—over 9× faster than LLM-based detectors—while effectively identifying adversarial interaction patterns. Ablation studies show which feature groups contribute most to detection performance.

Conclusion: Interaction-level behavioral detection is both feasible and efficient for LLM-powered agents. A dedicated, low-latency detection layer operating over multi-turn trajectories should be treated as a core deployment-time defense component, complementing prompt-level filters and rule-based guardrails to better mitigate adversarial agent interactions.

Abstract: Large Language Model (LLM)-powered agents demonstrate strong capabilities in autonomous task execution, tool use, and multi-step reasoning. However, their increasing autonomy also introduces a new attack surface: adversarial interactions can manipulate agent behavior through direct prompt injection, indirect content attacks, and multi-turn escalation strategies. Existing defense strategies focus on prompt-level filtering and rule-based guardrails, which are often insufficient when risk emerges gradually across interaction sequences. In this work, we propose a complementary defense mechanism: a low-latency fraud detection layer for detecting adversarial interaction patterns in LLM-powered agents. Instead of determining whether a single prompt is malicious, our approach models risk over interaction trajectories using structured runtime features derived from prompt characteristics, session dynamics, tool usage, execution context, and fraud-inspired signals. The detection layer can be implemented using lightweight models leading to low-latency real-time deployments. To evaluate the framework, we construct a synthetic corpus of 12,000 multi-turn agent interactions generated from parameterized templates that simulate realistic agentic workflows. Using 42 structured features and an XGBoost classifier, our detector achieves over 9 times faster than LLM-based detectors. Through the experiment and ablation studies, our work suggests that interaction-level behavioral detection should become a core component of deployment-time defense for LLM-powered agents.

</details>


### [113] [Position: Safety and Fairness in Agentic AI Depend on Interaction Topology, Not on Model Scale or Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01147)
*Tanav Singh Bajaj,Nikhil Singh,Karan Anand,Eishkaran Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that safety in multi-agent AI systems depends more on how agents interact than on individual model alignment, revealing topology-driven failure modes like ordering instability, information cascades, and functional collapse, which worsen with more capable models and are missed by model-centric evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are being used as interacting agents in high-stakes settings, and the community often assumes that aligning each individual model will lead to a safe overall system. The authors see this as a dangerous and incorrect assumption, motivating a re-examination of where safety actually comes from in multi-agent AI. They want to highlight that system-level interaction patterns, not just model properties, determine safety outcomes.

Method: This is a position paper that synthesizes empirical evidence from different model families and system configurations rather than running a single new experiment. The authors compare various multi-agent interaction topologies—such as sequential deliberation, parallel voting with a judge, and other aggregation schemes—to observe how information flows and decisions couple. They analyze how these topologies produce systematic pathologies (ordering instability, information cascades, functional collapse) across scales and models.

Result: They identify three robust, topology-driven pathologies in multi-agent LLM systems: (1) ordering instability, where the order in which agents act strongly influences final decisions; (2) information cascades, where early agent outputs unduly shape later agents and the final outcome regardless of correctness; and (3) functional collapse, where systems appear to satisfy fairness metrics but effectively stop differentiating real risks. They also find that increasing model capability tends to amplify these effects due to stronger consensus formation and easier early decisions. These issues are not detectable via standard, model-centric safety evaluations.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that safety in agentic AI cannot be guaranteed by aligning individual models alone. Multi-agent systems should be treated as dynamical systems whose safety depends critically on interaction topology. Consequently, safety research, evaluation, and regulation must explicitly target and stress-test interaction structures, requiring robustness across different architectural and topological configurations before deployment in high-stakes environments.

Abstract: As large language models are increasingly deployed as interacting agents in high-stakes decisions, the AI safety community assumes that safety properties of individual models will compose into safe multi-agent behavior. This position paper argues that this assumption is fundamentally mistaken. In agentic AI, safety is determined by interaction topology, not model weights. When agents deliberate sequentially or aggregate via parallel voting with a judge, the structure of information flow and decision coupling dominates outcomes. Evidence across model families and scales reveals three persistent topology-driven pathologies: ordering instability, where system behavior depends primarily on agent sequence; information cascades, where early judgments propagate regardless of correctness; and functional collapse, where systems satisfy fairness metrics while abandoning meaningful risk discrimination. Contrary to intuition, scaling to more capable models strengthens these effects by increasing consensus formation and reducing the challenge of initial decisions. These failure modes are invisible to model-centric evaluation and alignment procedures. We argue that agentic AI must be treated as a dynamical system rather than a collection of aligned components. Interaction topology must become a primary target of safety evaluation and regulation, with systems required to demonstrate robustness across architectural variations before deployment.
