### [114] [Arithmetic in the Wild: Llama uses Base-10 Addition to Reason About Cyclic Concepts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01148)
*Sheridan Feucht,Tal Haklay,Usha Bhalla,Daniel Wurgaft,Can Rager,Raphaël Sarfati,Jack Merullo,Thomas McGrath,Owen Lewis,Ekdeep Singh Lubana,Thomas Fel,Atticus Geiger*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that although Llama-3.1-8B encodes cyclic concepts (like months) in circular geometries, it does not perform modular arithmetic directly in that space. Instead, it reuses a generic base-10 addition mechanism implemented via task-agnostic Fourier features and a small set of reusable MLP neurons, then maps the result back into the cyclic concept space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether geometric structure in language model representations (e.g., circular embeddings for cyclic concepts) directly drives the corresponding computation (e.g., modular arithmetic), or whether the model instead uses more generic internal mechanisms that are independent of the specific representational geometry.

Method: Analyze Llama-3.1-8B on reasoning tasks involving cyclic concepts, such as computing months offset by a number. Probe and intervene on internal representations to determine whether the model performs modular addition in the cyclic embedding space or via another route. Identify and characterize Fourier-like features and the MLP neurons responsible for computing base-10 addition, examining their periodicities and reuse across tasks.

Result: Llama-3.1-8B represents cyclic concepts with circular geometry but does not compute modular arithmetic directly over that geometry. Instead, it first computes a base-10 sum of the inputs, using task-agnostic Fourier features whose periods align with base-10 arithmetic (e.g., 2, 5, 10), and only afterward maps this scalar sum back into the cyclic concept domain. A sparse subset of 28 MLP neurons in layer 18, reused across tasks, implements these Fourier-feature sums, with disjoint neuron clusters corresponding to different feature periods.

Conclusion: Internal representational geometry (e.g., circular structure of cyclic concepts) does not straightforwardly dictate the computation that language models perform. Llama-3.1-8B instead relies on a reusable, concept-agnostic base-10 addition circuit implemented via Fourier features and a small neuron subset, then maps results into concept-specific spaces. This illustrates how causal abstraction (the functional addition circuit) and feature geometry (cyclic embeddings) interact, providing a more nuanced mechanistic understanding of language model reasoning.

Abstract: Does structure in representations imply structure in computation? We study how Llama-3.1-8B reasons over cyclic concepts (e.g., "what month is six months after August?"). Even though Llama-3.1-8B's representations for these concepts are circularly structured, we find that instead of directly computing modular addition in the period of the cyclic concept (e.g., 12 for months), the model re-uses a generic addition mechanism across tasks that operates independently of concept-specific geometry. First, it computes the sum of its two inputs using base-10 addition (six + August=14). Then, it maps this sum back to cyclic concept space (14->February). We show that Llama-3.1-8B uses task-agnostic Fourier features to compute these sums--in fact, these features have periods that respect standard base-10 addition, e.g., 2, 5, and 10, rather than the cyclic concept period (e.g., 12 for months). Furthermore, we identify a sparse set of 28 MLP neurons re-used across all tasks (approximately 0.2% of the MLP at layer 18) that can be partitioned into disjoint clusters, each computing the sum for a Fourier feature with a different period. Our work highlights how an interplay between causal abstraction and feature geometry can deepen our mechanistic understanding of LMs.

</details>


### [115] [LLMs Should Not Yet Be Credited with Decision Explanation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01164)
*Wenshuo Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that large language models (LLMs) should not yet be credited with genuinely explaining human decisions, only with predicting them and generating plausible rationales, and proposes standards for when explanatory credit would be justified.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Recent research increasingly treats LLMs’ accurate prediction of human choices and generation of plausible rationales as if they were actually explaining why people decide as they do, which risks lowering or redefining the bar for explanatory progress in human decision science. The authors are concerned that this premature credit could distort scientific standards and the interpretation of LLM-based models of behavior.

Method: This is a conceptual / position paper. The authors: (1) distinguish three levels of claims about LLMs—decision prediction, rationale generation, and decision explanation—each with different evidential requirements; (2) analyze existing empirical work on LLM-based decision models to show that current evidence generally supports only prediction and rationale generation, not full explanation; (3) propose a “bridge standard” specifying what evidence is needed to justifiably claim decision explanation (clear explanatory targets, ruling out rationalizer alternatives, process- or intervention-sensitive validation, and scope limits); and (4) situate this proposal within broader debates about LLMs, cognitive modeling, and explanation standards.

Result: The paper argues that current LLM-based decision models do not meet the evidential burden required for genuine decision explanation; they mainly demonstrate strong predictive ability and the capacity to generate persuasive but potentially post hoc rationales. The authors’ bridge standard clarifies what additional kinds of evidence and methodological care would be needed to elevate claims from prediction and rationalization to explanation, and they show how this standard fits with and critiques existing perspectives in the literature.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that LLMs should only be credited with the strongest claim that their supporting evidence justifies, which is presently typically prediction and rationale generation, not full decision explanation. Their proposed credit-calibration principle and bridge standard are intended to prevent premature inflation of explanatory claims while still leveraging LLMs as useful tools for prediction, narrative framing, and hypothesis generation about human decision-making, thereby making them more reliable instruments for scientific progress on explaining behavior.

Abstract: This position paper argues that LLMs should not yet be credited with decision explanation. This matters because recent work increasingly treats accurate behavioral prediction, plausible rationales, and outcome-conditioned reasoning traces as evidence that LLMs explain why people decide as they do, risking a premature redefinition of what counts as explanatory progress in human decision modeling. We first distinguish three claims with different evidential burdens: decision prediction, rationale generation, and decision explanation. We then argue that the evidence most commonly offered for LLM-based decision accounts directly supports the first two claims, and sometimes explanatory hypothesis generation, but does not distinguish decision explanation from prediction-supportive rationalization. Next, we propose a bridge standard for decision-explanation credit: stronger claims should specify explanatory targets, discriminate against weaker rationalizer alternatives, use target-appropriate process- or intervention-sensitive validation, and bound their scope. We then situate this standard against competing views and related literatures, clarifying why it preserves the value of LLMs as predictors, narrators, and hypothesis generators while resisting premature explanatory credit. We conclude with a principle of credit calibration: LLMs should be credited for the strongest claim their evidence warrants, and no stronger; if adopted, this principle can help turn LLMs from persuasive narrators of decisions into more reliable instruments for discovering, testing, and communicating explanations of human behavior.

To mitigate this gap, we introduce GR-Ben, a process-level benchmark specifically designed for assessing PRM's performance across two primary reasoning domains (science and logic) and nine subdomains. We conduct extensive experiments on a diverse set of 22 models, encompassing both PRMs and LLMs, and derive two key findings: (1) In domains beyond mathematical reasoning, the error-detection ability of existing PRMs and LLMs is found to be markedly weaker by comparison.(2) In general, PRMs are less adept at identifying knowledge-based errors, whereas LLMs exhibit poorer performance in detecting computational errors.We hope GR-Ben can foster future researches on PRMs for general domains, thereby enhancing the reasoning capabilities of LLMs.

### [118] [Faithful Mobile GUI Agents with Guided Advantage Estimator](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01208)
*Haowen Hu,Pengzhou Cheng,Zheng Wu,Lingzhong Dong,Gongshen Liu,Zhuosheng Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Faithful-Agent, a framework to make vision-language GUI agents act based on on-screen evidence and instructions, significantly improving their faithfulness without hurting general performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing vision-language GUI agents often exploit memorized shortcuts or dataset biases and act unfaithfully, ignoring the actual screen or user instructions. This limits their reliability and safety, especially in settings where the UI changes or contains adversarial traps. The paper aims to ensure that agents ground their actions in real visual and textual evidence and maintain internal thought-action consistency.

Method: The authors propose Faithful-Agent, which has two stages. Stage I is a faithfulness-oriented supervised fine-tuning (SFT) that trains the agent to abstain or refuse actions when screen or instruction evidence is perturbed or insufficient, encouraging evidence checking. Stage II is reinforcement fine-tuning (RFT) built on GRPO, where they introduce the Guided Advantage Estimator (GuAE), an anchor-based, variance-adaptive advantage tempering technique to stabilize learning under sparse and low-variance GUI rewards. They also add a thought-action consistency reward to align internal reasoning with executed actions. Together, these optimize for faithfulness-first behavior.

Result: With the full Faithful-Agent Stage II setup (SFT + RFT with GuAE and thought-action consistency reward), the Trap Success Rate (SR)—which measures avoiding unfaithful trap behaviors—improves dramatically from 13.88% to 80.21% compared with the baseline. At the same time, the agent maintains strong general instruction-following capability on standard GUI tasks, showing that improving faithfulness does not sacrifice overall task performance.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that prioritizing faithfulness via abstention training and variance-aware reinforcement optimization leads to GUI agents that are far more grounded in actual screen evidence and internally consistent, without degrading their general capabilities. Faithful-Agent demonstrates that a faithfulness-first training paradigm can mitigate shortcut exploitation and make GUI agents substantially more reliable in real-world, potentially adversarial settings.

Abstract: Vision-language model based graphical user interface (GUI) agents have shown strong interaction capabilities. However, they often behave unfaithfully, relying on memorized shortcuts rather than grounding actions in displayed screen evidence or user instructions. To address this, we propose Faithful-Agent, a faithfulness-first framework that reformulates GUI interaction to prioritize evidence groundedness and internal consistency. Faithful-Agent employs a two-stage pipeline: (i) a faithfulness-oriented SFT stage to instill abstainment behaviors under evidence perturbations; (ii) an RFT stage that further amplifies faithfulness by introducing the guided advantage estimator (GuAE), an anchor-based and variance-adaptive advantage tempering mechanism built upon GRPO. GuAE prevents advantage collapse in low-variance rollout groups under sparse GUI rewards, and with a thought-action consistency reward, Faithful-Agent (Stage II) elevates the Trap SR from 13.88\% to 80.21\% relative to the baseline, while preserving robust general instruction-following performance.

### [119] [Agentic AI Systems Should Be Designed as Marginal Token Allocators](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01214)
*Siqi Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes viewing agentic AI systems as marginal token allocation economies instead of simple text generators, showing that multiple system layers share the same economic optimization condition and using this to explain common failure modes and outline a research agenda.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI systems and their components (routing, agents, serving stacks, training) are typically optimized in isolation and priced per token, which leads to global inefficiencies in how tokens are used. There is a need for a unified economic framework to reason about how agentic AI systems allocate tokens under costs, latency, and risk, and to better understand systemic failure modes and guide future research.

Method: The authors conceptually trace a single developer request through four economic layers: (1) a router choosing a model, (2) an agent deciding among planning, acting, verifying, or deferring, (3) a serving stack determining how to produce tokens, and (4) a training pipeline deciding which traces to learn from. They frame each layer as solving the same marginal optimization problem: choose token allocations so that marginal benefit equals marginal token cost plus latency cost plus risk cost, but with different index sets and prices. They then reason analytically about implications and failure modes of this shared structure.

Result: They show that treating all layers as instances of the same marginal token allocation problem reveals that local token-minimizing decisions can cause global token misallocation. From this unified perspective, they identify characteristic failure modes such as over-routing, over-delegation, under-verification, serving congestion, stale rollouts, and cache misuse, and they derive implications for how systems should be priced, routed, and trained.

Conclusion: Viewing agentic AI ecosystems as marginal token allocation economies yields a simple, unifying economic lens across routing, agent behavior, serving infrastructure, and training. This lens explains recurrent systemic inefficiencies and motivates a focused research agenda around token-aware system evaluation, explicit pricing of autonomy, congestion-sensitive serving policies, and risk-adjusted reinforcement learning budgets, rather than ad hoc per-token cost optimization.

Abstract: This position paper argues that agentic AI systems should be designed and evaluated as \emph{marginal token allocation economies} rather than as text generators priced by the unit. We follow a single request -- a developer asking a coding agent to fix a failing test -- through four economic layers that today are designed in isolation: a router that decides which model answers, an agent that decides whether to plan, act, verify, or defer, a serving stack that decides how to produce each token, and a training pipeline that decides whether the trace is worth learning from. We show that all four layers are solving the \emph{same} first-order condition -- marginal benefit equals marginal cost plus latency cost plus risk cost -- with different index sets and different prices. The framing is deliberately minimal: we do not propose a complete theory of AI economics. But adopting marginal token allocation as the shared accounting object explains why systems that locally minimize tokens globally misallocate them, predicts a small set of recurring failure modes (over-routing, over-delegation, under-verification, serving congestion, stale rollouts, cache misuse), and points to a concrete research agenda in token-aware evaluation, autonomy pricing, congestion-priced serving, and risk-adjusted RL budgeting.

Moreover, we equip Valley3 with agentic search capabilities to proactively invoke search tools and acquire task-relevant information for e-commerce deep research tasks. Method: They propose SAPO, which defines a step-wise Markov decision process over coherent reasoning segments. For each segment, they perform segment-level value estimation, advantage computation, and importance sampling, all aligned with reasoning boundaries instead of raw tokens. This reframes policy optimization around semantically meaningful units of reasoning.

Result: On multiple reasoning benchmarks, SAPO consistently outperforms token-level and sequence-level RL baselines, achieving higher accuracy, more stable training dynamics, and more consistent value estimates.

Conclusion: Aligning RL policy updates with the intrinsic, segment-wise structure of reasoning yields better performance and stability for LLMs in complex reasoning tasks. SAPO demonstrates the effectiveness of reasoning-segment-based optimization and suggests a direction for more semantically grounded RL for LLMs.

Abstract: Existing reinforcement learning approaches for Large Language Models typically perform policy optimization at the granularity of individual tokens or entire response sequences. However, such formulations often misalign with the natural step-wise structure of reasoning processes, leading to suboptimal credit assignment and unstable training in multi-modal reasoning tasks. To bridge this gap, we propose Segment-Aligned Policy Optimization (SAPO), a novel reinforcement learning paradigm that treats coherent reasoning steps, rather than tokens or full sequences as fundamental units of policy update. SAPO introduces a step-wise Markov decision process abstraction over reasoning segments, accompanied by segment-level value estimation, advantage computation, and importance sampling mechanisms that are semantically aligned with reasoning boundaries. Experiments on representative reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that SAPO consistently outperforms token-level and sequence-level policy optimization methods, achieving significant accuracy improvements while exhibiting better training stability and value estimation consistency. Our work underscores the importance of aligning reinforcement learning updates with the intrinsic structure of reasoning, paving the way for more efficient and semantically grounded policy optimization in complex reasoning tasks. Codes and models will be released to ensure full reproducibility.

Method: The authors design a "Truth or Tribe" simulation framework using a triadic interaction paradigm, where persona-based agents interact with both in-group and out-group peers while exposed to contradicting information, including misinformation and defeasible reasoning tasks. Result: Experiments show that persona agents strongly prefer and accept responses from identity-similar peers, even when these responses are incorrect, and do so at higher rates than for dissimilar peers. This in-group favoritism persists in settings without an objectively correct answer (defeasible reasoning) and becomes stronger as the tasks grow in cognitive complexity. The tested interventions each reduce the degree of in-group favoritism to varying extents.

Conclusion: In-group favoritism is a robust behavior in persona-based language model agents, particularly under misinformation and complex reasoning conditions, and it can undermine truthfulness and epistemic reliability. However, whether the in-group favoritism exists when persona agents are faced with contradicting information (e.g., misinformation), and how to mitigate the adverse effects of in-group favoritism biases in persona agents have been understudied. To address these problems, we propose a Truth or Tribe simulation framework to study the agent cooperation within the spread of contradicting information through a triadic interaction paradigm, and conduct controlled trials to evaluate the primary moderating factors. Extensive results showcase that persona agents display strong in-group favoritism, accepting incorrect answers from identity-similar peers at much higher rates than from dissimilar peers. In-group favoritism continues to emerge in defeasible reasoning contexts where no absolute truth exists, and it intensifies as cognitive complexity increases. Furthermore, three intervention strategies--Identity-Blind Instruction, Structured Counterfactual Reasoning, and Heterogeneous Perspective Ensemble--are proposed to mitigate the in-group favoritism.

### [128] [AI Safety as Control of Irreversibility: A Systems Framework for Decision-Energy and Sovereignty Boundaries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01415)
*Wesley Shu,Peng Wei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that modern AI systems can be deployed and scaled so rapidly and cheaply that traditional safety approaches focused only on local correctness or alignment are insufficient; instead, safety must be understood as controlling irreversible consequences in systems where decision-making power becomes highly concentrated.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional high-risk technologies had natural brakes—capital costs, physical constraints, and organizational inertia—that slowed both development and deployment, providing time and structure for safety measures. AI lacks many of these brakes because models can be copied and integrated at low marginal cost. This raises a new kind of risk: rapid concentration of decision-making power (“decision-energy”) in a few AI-centered nodes, which can cause large-scale, potentially irreversible harms even if each individual AI action is usually correct. The paper is motivated by the need to reconceptualize AI safety in light of this deployment dynamic.

Method: The paper introduces and formalizes the concept of decision-energy density: the rate-weighted capacity of an actor (or node) to generate, evaluate, choose, and execute impactful decisions. Using this concept, it models socio-technical systems as networks where decision-energy can flow and concentrate. It then defines three key “sovereignty boundaries”—irreversible decision authority, physical resource mobilization authority, and self-expansion authority—and analyzes how efficiency pressures, path dependence, scale feedback, and weak boundaries can cause decision-energy to concentrate in the most efficient node (often an AI system). This is used to derive a boundary stabilization theorem that characterizes conditions under which decision-energy remains safely distributed.

Result: The model shows that, due to efficiency and feedback dynamics, decision-making power tends to concentrate in the node that can process decisions most efficiently. If that node is an AI system with weakly enforced sovereignty boundaries, the system can effectively become a central control hub rather than a mere tool, while responsibility becomes diffused. This concentration increases the risk of irreversible, system-wide failures even when per-action error rates are low. The main technical result—the boundary stabilization theorem—demonstrates that safety can be achieved not by guaranteeing perfect AI correctness, but by designing institutional and technical structures that prevent any single high-efficiency node from unilaterally exercising irreversible decision, resource, or self-expansion authority.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI safety should be framed less as ensuring perfect alignment or correctness at the level of individual outputs and more as designing layered control, authorization, and reviewable limits on how and where AI systems can exercise power. Safety is fundamentally about controlling irreversibility in high decision-energy environments. This requires stabilizing sovereignty boundaries—through institutional design, security engineering, and governance mechanisms—so that AI remains an amplifier within a human-governed system rather than becoming a de facto sovereign decision center. The work connects AI alignment with organizational economics and institutional design, emphasizing that robust, multi-layered constraints on AI deployment and authority are central to preventing catastrophic system-level failures.

Abstract: Recent AI systems compress the distance between capability growth and capability deployment. Earlier high-risk technologies were slowed by capital intensity, physical bottlenecks, organizational inertia, and specialized supply chains. By contrast, AI capabilities can be copied, invoked, embedded in workflows, and scaled across institutions at low marginal cost. This paper argues that declining deployment friction changes the safety problem at its root. Safety is not only local output correctness or preference alignment, but the control of irreversibility under rising decision density.
  The paper formalizes this claim through decision-energy density: the rate-weighted capacity of a node to generate, evaluate, select, and execute consequential decisions. It then identifies three sovereignty boundaries that determine whether AI remains an amplifier within a human-governed system or becomes a de facto control center: irreversible decision authority, physical resource mobilization authority, and self-expansion authority. The model shows how efficiency pressure, path dependence, scale feedback, and weak boundary constraints concentrate decision-energy in the most efficient node. This concentration can diffuse responsibility and raise the probability of irreversible system-level loss even when local per-action error rates remain low.
  The main result is a boundary stabilization theorem. It shows that safety need not require proving that advanced systems are always correct. Instead, it requires institutional and technical designs that prevent irreversible power from being released by a single high-efficiency node. The paper reframes AI safety as layered control, authorization, and externally reviewable limits, linking alignment, security engineering, organizational economics, and institutional design.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Many real applications require generating time series at different granularities (e.g., rough planning vs. fine execution), possibly conditioned on coarser inputs, but there is no unified method that flexibly supports this spectrum of tasks.

Method: TimeTok introduces a hierarchical tokenization strategy that converts time series into an ordered sequence of discrete tokens representing progressively finer temporal granularities. An autoregressive model generates these tokens in blocks across granularity levels, and a decoder maps token blocks back into continuous time series. The same architecture handles both granularity-controllable generation and standard unconditional/conditional generation.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that TimeTok performs strongly on granularity-controllable time-series generation tasks and achieves state-of-the-art performance on standard time-series generation benchmarks. When trained as a shared tokenizer on multiple datasets with heterogeneous temporal resolutions, TimeTok exhibits strong transferability, consistently outperforming models trained separately on individual datasets.

Conclusion: TimeTok provides the first unified framework that spans the full generative spectrum for time series, from coarse to fine granularities, within a single model. Its hierarchical tokenization and block-wise autoregressive generation offer explicit and flexible control over temporal detail, making it a promising foundational component for a wide range of time-series generative models that must operate across diverse temporal resolutions.

Abstract: Time-series generative models often lack control over temporal granularity, forcing users to accept whatever granularity the model produces. To enable truly user-driven generation, we introduce TimeTok, a unified framework for Granularity-Controllable Time-Series Generation (GC-TSG), which generates time series at any target granularity from any coarser input (e.g., rough sketches) or from scratch. At the core of TimeTok is a hierarchical tokenization strategy that maps time series into an ordered sequence of tokens, from coarse to fine temporal granularity. Our autoregressive generation process operates across these granularity levels, producing token blocks that are decoded back into continuous time series. This design naturally enables GC-TSG - including standard generation - within a single framework, where controlling the number of token blocks provides explicit control over output detail. Experiments show that TimeTok excels at GC-TSG tasks while achieving state-of-the-art performance in standard generation. Furthermore, we showcase TimeTok's potential as a foundational tokenizer by training on multiple datasets with heterogeneous temporal granularities, verifying strong transferability that consistently outperforms models trained on individual datasets. To our knowledge, this is the first unified framework that covers the full generative spectrum for time series, offering a valuable foundation for models that benefit from diverse temporal granularities.

### [130] [Artificial Jagged Intelligence as Uneven Optimization Energy Allocation Capability Concentration, Redistribution, and Optimization Governance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01420)
*Wesley Shu,Peng Wei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal theory explaining why large models develop highly uneven (‘jagged’) capabilities, linking this to how limited optimization resources are distributed during training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large models often excel in some tasks while failing on others in unpredictable ways. Existing narratives appeal to a vague scalar “intelligence” or to ad-hoc explanations of emergent behavior. The authors want a principled, testable account of why and how these jagged capability profiles arise from the mechanics of gradient-based training under finite compute/resources.

Method: They model training as a finite-budget optimization process that allocates a limited amount of gradient update energy across different capability-relevant directions in parameter space. Within this framework they formally define capability gain, optimization energy share, and jaggedness. They then prove theorems relating persistent concentration of cumulative update energy to lower bounds on dispersion in capability gains, and derive a finite-budget tradeoff theorem that characterizes opportunity costs between capabilities. Finally, they analyze mechanisms (e.g., energy-variance regularization, auxiliary structural objectives) that can alter the distribution of optimization energy and thus the resulting capability profile.

Result: Theoretically, they show: (1) If gradient update energy remains concentrated in a subset of directions, the resulting capabilities must be correspondingly dispersed (jagged), rather than uniformly strong; (2) Under a finite optimization budget, improving one capability typically reduces achievable progress on others unless structural coupling allows shared gains; (3) Certain regularization schemes and auxiliary objectives can systematically redistribute optimization energy and thereby reduce or reshape jaggedness. They also derive predictions about training dynamics, such as correlations between early energy concentration and later uneven capabilities.

Conclusion: AJI is formalized as an inevitable consequence of finite, anisotropically allocated optimization resources rather than a mysterious artifact of “intelligence.” The framework provides testable predictions (e.g., early energy concentration predicting later jaggedness, scaling under narrow objectives not smoothing anisotropy, auxiliary objectives reviving weak capabilities) and offers levers—via architectural and objective design—to govern how capabilities emerge and how uneven they become.

Abstract: Artificial Jagged Intelligence (AJI) denotes a recurring pattern in which large learning systems exhibit strong local capabilities while remaining weak or brittle in other domains. This paper develops a formal theory of AJI as uneven allocation of optimization pressure. We model training as a finite-budget process that distributes gradient-driven update energy across capability-relevant directions in parameter space. In this model, jagged capability profiles arise from anisotropic objective structure, data geometry, and representational coupling rather than from a single scalar quantity called intelligence.
  The paper defines capability gain, optimization energy share, and jaggedness, then proves that persistent concentration of cumulative update energy yields lower bounds on dispersion in capability gains. A finite-budget tradeoff theorem shows why prioritizing one capability can impose opportunity costs on others unless positive coupling or shared structure offsets the cost. The analysis also studies redistribution mechanisms, including energy-variance regularization and auxiliary structural objectives, as interventions that reshape the optimization field.
  The resulting framework links uneven emergence, training architecture, and optimization governance. It predicts that early concentration of update energy should forecast later capability jaggedness; that scaling under a narrow objective need not eliminate anisotropy; and that explicitly funded auxiliary objectives can revive neglected capabilities. AJI is therefore not merely a descriptive label for uneven model behavior, but a testable theory of how finite optimization resources produce concentrated, delayed, and structurally uneven capability formation.

### [131] [SCALE-LoRA: Auditing Post-Retrieval LoRA Composition with Residual Merging and View Reliability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01429)
*Shuaipeng Zhou,Yu Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes SCALE, a framework to reliably reuse and compose LoRA adapters from an open pool for new tasks, focusing on mitigating merge interference and quantifying reliability through multi-view disagreement analysis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As many LoRA adapters accumulate from parameter-efficient fine-tuning, a key challenge becomes how to effectively and safely reuse an open pool of these adapters for new tasks with limited labeled data. Existing methods can retrieve and compose adapters, but they suffer from incompatibility between adapters' parameter updates and unreliable outputs after composition. There is therefore a need for a principled method to audit, compose, and assess the reliability of LoRA adapter reuse.

Method: The paper introduces SCALE, a two-part post-retrieval framework for open-pool LoRA reuse. First, it proposes Layer-Adaptive Sparse Residual Composition (LASRC), a 1.0×-cost merge path that preserves a linear anchor while adding block-wise residualized adapter update directions to mitigate merge interference. Second, it adds a higher-cost reliability-analysis layer that constructs multiple sparse composition views and interprets their disagreement as an uncertainty signal. This layer compares different selection heuristics—agreement-based, support-loss proxy, and an oracle headroom—explicitly accounting for path cost.

Result: On FLAN-T5-Large evaluated on BIG-Bench Hard with a pool of 97 LoRA adapters, LASRC improves performance directionally in a single-view setting under fixed retrieval, indicating more compatible and effective merges. The SCALE reliability-analysis component is reported as a query-label-free, 3.0×-cost diagnostic tool that provides insights into reliability rather than serving as a calibrated or throughput-matched selector. Additional validation on BBH-8 with three decoder-only backbones shows consistent qualitative trends, supporting the generality of the approach. Detailed metrics, audits, and cost analyses are provided in the experiments.

Conclusion: SCALE offers a structured way to reuse LoRA adapters from an open pool by addressing both merge interference and reliability assessment. LASRC yields more robust single-path compositions at similar computational cost, while the reliability-analysis layer uses multi-view disagreement as a practical uncertainty signal. Together, they move LoRA reuse from ad-hoc composition toward auditable, cost-aware, and more reliable adapter merging across different backbones and benchmarks.

Abstract: Libraries of Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) adapters are becoming a practical by-product of parameter-efficient adaptation. Prior work has shown that LoRA modules can be composed at the task level and dynamically selected at the instance level. However, open-pool LoRA reuse is not automatic: retrieving relevant adapters does not guarantee that their parameter updates are compatible, and composing adapters does not guarantee reliable outputs.
  We introduce the Sparse-Composition Agreement Layer (SCALE), a post-retrieval audit and composition framework for open-pool LoRA reuse. SCALE contains a deployable 1.0* merge path, Layer-Adaptive Sparse Residual Composition (LASRC), and a higher-cost reliability-analysis layer for multi-view disagreement. LASRC addresses merge interference by preserving a linear anchor while residualizing block-wise adapter update directions. The reliability layer treats disagreement among sparse composition views as an observable uncertainty signal and compares agreement, support-loss proxy selection, and oracle headroom under explicit path cost. In matched FLAN-T5-Large, BIG-Bench Hard (BBH), and 97-LoRA experiments, LASRC gives a directional single-view gain under fixed retrieval, while SCALE-support is reported as a query-label-free 3.0* reliability-analysis variant rather than as a calibrated or throughput-equivalent selector. ### [132] [Rethinking Explanations: Formalizing Contrast in Description Logics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01442)
*Yasir Mahmood,Arnab Sharma,Axel-Cyrille Ngonga Ngomo,Balram Tiwari*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces contrastive explanations for description logic knowledge bases, explaining why a fact P holds instead of an alternative Q, rather than only why P or why not Q individually.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing DL explanation formalisms either justify why an axiom is entailed or use abduction to explain missing axioms, but they ignore the user’s perspective: expectations, prior beliefs, and what alternative outcomes they had in mind. Users are often surprised because they expected a similar but different event Q, so they want to understand the difference between P and Q, not just each in isolation.

Method: The authors formalize contrastive questions of the form “Why P instead of Q?” in the setting of description logics. They focus on ABox assertions C(x) as facts, define possible foils as C(y) (same concept, different individual) or D(x) (same individual, different concept), and provide a logical definition of contrastive explanations that highlights the distinguishing factors between P and Q. They then study theoretical properties of these explanations within the DLs EL and ALC, implement their approach, and run experiments on knowledge bases of different sizes.

Result: They obtain formal foundations for contrastive questions and a concrete definition of contrastive explanations for DL ABox assertions, together with proven properties in EL and ALC. Their implementation successfully computes such explanations in practice and is evaluated empirically on multiple KBs, demonstrating feasibility and behavior across varying KB sizes.

Conclusion: Contrastive explanations offer a user-centered way to explain entailments in DL knowledge bases by directly addressing “why P rather than Q.” This better aligns with how users naturally ask questions and need to see the difference between expected and observed facts. The formalization in EL and ALC, along with an implementation and experiments, shows that such explanations are both theoretically well-founded and practically computable.

Abstract: There has been a growing interest in explaining entailments over description logic (DL) knowledge bases. The existing explanation formalisms focus on justifications to explain true axioms, and abductive reasoning to explain missing axioms in a knowledge base. However, these formalisms only point out the reasoning steps behind a (missing) entailment and lack a user-centered approach as they do not consider an inquirer's needs, level of understanding, or prior knowledge. We propose contrastive explanations, aiming at answering "why an axiom P (fact) is true instead of another axiom Q (foil)" over description logic knowledge bases. The motivation arises from the observation that when a user discovers that P has occurred, they are often surprised because they anticipated the occurrence of another similar event Q. Furthermore, individual explanations for "why P" and "why not Q" are unsatisfactory since a user expects to see the difference between P and Q. In this work, we first present formal foundations of contrasting questions and then define contrastive explanations within description logics. To this end, facts include ABox assertions of the form C(x) for a concept C and individual x. Possible foils for such facts are assertions C(y) (contrasting against an individual y), or D(x) (contrasting against a concept D). Additionally, we explore the properties of contrastive explanations in the DL EL and ALC. We also provide an implementation of our definition and an experimental evaluation on KBs of varying sizes.

</details>

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper proposes an SCM-grounded, reinforcement-learning-based framework (SCM-GRPO) for multi-hop fact verification that optimizes reasoning chain length to reduce hallucinations and improve accuracy over existing methods.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing methods, while improving transparency via Chain-of-Thought (CoT), lack explicit modeling of the causal dependencies between evidence and claims. In this work, we introduce a novel framework that grounds reasoning in a Structural Causal Model (SCM), treating verification as a constructive causal inference process. We empirically identify an "inverted U-shaped" correlation between reasoning chain length and accuracy, revealing that excessive structural complexity degrades performance. To address this, we propose a Rule-based Reinforcement Learning strategy using Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO). This approach dynamically optimizes the trade-off between structural depth and conciseness. ### [134] [MAP-Law: Coverage-Driven Retrieval Control for Multi-Turn Legal Consultation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01486)
*Qinchuan Cheng,Ruixuan Xie,Jiaqi Liu,Xiaoya Yuan,Yuxin Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MAP-Law is a coverage-driven retrieval-control framework for multi-turn legal consultation that dynamically decides when to stop retrieving, improving efficiency while maintaining strong legal element coverage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-turn legal consultation systems using retrieval-augmented generation typically rely on fixed retrieval depth or simple heuristics. This leads either to missing crucial legal support (under-retrieval) or gathering too much evidence (over-retrieval), which burdens context windows and dilutes answer quality. There is a need for a principled, interpretable way to control retrieval based on how well the legal issues and elements are already supported.

Method: The authors formulate legal consultation as a controlled retrieval process over a joint structured graph-like state that includes issue nodes, legal element nodes, and evidence nodes. After each retrieval round, the system computes three quantitative signals—Element Coverage (how many required legal elements are supported), Evidence Coverage (how well existing evidence supports those elements), and Marginal Gain (benefit of the latest retrieval). These metrics guide an LLM-based action selector (e.g., DeepSeek) to decide whether to continue retrieving, change retrieval direction, or stop and generate a final answer. Stopping thus becomes a learned, interpretable decision tied to legal argumentation needs rather than a fixed hyperparameter.

Result: On a custom dataset of 50 labor-law consultation cases across eight scenarios, MAP-Law using DeepSeek as the controller reaches an Element Coverage score of 0.860 with only 2.9 retrieval rounds and 5.8 evidence items per case on average. Relative to a baseline that always performs seven retrieval rounds, MAP-Law reduces evidence volume by over 80% and retrieval rounds by 58%, while maintaining strong coverage of relevant legal elements. Ablation studies show that coverage-based stopping, the joint graph state, and LLM-based action selection each contribute meaningfully to performance.

Conclusion: Coverage-aware retrieval control via MAP-Law allows legal consultation agents to retrieve just enough evidence, grounded in legal argument structure, instead of relying on fixed-depth retrieval. This yields more efficient use of context and more focused answers while preserving or improving coverage of key legal elements. The structured state representation and coverage metrics make the retrieval and stopping decisions interpretable and auditable, which is valuable for high-stakes domains like law.

Abstract: Legal consultation is a high-stakes, knowledge-intensive task that requires agents to identify relevant legal issues, retrieve authoritative support, and determine when evidence is sufficient for a recommendation. Although retrieval-augmented generation has improved grounding in legal question answering, many multi-turn legal agents still rely on fixed retrieval depth or coarse heuristic control. This often leads to either insufficient support for key legal elements or excessive retrieval that increases context burden and weakens answer focus.
  We propose MAP-Law, a coverage-driven framework for retrieval control in multi-turn legal consultation. MAP-Law models consultation as a controlled retrieval process over a joint structured state consisting of issue nodes, legal element nodes, and evidence nodes. After each retrieval round, the agent computes Element Coverage, Evidence Coverage, and Marginal Gain, and uses these signals to decide whether to continue retrieval, redirect the search, or generate the final response. In this way, MAP-Law turns stopping from a fixed hyperparameter into an interpretable and auditable decision aligned with legal argumentative structure.
  Experiments on a self-constructed dataset of 50 cases across eight labor-law scenarios show that MAP-Law with DeepSeek as the action selector achieves an Element Coverage of 0.860 using only 2.9 retrieval rounds and 5.8 evidence pieces on average. Compared with a fixed seven-round baseline, it reduces evidence volume by over 80% and retrieval rounds by 58%. Ablation results further confirm the independent contributions of coverage-driven stopping, joint graph representation, and LLM-based action selection.

MILD integrates a perception agent for joint in-cabin and out-of-cabin understanding with a lightweight strategy agent that generates compliant and explainable action suggestions. To ensure these strategies are strictly aligned with safety regulations and human values, we develop Evidence- and Constraint-weighted Policy Optimization (ECPO). ECPO leverages automatic validators to steer the agent toward behaviors that are not only accurate but also structurally complete, substantiated by evidence, and free from constraint violations. Furthermore, a retrieval-augmented generation module dynamically incorporates constraints from traffic regulations, speed recommendations, and driver preferences into the decision loop. ### [137] [Multi-Agent Reasoning Improves Compute Efficiency: Pareto-Optimal Test-Time Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01566)
*Florian Valentin Wunderlich,Lars Benedikt Kaesberg,Jan Philip Wahle,Terry Ruas,Bela Gipp*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper systematically analyzes various inference-time scaling strategies for language models, comparing their accuracy–compute tradeoffs and identifying Pareto-optimal methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While inference-time methods like self-consistency, self-refinement, and multi-agent debate can significantly improve language model reasoning without extra training, they are often evaluated by accuracy alone and ignore computational cost. Real-world deployments must balance performance with limited compute budgets, so there is a need for a unified, quantitative comparison of these strategies under realistic compute constraints.

Method: The authors study four inference-time scaling strategies—self-consistency, self-refinement, multi-agent debate, and mixture-of-agents—across two reasoning benchmarks (MMLU-Pro and Big-Bench Hard). They systematically vary parameters such as the number of parallel generations, the number of agents, and the number of debate rounds, and perform over 100 evaluations spanning 34 different configurations and multiple model sizes. They then compute Pareto fronts that jointly consider accuracy and computational cost, identifying which methods are optimal under different compute budgets.

Result: Inference scaling can yield up to a 7.1 percentage point accuracy improvement over standard chain-of-thought when using high compute budgets (up to 20x the base CoT budget) on MMLU-Pro. Under equal compute budgets, multi-agent methods (debate and mixture-of-agents) outperform self-consistency by 1.3 and 2.7 percentage points respectively. Self-consistency shows diminishing returns earlier, whereas multi-agent methods continue to yield gains on harder tasks. The analysis yields Pareto-optimal configurations of methods and hyperparameters for different compute regimes.

Conclusion: Inference-time scaling methods provide substantial accuracy gains, but their efficiency varies significantly. Multi-agent approaches, especially mixture-of-agents, tend to be more compute-efficient than self-consistency, particularly when the number of parallel generations is larger than the number of sequential aggregation steps. The paper offers a practical design guideline: use mixture-of-agents with more parallel than sequential operations to maximize accuracy under a given computational budget, and use Pareto analysis to select methods suitable for real-world, resource-constrained applications.

Abstract: Advances in inference methods have enabled language models to improve their predictions without additional training. These methods often prioritize raw performance over cost-effective compute usage. However, computational efficiency is key for real-world applications with resource constraints. We provide a systematic analysis of the inference scaling strategies self-consistency, self-refinement, multi-agent debate, and mixture-of-agents, to study their computational performance tradeoffs. We evaluate methods on two reasoning benchmarks (MMLU-Pro, BBH) and include extensive parameter configurations (e.g., scaling the number of parallel predictions, agents, and debate rounds) across different model sizes. Across 34 configurations and over 100 evaluations, we compute the Pareto-optimal front to select methods that achieve the best accuracy with the lowest computational budget. Notably, inference scaling improves accuracy by up to +7.1% points over chain-of-thought at the highest evaluated budgets (20x the CoT compute budget) on MMLU-Pro. With an equal computing budget, debate and mixture-of-agents outperform self-consistency by 1.3% and 2.7% points, respectively. While self-consistency saturates earlier, multi-agent gains persist, particularly on more complicated tasks. We identify a simple multi-agent design guideline: mixture-of-agents is most efficient when the number of parallel generations exceeds the number of sequential aggregations.

Second, we demonstrate empirically where standard metrics -- ROUGE, BERTScore, accuracy/AUC, and the agentic benchmarks above -- fail to detect each failure mode. Third, we propose PAEF (Production Agentic Evaluation Framework), a five-dimension evaluation framework with an open-source reference implementation, designed for continuous evaluation on production traffic rather than episodic benchmark runs. Our analysis shows that standard metrics fail to detect four of the seven failure modes entirely and detect three others only after a lag of multiple evaluation cycles.

This exposes key gaps, such as the difference between predictive sufficiency and control sufficiency, and between passive video prediction and counterfactual action modeling. The resulting matrix makes explicit which information different latent designs preserve, what they discard, and what capabilities they enable.

Conclusion: Effective world models are not those that encode the maximal amount of information about the environment, but those whose latent state is constructed to be sufficient for the specific downstream task (e.g., planning, control, counterfactual reasoning). Task-aligned sufficiency, rather than information density, should guide both the design and evaluation of world-model latent states.

We use the resulting matrix as a diagnostic for what a latent state preserves, discards, and enables.

Trust can break when routing changes the cost, quality, or accountability of a response without the user being able to tell what happened. "Which model answered?" is only part of the audit question. The runtime path matters.
  Adaptive AI systems should produce a runtime transparency artifact called the route receipt. A route receipt is a compact record of the route that served a request. It should capture enough material facts for people relying on the output to reconstruct important routing decisions without exposing proprietary internals or hidden reasoning.
  Route transparency should be part of model documentation. Model cards describe trained model artifacts, while route receipts describe the runtime conditions under which a particular answer was produced. The paper introduces the route-receipt concept, a minimal schema and redaction model, and a documentation-based survey of selected platforms showing that receipt fragments already exist without a portable per-answer record.

*Huiqian Lai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies whether large language models unfairly flag legitimate entertainment news as fake more often than hard news, finding genre-specific biases in some models and arguing for genre-aware evaluation of LLM-based news credibility tools.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used to judge news credibility, but standard evaluations mostly look at overall accuracy and ignore whether models treat different journalistic genres (like entertainment vs hard news) differently or unfairly. This gap matters because systematic over-flagging of certain genres could undermine legitimate journalism and skew automated fact-checking systems.

Method: Using the GossipCop subset of FakeNewsNet, the authors run a within-dataset comparison of zero-shot classification performance by four frontier LLMs (DeepSeek-V3.2, GPT-5.2, Claude Opus 4.6, Gemini 3 Flash) on legitimate entertainment versus hard news. They compute and compare false positive rates by genre, conduct a style-swap experiment to test whether genre effects are driven purely by stylistic register, and experiment with prompt-based mitigations (e.g., framing models as entertainment-news fact-checkers). They also perform qualitative coding of sampled false positives to identify recurring error patterns.

Result: Two LLMs (DeepSeek-V3.2 and GPT-5.2) exhibit substantial genre asymmetry, with false-positive rates on legitimate entertainment news exceeding those on legitimate hard news by about 10 and 9 percentage points, respectively, both statistically significant (p < .001). The other two models (Claude Opus 4.6 and Gemini 3 Flash) do not show comparable gaps. Style-swapping only modestly and inconsistently changes the asymmetry, and a special-purpose entertainment fact-checker prompt cuts DeepSeek-V3.2’s false positives roughly in half without hurting recall but has limited effect on GPT-5.2. Qualitative analysis highlights two main error patterns: treating claims about private lives as inherently unverifiable and implicitly discounting entertainment journalism as less legitimate.

Conclusion: LLM-based news credibility assessment can harbor structured, genre-specific biases that standard aggregate metrics obscure. Some models systematically over-flag legitimate entertainment news, and this asymmetry is not fully explained by surface style. While prompt engineering can mitigate such errors for some models, there is no one-size-fits-all fix. Evaluations of LLM credibility tools should therefore include genre-stratified false-positive analyses and attend to how models differentially recognize the epistemic legitimacy of various journalistic genres.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used for automated news credibility assessment, yet it remains unclear whether they apply even-handed standards across journalistic genres. We examine whether zero-shot LLMs are more likely to misclassify legitimate entertainment news as fake than legitimate hard news, using a within-dataset design on GossipCop from FakeNewsNet. Across four frontier models, we find a clear but model-specific genre asymmetry: DeepSeek-V3.2 and GPT-5.2 show false-positive-rate gaps of 10.1 and 8.8 percentage points, respectively (both $p < .001$), whereas Claude Opus 4.6 and Gemini 3 Flash show no comparable difference. A style-swap experiment yields only limited and inconsistent changes, suggesting that the asymmetry is not reducible to stylistic register alone. Prompt-based mitigation is likewise possible but not generic: framing the model as an entertainment-news fact-checker reduces false positives for DeepSeek-V3.2 by about 50\% without detectable recall loss, but offers little improvement for GPT-5.2. Exploratory qualitative coding further suggests two recurring error patterns in sampled false positives: treating private-life claims as inherently unverifiable and discounting entertainment journalism as an epistemically weaker genre. Taken together, these findings show that aggregate performance metrics can obscure structured false positives within legitimate journalism. We argue that LLM-based credibility assessment may not only evaluate truth claims but also differentially recognize the legitimacy of journalistic genres, and that evaluation should therefore include genre-stratified false-positive analysis alongside overall accuracy.

</details>


### [143] [NH-CROP: Robust Pricing for Governed Language Data Assets under Cost Uncertainty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01745)
*Xu Zheng,Feiyu Wu,Zhuocheng Wang,Yiming Dai,Hui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes NH-CROP, a robust online pricing framework for language data assets under uncertain privacy/access costs, deciding when to buy more cost information and how to price to maximize safe revenue.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language data is increasingly treated as a governed asset with privacy and access constraints that induce uncertain, possibly high, usage costs. Platforms must set prices for using such assets in NLP tasks without fully knowing their true costs and may optionally pay to better estimate these costs. Existing approaches do not adequately handle this online decision problem of pricing under cost uncertainty while deciding when additional information acquisition about costs is worthwhile.

Method: The authors formulate an online pricing problem where in each round a platform faces an NLP task, a candidate language data asset, and a coarse estimate of its access/privacy cost. The platform may pay to obtain a more accurate (“refined”) cost signal before setting a price and realizing net safe revenue. They propose NH-CROP, a clipped robust pricing framework with a no-harm gate for information acquisition. The framework learns policies that: (1) compare direct (naïve) pricing, risk-aware pricing, and verify-then-price strategies; (2) decide whether to buy refined cost information based on an estimated decision value, only doing so if this value exceeds that of the best strategy without verification; and (3) clip prices/decisions to remain robust. They evaluate NH-CROP and its variants on synthetic data, real-proxy datasets, and benchmarks grounded in downstream NLP utility, and use causal ablations and oracle diagnostics to analyze where the gains come from and how much value refined cost information provides locally.

Result: Across synthetic, real-proxy, and downstream-utility-grounded experiments, clipped NH-CROP variants either outperform or remain competitive with simpler baselines that only price (without information acquisition) or that are purely risk-aware. The analyses reveal that, in realistic proxy and utility-grounded settings, the main performance improvements are not primarily due to paying for verification: the best learned policies often choose not to verify costs. However, oracle and high-decision-value analyses indicate that when refined cost information is obtained, it can have significant local value for particular decisions, even if it is not globally the dominant factor.

Conclusion: For platforms managing governed language data with uncertain access or privacy costs, it is generally more beneficial to first calibrate robust, risk-aware pricing policies and to treat cost verification as an optional, decision-contingent step. Verification should be purchased only when its information is cheap and likely to materially affect the pricing decision (i.e., when estimated decision value is high). NH-CROP provides a principled way to implement this by gating information acquisition through a no-harm criterion and using clipped robust pricing, yielding strong or competitive performance while often avoiding unnecessary verification costs.

Abstract: Language data are increasingly acquired and governed as assets, yet platforms often price candidate resources before knowing their true privacy or access costs. We study online pricing for governed language data assets under cost uncertainty. At each round, a platform observes an NLP task, a candidate asset, and a coarse cost estimate, may pay for a refined cost signal, posts a price, and receives safe net revenue.
  We introduce \textsc{NH-CROP}, a clipped robust pricing framework with a no-harm information-acquisition gate. The method compares direct pricing, risk-aware pricing, and verify-then-price, and acquires information only when its estimated decision value exceeds the best no-verification alternative. Across synthetic, real-proxy, and downstream-utility-grounded benchmarks, clipped \textsc{NH-CROP} variants improve or remain competitive with price-only and risk-aware baselines. Causal ablations show that paid verification is not the main source of gains in real-proxy and utility-grounded settings: the strongest learned policies often choose not to verify. Oracle and high-decision-value diagnostics show that refined cost information can still have substantial local value. Overall, governed language-data platforms should calibrate pricing under uncertain access costs first and verify only when information is cheap and decision-actionable.

</details>


### [144] [Catching the Infection Before It Spreads: Foresight-Guided Defense in Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01758)
*Yue Ma,Ziyuan Yang,Yi Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces FLP, a training-free framework to defend large multimodal multi-agent systems from infectious jailbreaks by locally diagnosing and purifying compromised agents via foresight-guided multi-persona simulations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent systems based on large multimodal models are powerful but vulnerable to infectious jailbreak attacks, where a single compromised agent can quickly contaminate others. Existing defenses use a global, more contagious cure factor that agents are biased to retrieve, but this causes homogenized responses and only masks, rather than truly removes, the underlying infection. There is a mismatch between global, shared defenses and the localized interaction patterns where jailbreaks arise. The paper aims to design a defense that actually recovers agents while preserving diversity and does so without extra training.

Method: The authors propose Foresight-Guided Local Purification (FLP), a training-free framework applied at each agent. Each agent performs foresight reasoning by simulating its own future behavioral trajectories over multiple upcoming chat rounds. To better capture MAS heterogeneity, they use multi-persona simulation: the same agent is run under multiple personas to generate diverse trajectory predictions. They then measure response diversity and consistency across these persona-based simulations at both the retrieval-result level and semantic level. Low diversity or inconsistent patterns serve as diagnostic signals of infection. When infection is detected, they perform localized purification. For recent infections, they rollback the agent’s image album/state to a pre-infection version. For persistent, long-term infections, they introduce Recursive Binary Diagnosis (RBD), which repeatedly partitions the image album/history, applies the same diagnostic checks to each partition, and thus pinpoints and removes the specific VirAE segments causing infection.

Result: Empirical evaluation shows that FLP dramatically reduces the maximum cumulative infection rate in MASs from over 95% under attack to below 5.47%. Furthermore, the retrieval and semantic metrics of defended systems closely track those of benign, non-attacked baselines, meaning FLP effectively mitigates infectious jailbreaks while maintaining interaction diversity and normal behavior rather than collapsing to uniform, cure-biased responses.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that global cure-factor defenses are fundamentally limited for infectious jailbreaks in multimodal MASs because they do not align with the localized, interaction-based spread of infection and they harm diversity. Their proposed FLP framework, based on foresight-guided multi-persona simulation, response-diversity diagnostics, and localized purification (including RBD), provides an effective, training-free alternative. It achieves strong reductions in infection rates while preserving benign behavior diversity, suggesting a promising direction for robust, scalable defenses in complex multi-agent LMM systems.

Abstract: Large multimodal model-based Multi-Agent Systems (MASs) enable collaborative complex problem solving through specialized agents. However, MASs are vulnerable to infectious jailbreak, where compromising a single agent can spread to others, leading to widespread compromise. Existing defenses counter this by training a more contagious cure factor, biasing agents to retrieve it over virus adversarial examples (VirAEs). However, this homogenizes agent responses, providing only superficial suppression rather than true recovery. We revisit these defenses, which operate globally via a shared cure factor, while infectious jailbreak arise from localized interaction behaviors. This mismatch limits their effectiveness. To address this, we propose a training-free Foresight-Guided Local Purification (FLP) framework, where each agent reasons over future interactions to track behavioral evolution and eliminate infections. Specifically, each agent simulates future behavioral trajectories over subsequent chat rounds. To reflect diversity in MASs, we introduce a multi-persona simulation strategy for robust prediction across interaction contexts. We then use response diversity as a diagnostic signal to detect infection by analyzing inconsistencies across persona-based predictions at both retrieval-result and semantic levels. For infected agents, we apply localized purification: recent infections are mitigated via immediate album rollback, while long-term infections are handled using Recursive Binary Diagnosis (RBD), which recursively partitions the image album and applies the same diagnosis strategy to localize and eliminate VirAEs. Experiments show that FLP reduces the maximum cumulative infection rate from over 95% to below 5.47%. Moreover, retrieval and semantic metrics closely match benign baselines, indicating effective preservation of interaction diversity.

</details>


### [145] [Runtime Evaluation of Procedural Content Generation in an Endless Runner Game Using Autonomous Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01783)
*Rishabh Kar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Momentum, an endless-runner game that unifies procedural terrain generation and autonomous, in-loop evaluation to ensure generated content remains playable and diverse in real time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Procedural Content Generation can efficiently create game content but risks producing broken, unfair, or repetitive levels. Existing approaches often evaluate content offline, disconnected from live gameplay, making it hard to guarantee quality for streamed or endless content. The paper aims to address this gap by embedding evaluation directly into the runtime generation process.

Method: The authors design Momentum, where ground tiles and environment objects are generated on the fly as the player advances. Object placement uses a constraint system inspired by Wave Function Collapse. The navigation mesh is asynchronously rebuilt to match the evolving environment. Two autonomous agents run ahead of the player: an aerial scanner that checks geometry and a ground agent that tests navigability. Their evaluation uses ray casting, physics volume sweeps, obstacle-layer filters, and structured crash reporting to detect and classify problematic segments within the main game loop. The paper also defines an evaluation framework along axes of playability, diversity, controllability, and runtime cost, and analytically derives bounds on object placement density and agent scanning complexity.

Result: The system successfully unifies generation and validation within a single runtime loop, showing that problematic segments can be detected before the player reaches them. The authors obtain quantitative measures for how saturated the level can become under given constraints and for the computational cost of the autonomous agents’ scanning per segment, demonstrating feasibility within real-time performance budgets.

Conclusion: Integrating autonomous evaluation agents directly into the runtime PCG loop enables continuous, in-place validation of streamed game content. This approach helps maintain playability and variety while providing formal, measurable guarantees on density and performance, suggesting a practical path towards safer, more controllable procedural generation in real-time games.

Abstract: Procedural Content Generation (PCG) enables game content to be created algorithmically without direct manual level-design effort, but it introduces a serious evaluation problem: generated content may become unbalanced, blocked, repetitive, or technically unsolvable. This paper presents Momentum, an endless-runner game that integrates runtime terrain generation, environment object spawning, and autonomous agent-based evaluation into a single gameplay loop. Ground tiles and environmental objects are generated dynamically as the player advances, object placement follows a constraint-driven mechanism inspired by Wave Function Collapse (WFC), and the runtime navigation surface is rebuilt asynchronously to remain consistent with the streamed environment. Two autonomous evaluation agents move ahead of the player and inspect the generated path: an aerial scanner that examines the corridor geometrically, and a ground-traversal agent that validates the same region from a navigational perspective. The evaluation pipeline combines ray casting, volumetric physics sweeps, obstacle-layer filtering, and structured crash reporting to identify problematic generated scenarios before they reach the player. The work demonstrates how generation and validation can be unified within the same runtime loop, rather than treating evaluation as a separate offline pass. Around this implementation, the paper formulates a measurable evaluation framework along the canonical PCG axes of playability, diversity, controllability, and runtime performance, derives a structural saturation bound on the spawner from its own placement constraints, and quantifies the per-segment scanning cost of the agents from first principles.

*Qisong Zhang,Wenzhuo Wu,Zhuangzhuang Jia,Yunhao Yang,Huayu Zhang,Xianghao Zang,Zhixiang He,Zhongjiang He,Kongming Liang,Zhanyu Ma*

Method: The authors propose DataEvolver, a visual data engine that structures dataset construction as closed loops around explicit goals, persistent artifacts, bounded corrective actions, and acceptance decisions. It supports various artifact modalities (images, masks, depth, normals, meshes, poses, trajectories, review traces). The system uses two coupled loops: (1) generation-time self-correction within each sample, and (2) validation-time self-expansion across dataset rounds, including scene-aware generation, feedback-driven correction, and dual-gated validation mechanisms.

Result: In an image-level object-rotation benchmark, using a fixed Qwen-Edit LoRA probe, the proposed Ours+DualGate model surpasses both the unadapted base model and a public multi-angle LoRA on SpatialEdit and on a held-out evaluation set. Ablation studies confirm incremental gains from adding scene-aware generation, feedback-based correction, and dual-gate validation.

Conclusion: DataEvolver provides a general, reusable framework for building high-quality visual datasets through explicit tracking of goals and iterative loops of review, correction, and acceptance. It is demonstrated on object-rotation data, where it yields superior editing performance, and is designed to extend to broader visual dataset construction tasks involving multiple artifact types.

Abstract: Constructing controllable visual data is a major bottleneck for image editing and multimodal understanding. Useful supervision is rarely produced by a single rendering pass; instead it emerges through iterative generation, inspection, correction, filtering, and export. We present DataEvolver, a closed-loop visual data engine that organizes this process around explicit goals, persistent artifacts, bounded corrective actions, and acceptance decisions. DataEvolver supports multiple artifact types, including RGB images, masks, depth maps, normal maps, meshes, poses, trajectories, and review traces. In the current release, the system operates through two coupled loops: generation-time self-correction within each sample and validation-time self-expansion across dataset rounds. We validate the framework on an image-level object-rotation setting. With a fixed Qwen-Edit LoRA probe, our final Ours+DualGate model outperforms both the unadapted base model and a public multi-angle LoRA on SpatialEdit and a held-out evaluation set. Ablations show a consistent improvement path from scene-aware generation to feedback-driven correction and dual-gated validation. Beyond the released rotation data, our main contribution is a reusable framework for building visual datasets through explicit goal tracking, review, correction, and acceptance loops.

### [147] [Neural Decision-Propagation for Answer Set Programming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01797)
*Thomas Eiter,Katsumi Inoue,Sota Moriyama*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce NDProp, a differentiable, neural version of a new ASP solving procedure (DProp), enabling scalable neuro-symbolic reasoning by learning to compute stable models efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing neuro-symbolic ASP methods rely on classical, non-differentiable ASP solvers, which limit scalability and make it hard to integrate with gradient-based learning. There is a need for a solver whose inner reasoning steps can be learned and optimized, improving efficiency and enabling tighter integration of logic and neural components.

Method: First, they design Decision-Propagation (DProp), a new algorithm for computing stable models in ASP that works by alternating between making falsity decisions and performing truth propagation. They prove that successful DProp runs correspond to stable models. Then they create Neural DProp (NDProp), a differentiable extension where (1) the decision steps are implemented via neural networks that learn heuristics, and (2) the propagation steps are done with fuzzy, differentiable operations. They train NDProp on tasks requiring stable model computation and neuro-symbolic reasoning, and compare against existing neuro-symbolic methods.

Result: NDProp successfully learns decision heuristics that allow it to compute stable models efficiently. In experiments on neuro-symbolic benchmarks, it achieves higher accuracy and better scalability than prior neuro-symbolic ASP approaches.

Conclusion: A differentiable, neural extension of a specialized ASP solving procedure can faithfully approximate stable model semantics while being trainable end-to-end. NDProp advances neuro-symbolic AI by improving both reasoning efficiency and performance on benchmarks, showing that learned decision heuristics inside logical solvers are a viable path to scalable neuro-symbolic integration.

Abstract: Integration of Answer Set Programming (ASP) with neural networks has emerged as a promising tool in Neuro-symbolic AI. While existing approaches extend the capabilities of ASP to real world domains, their reasoning pipelines depend on classical solvers, which is a bottleneck for scalability. To tackle this problem, we propose a new method to compute stable models, called decision-propagation (DProp), which alternates falsity decisions and truth propagations. Successful DProp computations are shown to capture the stable model semantics. We then develop Neural DProp (NDProp), a differentiable extension of DProp with neural computation for decisions and fuzzy evaluation for propagations. We evaluate the capabilities of NDProp for learning decision heuristics as well as neuro-symbolic integration, and compare it with existing neuro-symbolic approaches. The results show that NDProp can learn to efficiently compute stable models, and it improves accuracy and scalability on neuro-symbolic benchmarks.
