### [148] [NeuroState-Bench: A Human-Calibrated Benchmark for Commitment Integrity in LLM Agent Profiles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01847)
*Jia Xiao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces NeuroState-Bench, a benchmark to evaluate whether language model agents maintain coherent internal commitments over multi-turn tasks, beyond just task success.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations that focus only on final task outcomes cannot tell whether an agent maintained consistent internal commitments throughout a multi-turn interaction. This under-specifies important aspects of reliability, as two agents with identical success rates might differ in how coherently they reason and stick to previous commitments, especially under distractions. The authors aim to create a human-calibrated framework to measure this 'commitment integrity' directly and systematically across many models and tasks.

Method: The authors design NeuroState-Bench, a benchmark that uses explicit side-query probes embedded in multi-turn tasks to test whether an agent preserves its earlier commitments. The benchmark contains 144 deterministic tasks and 306 probes, organized by eight cognitively motivated failure types, clean vs distractor variants, and three difficulty levels. They evaluate 32 model profiles: 16 local models and 16 hosted large models, all run through the same pipeline. Human experts annotate and adjudicate a subset of task units to calibrate the benchmark, achieving high inter-rater reliability. They introduce commitment-integrity scoring heuristics (HCCIS-CORE, HCCIS-FULL, HCCIS+N) and related metrics (probe accuracy, state drift), then measure how well these predict terminal task failure and how rankings differ from standard task-success rankings.

Result: The authors find that task success and commitment integrity are meaningfully different dimensions: the model with highest task success is not the leader on commitment integrity, and 31 of 32 profiles change rank when integrity is used instead of success. Integrity-based rankings are also more stable when distractor variants are introduced. Their main integrity score, HCCIS-CORE, achieves 0.8469 ROC-AUC and 0.6992 PR-AUC in predicting final task failure from probe responses, outperforming the older HCCIS-FULL on PR-AUC and better aligning with the intended construct. Probe accuracy and state drift yield slightly higher ROC-AUC but worse calibration, while the neural-augmented HCCIS+N underperforms and a randomized baseline is near chance.

Conclusion: NeuroState-Bench provides a calibrated, human-validated benchmark and scoring framework that exposes commitment-integrity failures in language model agents, complementing traditional task-success metrics. It demonstrates that models that look strong on outcomes may still be weak at maintaining coherent internal commitments, and it offers a robust, distraction-resistant evaluation axis for comparing a wider variety of models than prior, more limited local-only evaluations.

Abstract: Outcome-only evaluation under-specifies whether an evaluated agent profile preserves the commitments required to solve a multi-turn task coherently. NeuroState-Bench is a human-calibrated benchmark that operationalizes commitment integrity through benchmark-defined side-query probes rather than inferred hidden activations. The released inventory contains 144 deterministic tasks and 306 benchmark-defined side-query probes spanning eight cognitively motivated failure families, paired clean and distractor variants, and three difficulty bands. The main 32-profile evaluation contains a fixed 16-profile local subset and a matched 16-profile hosted large-model subset evaluated through the same benchmark pipeline. Human calibration uses the final merged reporting scope: 104 sampled task units, 216 raw annotations, and 108 adjudicated task rows, with weighted kappa = 0.977 and ICC(2,1) = 0.977. Empirically, task success and commitment integrity diverge across this expanded grid: the success leader is not the integrity leader, 31 of 32 profiles change rank when integrity replaces task success, and integrity rankings are more stable under distractor perturbation. The primary confidence-free score HCCIS-CORE reaches 0.8469 AUC and 0.6992 PR-AUC for post-probe diagnostic discrimination of terminal task failure; the legacy full heuristic variant HCCIS-FULL reaches 0.7997 AUC and 0.6410 PR-AUC. Probe accuracy and state drift achieve slightly higher ROC-AUC, 0.8587, and better Brier/ECE, while HCCIS-CORE has substantially higher point-estimate PR-AUC and remains more closely tied to the benchmark's intended construct. The exploratory neural-augmented variant HCCIS+N is weaker overall, and a randomized subspace control approaches chance. NeuroState-Bench therefore contributes a calibrated evaluation axis for exposing commitment failures over a broader model grid than the original local-only subset.

### [149] [Sheaf-Theoretic Planning: A Categorical Foundation for Resilient Multi-Agent Autonomous Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01879)
*Manuel Hernández,Eduardo Sánchez-Soto*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper critiques classical logic-based multi-agent systems and proposes Sheaf-Theoretic Planning (STP), grounded in topos theory and sheaf semantics, as a more resilient framework for autonomous multi-agent coordination in uncertain, adversarial environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical multi-agent systems, built on event or situation calculus, struggle in realistic environments because they assume a closed world and fully known, uninterrupted plans. They handle the frame problem well but fail when there are unobserved actions, interruptions, or mismatches between agents’ beliefs and reality. The authors want a principled way to model coordination, uncertainty, and partial information in multi-agent systems that goes beyond monolithic logical models.

Method: The paper introduces and elaborates Sheaf-Theoretic Planning (STP), recasting multi-agent coordination within categorical structures, specifically topos theory and sheaf semantics. It provides a theoretical analysis of how local agent behaviors and information can be represented as sheaves, and how global system behavior emerges from gluing these local pieces. The authors examine the categorical foundations, outline how STP can be implemented in practice, and compare it to traditional logical formalisms for planning and control.

Result: The authors develop a detailed theoretical framework showing that STP can model multi-agent coordination in a way that accommodates partial observability, interruptions, and divergent beliefs. They demonstrate, at least conceptually, that STP supports more flexible and resilient planning than classical event/situation calculus approaches, and they clarify how categorical constructs (e.g., sheaves and toposes) map to concrete planning and coordination tasks.

Conclusion: Sheaf-Theoretic Planning is argued to be a promising new paradigm for resilient autonomous multi-agent systems. By grounding planning in sheaf and topos theory, it overcomes limitations of traditional logic-based frameworks tied to closed-world assumptions. The paper concludes that STP offers a mathematically rigorous path forward for designing autonomous agents capable of robust coordination in stochastic and adversarial physical environments, and it outlines STP as a foundation for future autonomous systems research and implementations.

Abstract: The challenge of engineering autonomous agents capable of navigating the stochastic and adversarial nature of the physical world has historically resided at the intersection of symbolic logic and control theory. Traditional multi-agent system (MAS) frameworks have relied heavily on monolithic logical models -- primarily variations of the event calculus and situation calculus -- to represent action, change, and temporal persistence. While these classical systems provide robust solutions to the frame problem through mechanisms like circumscription and successor state axioms, they are inherently limited by a closed-world assumption that fails in the face of unobserved agent interventions, plan interruptions, and divergent belief-reality states. The paradigm of Sheaf-Theoretic Planning (STP) emerges as a transformative alternative, grounding the problem of multi-agent coordination under the mathematical structures of topos theory and sheaf semantics. This report provides an exhaustive analysis, justification, and extension of the STP framework, exploring its categorical foundations, implementation feasibility, and role in the future of resilient autonomous systems.

### [150] [CyberAId: AI-Driven Cybersecurity for Financial Service Providers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01892)
*George Fatouros,Georgios Makridis,John Soldatos,Dimosthenis Kyriazis,Pedro Malo,George Kousiouris,Giannis Ledakis,Louiza Kachrimani,Panagiotis Rizomiliotis,Bruno Almeida,Despina Tomkou,Kostas Metaxas,Konstantinos Ilias,Christos Gkizelis,Ernstjan de Gooyert,Amin Babazadeh,Kostis Mavrogiorgos,Pepi Paraskevoulakou,Christos Xenakis,Giannis Chouchoulis,Konstantina Tripodi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes CyberAId, a hybrid multi-agent LLM-based platform that augments, rather than replaces, existing SIEM/XDR systems to improve financial-sector SOC effectiveness and regulatory alignment through shared, privacy-preserving agent skills and coordinated automation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: European financial institutions face intense regulatory pressure and high alert volumes, yet SOCs are limited by reasoning capacity rather than data or staffing. Current SIEMs cover only a subset of MITRE ATT&CK, most SOCs cannot investigate all alerts, and many breaches follow uninvestigated alerts. Frontier LLMs show strong performance on narrow cybersecurity tasks, but there is no holistic platform that integrates these capabilities, supports multi-tenant state, maps outputs to regulatory frameworks, and is robust to audits. The paper is motivated by this gap between point-solution LLM successes and the need for an integrated, regulation-aware, institution-spanning defense platform.

Method: The paper proposes a position and design blueprint for CyberAId, a hybrid multi-agent architecture where specialized LLM subagents reason over traditional SIEM/XDR telemetry instead of supplanting it. A Main Agent coordinates subagents and Reporting capabilities within a shared runtime, under bounded human-in-the-loop autonomy and guided by four falsifiable design principles. The system enables privacy-preserving federation of agent state across institutions and supports integration with additional capability packs (e.g., quantum-based authentication, digital twins for adversarial validation, eBPF kernel telemetry). The platform is model-agnostic and deployable on-premise, with explicit alignment to regulatory requirements.

Result: As a position/architecture paper, it does not report empirical benchmarks yet but specifies CyberAId as a concrete, deployable platform design. It outlines how multi-agent LLM subagents, coordinated by a Main Agent and a Reporting layer, can share state, connect to advanced telemetry and security capabilities, and operate compliantly within financial institutions. The main “result” is an architecture plus a validation plan across four financial-sector use cases, rather than measured performance improvements.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that a hybrid multi-agent LLM architecture, integrated with existing SIEM/XDR telemetry and enhanced through privacy-preserving federation, is the appropriate unit of construction for next-generation SOCs in regulated European finance. It proposes CyberAId as a practical instantiation, organized around falsifiable design principles and aligned with regulatory needs. The authors argue that validating CyberAId on selected financial use cases will demonstrate the viability of this approach, and they identify skill-based agent adaptation—where each deployment’s learned capabilities feed back into a shared defense—as the key research direction for evolving the platform into a continuously improving collective security system.

Abstract: European financial institutions face mounting regulatory pressure while their security operations centres remain constrained not by data or staffing but by reasoning capacity: enterprise SIEMs cover only a fraction of MITRE ATT&CK techniques, two thirds of SOC teams cannot keep pace with alert volumes, and the majority of breaches are preceded by alerts that are generated but never investigated. Frontier large language models now achieve state-of-the-art results on isolated cybersecurity tasks (one-day vulnerability exploitation, code-level patching, intrusion detection) yet no narrow win constitutes a platform that can compose across functions, persist multi-tenant state, map findings to regulatory regimes and survive an audit. This position paper argues that the right unit of construction is a hybrid multi-agent system in which specialised LLM subagents reason over classical SIEM/XDR telemetry rather than replacing it, share accumulated agent state across institutions through privacy-preserving federation, and can connect to complementary capability packs such as quantum-based authentication, digital twins for adversarial validation, and eBPF-based kernel telemetry. We present CyberAId, a model-agnostic, on-premise-deployable platform in which a Main Agent coordination layer, a Reporting capability, and specialist subagents operate within a shared runtime under bounded human-in-the-loop autonomy, organised around four falsifiable design principles, and aligned with relevant regulations. CyberAId will be validated at four representative financial use cases (client impersonation, anti-money-laundering for payment service providers, retail-banking incident response, and high-frequency-trading resilience) and propose skill-based agent adaptation as the most promising research direction for turning each deployment into a contribution to a continuously refined collective defence.

### [151] [Disentangling Intent from Role: Adversarial Self-Play for Persona-Invariant Safety Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01899)
*Jiajia Li,Xiaoyu Wen,Zhongtian Ma,Shuyue Hu,Qiaosheng Zhang,Zhen Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper proposes Persona-Invariant Alignment (PIA), an adversarial self-play framework to defend LLMs against persona-based jailbreak attacks by jointly evolving attack personas and training defenses that decouple safety decisions from persona context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing safety-aligned LLMs are still vulnerable to persona-based jailbreak attacks, where malicious or role-playing personas can bypass safeguards. Prior work emphasizes designing new attack strategies but lacks a systematic, mechanism-based defense that remains robust regardless of persona. The authors aim to build a defense framework that explicitly handles evolving persona-based attacks while preserving model capabilities.

Method: The authors introduce PIA, composed of two key components: (1) Persona Lineage Evolution (PLE) on the attack side, which performs adversarial self-play to iteratively evolve high-risk personas using lineage-based credit propagation to explore the persona space more efficiently; and (2) Persona-Invariant Consistency Learning (PICL) on the defense side, which imposes a unilateral KL-divergence constraint grounded in a structural separation hypothesis. PICL encourages the model’s safety-relevant decisions to remain consistent across different persona contexts, effectively decoupling safety behavior from persona instructions, and is trained in an adversarial loop against PLE-generated personas.

Result: Experiments show that PLE is effective at discovering highly adversarial persona configurations, increasing the coverage of risky persona space. When models are trained with PICL, the Attack Success Rate (ASR) of persona-based jailbreaks drops substantially compared to baselines, while standard capability metrics (e.g., general language understanding or task performance) remain largely unaffected, indicating that safety is improved without sacrificing utility.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that adversarial co-evolution of personas and defenses, combined with a persona-invariant safety objective, yields more robust alignment against persona-based jailbreak attacks. The PIA framework, especially PICL, provides a structurally motivated way to decouple safety from persona context, reducing vulnerabilities while keeping overall model performance intact, and offers a scalable paradigm for future safety alignment research.

Abstract: The growing capabilities of large language models (LLMs) have driven their widespread deployment across diverse domains, even in potentially high-risk scenarios. Despite advances in safety alignment techniques, current models remain vulnerable to emerging persona-based jailbreak attacks. Existing research on persona-based jailbreak has primarily focused on attack iterations, yet it lacks systemic and mechanistic constraints on the defense side. To address this challenge, we propose Persona-Invariant Alignment (PIA), an adversarial self-play framework that achieves co-evolution through Persona Lineage Evolution (PLE) on the attack side and Persona-Invariant Consistency Learning (PICL) on the defense side. Theoretically, PICL is grounded in the structural separation hypothesis, using a unilateral KL-divergence constraint to enable the structural decoupling of safety decisions from persona context, thereby maintaining safe behavior under persona-based jailbreak attacks. Experimental results demonstrate that PLE efficiently explores high-risk persona spaces by leveraging lineage-based credit propagation. Meanwhile, the PICL defense method significantly reduces the Attack Success Rate (ASR) while preserving the model's general capability, thereby validating the superiority and robustness of this alignment paradigm. Codes are available at https://github.com/JiajiaLi-1130/PIA.

*Noga Peleg Pelc,Gal A. Kaminka,Yoav Goldberg*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based agents rely on complex, evolving input contexts (instructions, observations, history). The quality of these contexts strongly affects system performance, so people invest effort in context engineering. However, there is no precise, standard way to describe how contexts are built and change over interaction steps. Existing descriptions using prose, ad hoc diagrams, or code inspection are informal, ambiguous, and hard to compare across systems. The authors want a clear, implementation-independent way to communicate and reason about context designs.

Method: The authors design the Agentic Context Description Language (ACDL), a domain-specific language and associated visual notation to specify LLM input contexts. ACDL includes constructs to represent role-based message sequences, dynamic content insertion, time-indexed references to past states, and conditional or iterative structures. It is meant to describe the full prompt architecture independently of any specific implementation. They show both a formal textual syntax and diagrammatic representations that can be hand-drawn or rendered from ACDL code. They then apply ACDL to document several existing LLM systems and variations to demonstrate expressiveness and usability, and provide tooling, examples, and documentation on a public website.

Result: ACDL can capture the structure and temporal evolution of LLM contexts for agentic systems in a precise yet readable way. The language and diagrams successfully describe diverse existing systems and highlight differences between alternative context strategies. The authors provide working tooling and visualizations, enabling users to write ACDL specifications and render them, thereby supporting both informal whiteboard use and more formal documentation.

Conclusion: ACDL offers a standard, implementation-agnostic way to specify and visualize how LLM agent contexts are constructed and evolve, addressing the current gap of ad hoc and imprecise descriptions. The authors advocate for its adoption by the community for day-to-day communication and academic papers about LLM systems, expecting that standardized context descriptions will improve clarity, reproducibility, and design comparison in LLM agent research and practice.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used within larger systems ("LLM agents"). These make a sequence of LLM calls, each call providing the LLM with a combination of instructions, observations, and interaction history. The design of the encoded information and its structure play a central role in the quality of the resulting system, leading to efforts spent on context engineering. It is therefore critical to communicate the composition of the LLM context in a system, and how it evolves over time. Yet, no standard exists for doing so: context construction is typically conveyed through informal prose, ad hoc diagrams, or direct inspection of code, none of which precisely capture how a prompt evolves across interaction steps or how two context representation strategies differ. To remedy this, we introduce the Agentic Context Description Language (ACDL), a language for specifying the structure and dynamics of LLM input contexts in a precise, readable, and standard manner, along with visualizations. ACDL provides constructs for specifying context aspects such as role message sequences, dynamic content, time-indexed references, and conditional or iterative structure, capturing the full architecture of a prompt independently of any particular implementation. ACDL diagrams can be hand drawn on a whiteboard, or written in formal language which can then be rendered. We describe the language, demonstrate it by documenting several existing systems and their variants, and encourage the community to adopt it for describing LLM systems context, both in day-to-day communication and in papers. Tooling, examples and documentation are available at www.acdlang.org.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing methods struggle to adapt abstractions and execution jointly without causing non-stationarity and often rely on gradient-based fine-tuning that is costly and inflexible.

Method: The authors cast pair trading as a hierarchical RL problem and parameterize both the high-level semantic policy (asset pair selection) and the low-level execution policy (trading under partial observability) with pretrained LLMs. They introduce a language-driven optimization framework that updates only the prompts for these LLM-based policies, guided by trajectory- and episode-level textual feedback. The design explicitly separates abstraction (high-level choices) from execution (low-level actions) to stabilize learning and allow targeted adaptation to delayed rewards, all without gradient-based fine-tuning.

Result: On real-world market data, the proposed framework consistently outperforms traditional pair trading approaches and other LLM-based baselines in terms of trading performance, validating that language-driven hierarchical RL can better handle credit assignment and delayed feedback in hierarchical decision-making.

Abstract: Many sequential decision-making problems exhibit hierarchical structure, where high-level semantic choices constrain downstream actions and feedback is delayed and ambiguous. Learning in such settings is challenging due to credit assignment: performance degradation may arise from flawed abstractions, suboptimal execution, or their interaction. We study this challenge through pair trading, a domain that naturally combines long-horizon semantic reasoning for asset pair selection with short-horizon execution under partial observability. We formulate pair trading as a hierarchical reinforcement learning problem and propose a language-driven optimization framework in which both high-level and low-level policies are parameterized by large language models (LLMs) and optimized exclusively through prompt updates. Our approach leverages pretrained LLMs as hierarchical policies and uses trajectory- and episode-level textual feedback to adapt abstractions and execution without gradient-based fine-tuning. By explicitly separating abstraction selection from execution, the framework reduces non-stationarity across hierarchical levels and enables targeted adaptation under delayed feedback. Experiments on real-world market data show consistent improvements over traditional and LLM-based baselines, demonstrating the effectiveness of language-driven hierarchical reinforcement learning.

### [154] [12 Angry AI Agents: Evaluating Multi-Agent LLM Decision-Making Through Cinematic Jury Deliberation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.01986)
*Ahmet Bahaddin Ersoz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper turns the film '12 Angry Men' into a benchmark for how multiple LLMs deliberate together, revealing that current models mostly anchor on their initial views and rarely reach consensus.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and evaluate how large language models behave in multi-agent deliberation settings, especially whether they can mirror human-like processes of persuasion and consensus-building as depicted in '12 Angry Men'.

Method: The authors simulate a 12-person jury from '12 Angry Men' using LLM agents with film-faithful personas, testing two models (GPT-4o and Llama-4-Scout) under three prompting conditions (baseline, open-minded instruction, and no initial vote), with three repeated trials per setup (18 runs total). They analyze voting dynamics, vote changes, and final verdicts to assess deliberative behavior.

Result: (i) In 17 of 18 simulations, the jury fails to reach unanimity, and the movie-like minority-to-majority persuasion almost never occurs, showing strong anchoring. (ii) GPT-4o shows very few vote changes (about one per run), whereas Llama-4-Scout shows more flexibility and is the only one to reach a NOT_GUILTY verdict, especially under open-minded prompts and no-initial-vote conditions. (iii) The key driver of flexibility appears to be the degree of RLHF alignment rather than raw model capability.

Conclusion: Stronger RLHF alignment appears to reduce deliberative flexibility in multi-agent LLM settings, causing models to anchor on initial positions and resist persuasion; lighter-aligned models better mimic human-like flexible deliberation. The authors position this as exploratory but argue it has implications for designing jury-of-LLMs evaluations and multi-agent debate systems.

Abstract: What if the twelve jurors of Sidney Lumet's 12 Angry Men (1957) were not men, but large language models? Would the one juror who disagrees still be able to change everyone's mind? This paper instantiates that scenario as a multi-agent benchmark for LLM deliberation: twelve agents, each conditioned on a film-faithful persona, debate the film's murder case using multi-agent framework. Two models representing opposite ends of the RLHF spectrum are tested: GPT-4o (closed-source, heavy alignment) and Llama-4-Scout (open-weight, lighter alignment), across three conditions (baseline, open-minded prompt, no initial vote), with N = 3 replications per cell (18 runs total). Three findings emerge. (i) Seventeen of eighteen runs end in a hung jury (a state where the jury fails to reach a unanimous verdict); the film's central event, gradual minority-to-majority persuasion, almost never occurs, indicating that anchoring is the dominant failure mode of current LLMs in this setting. (ii) The two models exhibit sharply different internal dynamics: GPT-4o produces a mean of 1.0 vote changes per run across all conditions, while Llama-4-Scout ranges from 2.0 (baseline) to 6.0 (open-minded prompt), and is the only model to reach a NOT\_GUILTY verdict (1 of 3 runs in the no-initial-vote condition). The same ``open-minded'' instruction is internalized by Llama and ignored by GPT-4o. (iii) This asymmetry suggests that the intensity of RLHF alignment training, not model capability, is the primary determinant of deliberative flexibility in multi-agent settings. Flexibility, not capability, tracks human deliberation. The work is framed as an exploratory study and discusses implications for jury-of-LLMs evaluation and multi-agent debate.

Results show that, when labels are limited, SSL-pretrained models outperform supervised baselines in terms of F1-score, recall, accuracy, and precision. Additionally, by providing visual insights into model decisions, Explainable AI techniques (Grad-CAM, Grad-CAM++, EigenCAM) enhance interpretability. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing personalization approaches mainly pretrain on population data or use only similar users, which can introduce bias, overlook useful information from dissimilar users, and fail to generalize well in low-data scenarios.

Method: The authors propose a unified personalization framework that trains a personalized model for each user using adaptively weighted contributions from other users. The objective function combines three parts: (1) a personal loss term on the target user’s data; (2) a similarity-weighted transfer term that leverages data from similar users; and (3) a contrastive regularization term that uses dissimilar users to penalize or suppress misleading correlations. An iterative optimization algorithm jointly updates the model parameters and the similarity weights assigned to each support user, allowing the method to learn how much to trust different users’ data.

Result: On six prediction tasks drawn from four real-world digital health datasets, the proposed method outperforms both population-level models and existing personalized baselines. It achieves up to 10% lower RMSE on large datasets and around 25% lower RMSE when the target user has limited data. The adaptively learned user weights improve data efficiency and yield interpretable patterns that can guide which users’ data is most beneficial for transfer.

Conclusion: Incorporating both similar and dissimilar users via adaptive weighting and contrastive regularization leads to more accurate and robust personalized models in digital health, especially under data scarcity. The framework reduces prediction error, enhances data efficiency, and provides interpretable insights for targeted data selection, offering a more reliable alternative to traditional population pretraining and similarity-only transfer methods.

Abstract: Personalized models are essential in digital health because individuals exhibit substantial physiological and behavioral heterogeneity. Yet personalization is limited by scarce and noisy user-specific data. Most existing methods rely on population pretraining or data from similar users only, which can lead to biased transfer and weak generalization. We propose a unified personalization framework that trains a personal model using adaptively weighted support users, including both similar and dissimilar individuals. The objective integrates personal loss, similarity-weighted transfer from similar users, and contrastive regularization from dissimilar users to suppress misleading correlations. An iterative optimization algorithm jointly updates model parameters and user similarity weights. Experiments on six tasks across four real-world digital health datasets show consistent improvements over population and personalized baselines. The method achieves up to 10% lower RMSE on large-scale datasets and approximately 25% lower RMSE in low-data settings. The learned adaptive weights improve data efficiency and provide interpretable guidance for targeted data selection.

Existing reliability techniques mainly check explicit claims against explicit sources and thus cannot adequately validate the most important aspects of AI behavior—reasoning, judgment, and intuition—which depend heavily on unverified implicit knowledge. There is a need for a mechanism to surface and validate that implicit layer.

The authors argue that by designing KOs so humans can efficiently inspect, critique, and endorse them, we change the economics of verification: what used to be too costly to document and validate becomes manageable. Over time, this creates a growing repository of vetted implicit knowledge that AI systems can be trained on or constrained by.

Result: The immediate result is a conceptual framework rather than quantitative findings. The paper articulates how KOs could bridge the gap between powerful but unverified AI reasoning and human oversight, outlines the expected benefits (more systematic validation, better tracking of provenance, incremental improvement), and clarifies why existing reliability tools that only operate on explicit knowledge are insufficient.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that future reliable AI systems must be built on infrastructure that makes implicit human knowledge visible and verifiable. Knowledge Objects provide such an infrastructure by externalizing reasoning patterns and other tacit practices into structured, human-inspectable forms. With KOs, human validation can accumulate over time, progressively improving AI reliability specifically in high‑value areas like reasoning and judgment, where current verification approaches are weakest.

Abstract: This position paper argues that reliable AI requires infrastructure for human validation of implicit knowledge. AI learns from both explicit knowledge (papers, documentation, structured databases) and implicit knowledge (reasoning patterns, debugging processes, intermediate steps). Implicit knowledge remains unexternalized because documentation cost exceeds perceived value -- yet AI learns from it indiscriminately, acquiring both beneficial patterns and harmful biases. Current reliability methods can only verify explicit knowledge against sources, creating a fundamental gap: the most valuable AI capabilities (reasoning, judgment, intuition) are precisely those we cannot verify. We propose Knowledge Objects (KOs) -- structured artifacts that externalize implicit knowledge into forms humans can inspect, verify, and endorse. KOs transform verification economics: what was previously too costly to verify becomes feasible, enabling accumulated human validation to improve reliability over time.

### [158] [Tenability and Weak Semantics: Modeling Non-uniform Defense -- Extended Version](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02024)
*Uri Andrews,Luca San Mauro,John Spoerl*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new family of dialogue-based semantics, called tenability, for abstract argumentation frameworks, focusing on when an argument can be strategically maintained in debate, and studies its formal properties and computational complexity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing weak argumentation semantics relax which attacks count as relevant but still demand a uniform defense against all reasonable counterarguments, even if those counterarguments are mutually inconsistent. This uniformity fails to capture inherently strategic aspects of debate where the appropriate response depends on which particular attack is actually pursued by an opponent. The paper is motivated by the need for semantics that better model such strategic, dialogue-based defensibility, illustrated via benchmark patterns (self-defeating attack, floating assignment, disjunctive reinstatement) where previous semantics behave counterintuitively.

Method: The authors define a new family of semantics, called tenability, via dialogue/commitment-game formalisms over abstract argumentation frameworks. They construct three variants—static tenability, tenability, and strong tenability—using monotone commitment games constrained to finite, conflict-free moves and differing in how strictly they constrain the players' obligations. They then compare these semantics to existing weak semantics (showing implications and separations) and perform complexity-theoretic analysis to classify the decision problems associated with each tenability notion.

Result: The paper introduces and formally defines static tenability, tenability, and strong tenability as dialogue-based semantics for abstract argumentation. It demonstrates that these notions behave differently from all existing weak semantics on key benchmark patterns (self-defeating attack, floating assignment, disjunctive reinstatement), and establishes their relative strengths and relationships with known semantics via implication and separation results. Computationally, the authors show that deciding whether an argument is statically tenable is Π^P_2-complete, while deciding tenability and strong tenability is PSPACE-complete for finite argumentation frameworks.

Conclusion: Tenability semantics provide a more strategically sensitive account of when arguments can be maintained in debate than existing weak semantics, especially in settings where opponents may launch mutually inconsistent but individually reasonable attacks. The three variants capture different dialogue obligations, are formally well-characterized relative to prior work, and come with precise complexity classifications, albeit at a significant computational cost (Π^P_2- and PSPACE-completeness). This framework opens up new directions for modeling dialogical, strategy-dependent defensibility in abstract argumentation.

Abstract: In Dung-style abstract argumentation, various semantics capture notions of acceptability of arguments. The admissibility semantics capture the notion that an argument can be consistently defended from any potential counterargument. Weak semantics often relax the demands of admissibility by restricting which counterarguments must be taken seriously (e.g., discounting self-defeating or otherwise incoherent attacks). Many prominent proposals for weak semantics remain extension-based in a stronger sense. While these semantics discount attacks from arguments which are considered unreasonable, they still require a uniform defense against all reasonable arguments, even if they are collectively inconsistent. This uniformity can be too demanding when defensibility is inherently strategic, and thus the appropriate reply depends on the opponent's line of attack.
  We introduce tenability, a family of dialogue-based semantics that formalize when a designated argument (or a set of arguments) can be maintained in debate by a proponent against any conflict-free attack which the opponent may present. The approach is motivated by three natural benchmark patterns: self-defeating attack, floating assignment, and disjunctive reinstatement, on which tenability behaves differently from all weak semantics previously considered in the literature. We define three variants -- static tenability, tenability, and strong tenability -- via monotone commitment games over finite conflict-free moves, differing in the obligations imposed on the disputants. We establish the relative strength of these notions, prove implications and separations with previously studied weak semantics, and we analyze computational complexity on finite frameworks: deciding static tenability is $Π^P_2$-complete, while deciding tenability and strong tenability is PSPACE-complete.

### [160] [The Dynamic Gist-Based Memory Model (DGMM): A Memory-Centric Architecture for Artificial Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02106)
*Terry Dorsey,Kevin Huggins*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a memory-centric AI architecture, DGMM, where experience is stored explicitly in a persistent, graph-structured memory to improve temporal grounding, provenance, and interpretability, instead of relying solely on fixed model parameters.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large AI models, especially LLMs, encode experience implicitly in parameters trained on static corpora. This leads to limitations in persistent memory across interactions, temporal grounding of knowledge, tracking provenance of information, and interpreting how and why the system arrives at outputs. The paper is motivated by the need for an architecture where experience is stored explicitly, can evolve over time, and can be inspected and reinterpreted without retraining.

Method: The authors define a Dynamic Gist-Based Memory Model (DGMM) that treats memory as a first-class reasoning substrate. Experience is encoded as an evolving graph-structured episodic-semantic memory, with nodes and edges representing conceptual structures linked to time, source, and interaction context. They formalize a schema and architectural invariants based on additive memory growth (no destructive overwriting) and recall-conditioned interpretation, where selective, cue-based retrieval constructs a working memory for reasoning.

Result: They derive and specify theoretical properties of DGMM: (1) episodic persistence, where experiences remain stored over time; (2) locality of cue-conditioned surprise, where unexpected information is localized to specific parts of the memory graph; and (3) the ability to vary context and interpretation without altering the underlying stored structures. These results characterize how DGMM supports dynamic interpretation and recall while keeping memory structurally stable.

Conclusion: DGMM offers a coherent, memory-centric architectural theory for AI in which experience is explicit, persistent, and temporally grounded. By separating persistent memory from model parameters and using cue-conditioned recall to build working memory, the architecture enables evolving reinterpretation of experience without retraining and improves provenance, interpretability, and context-awareness in AI systems.

Abstract: Contemporary artificial intelligence systems achieve strong performance through large-scale parameterization, retrieval augmentation, and training on extensive static corpora. Despite these advances, they continue to face limitations in persistent memory, temporal grounding, provenance, and interpretability. These challenges are especially pronounced in large language models, where experience is encoded implicitly in fixed parameters, limiting the ability to preserve, inspect, and reinterpret past interactions over time.
  This paper establishes a memory-centric architectural foundation for artificial intelligence in which experience is represented explicitly and persistently to support temporal grounding, provenance, and interpretability. It proposes an alternative to parameter-centric approaches by treating memory as a first-class, structured substrate for reasoning.
  We introduce the Dynamic Gist-Based Memory Model (DGMM), an architecture in which experience is represented as an evolving, graph-structured episodic-semantic memory. DGMM encodes experience as interconnected conceptual structures grounded in time, source, and interaction context, and defines selective, cue-conditioned recall as the mechanism for constructing working memory. A formal schema and architectural invariants are provided based on additive memory growth and recall-conditioned interpretation.
  The results specify properties of DGMM, including episodic persistence, locality of cue-conditioned surprise, and contextual variability without structural modification of stored memory. DGMM provides a coherent architectural theory in which memory is explicit and persistent, supporting evolving interpretation without retraining and enabling interpretable, context-aware, and temporally grounded AI systems.

### [161] [Reinforcement Learning Trained Observer Control for Bearings-Only Tracking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02120)
*Branko Ristic,Sanjeev Arulampalam*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Deep RL-based observer control (using DQN on belief states from a cubature Kalman filter) improves autonomous bearings-only target tracking, achieving a superior trade-off between accuracy and robustness via a multi-objective reward balancing Euclidean and Mahalanobis errors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bearings-only target tracking suffers from poor observability and filter inconsistency when the observer path is not well chosen. Classical heuristic or information-theoretic manoeuvre strategies either focus on average accuracy or on information gain but can yield large worst-case errors and inconsistent filtering. There is a need for an autonomous, data-driven control policy that directly optimises tracking performance while explicitly accounting for estimation consistency and robustness.

Method: Formulate the observer manoeuvring problem as a belief Markov decision process, where the belief is the posterior distribution output by a cubature Kalman filter (CKF). Define a reward that encodes two conflicting objectives: (1) minimise Euclidean distance between true and estimated target position; (2) maintain CKF consistency via the Mahalanobis distance. Combine these via geometric interpolation along the Pareto front, controlled by a scalar weight β∈[0,1]. Implement the control policy as a deep Q-network (DQN) that maps belief states to manoeuvre actions. Train the DQN over 50,000 simulated tracking episodes. Evaluate the trained policy via 5,000 Monte Carlo runs and compare against a perpendicular-to-bearing heuristic and a D-optimal Fisher-information-based controller.

Result: Across extensive Monte Carlo simulations, the DQN policies achieve competitive or better tracking performance than both baselines. In particular, with β=0.7, the learned policy attains similar mean target position accuracy to the D-optimal Fisher information strategy while dramatically improving robustness: the worst-case tracking error is reduced by nearly an order of magnitude. This improvement is attributed to the reward’s Mahalanobis component, which encourages filter-consistent trajectories and avoids highly informative but risky manoeuvres that can destabilise estimation.

Conclusion: A deep reinforcement learning approach using DQN on CKF-based belief states can effectively control an observer for bearings-only tracking, simultaneously accounting for accuracy and estimation consistency. By tuning the geometric interpolation parameter β in the reward, one can navigate the Pareto trade-off between mean performance and robustness. The best-performing setting (β≈0.7) matches an information-theoretic benchmark in mean error while substantially lowering worst-case errors, suggesting that learned, consistency-aware policies are a promising alternative to handcrafted manoeuvre heuristics and pure information-maximisation criteria.

Abstract: This paper develops a deep reinforcement learning based observer control policy for autonomous bearings-only tracking of a moving target. The observer manoeuvre problem is formulated as a belief Markov decision process, where the belief state is represented by the posterior of a cubature Kalman filter (CKF). The reward function is designed to address two conflicting objectives: minimising the absolute target position estimation error (Euclidean distance) and maintaining CKF estimation consistency (Mahalanobis distance). The reward is formulated as a geometric interpolation between the two objectives on the Pareto front, parametrised by a weighting factor $β\in [0,1]$. The policy is implemented as a deep Q-network (DQN) trained over 50,000 episodes. Performance is evaluated over 5,000 Monte Carlo episodes and compared against two baselines: the perpendicular-to-bearing heuristic and the D-optimal Fisher information maximisation criterion. The results show that the DQN policy at $β= 0.7$ achieves the best trade-off between accuracy and robustness: it matches the information-theoretic baseline on mean tracking accuracy while reducing the worst-case error by nearly a factor of ten, owing to the implicit filter-consistency regularisation provided by the Mahalanobis term in the reward.

It is unclear how to best allocate compute and model capacity across components (planning, acting, memory) in multi-agent systems, and whether training should focus on all parts or mainly on planning.

Method: Design a modular three-role architecture: planner (high-level decisions), actor (execution), and memory manager (context). Perform systematic compute-allocation experiments varying model capacity and compute per component. Result: Compute-allocation analysis shows planning capacity and compute dominate overall task performance, whereas execution and memory need much less to be competitive. Planner-centric RL yields robust performance gains in long-horizon benchmarks, improving automation quality without significantly increasing overall compute. The architecture attains state-of-the-art or strong results on web navigation, OS control, and tool-use tasks.

Conclusion: In long-horizon LM-based agents, high-level planning is the primary bottleneck and the most compute-sensitive component. This supports planner-centric design as a practical recipe for building scalable long-horizon automation systems.

Abstract: Language model (LM)-based agents have demonstrated promising capabilities in automating complex tasks from natural language instructions, yet they continue to struggle with long-horizon planning and reasoning. To address this, we propose an enhanced multi-agent framework that decomposes automation into three roles: a planner for high-level decision-making, an actor for task execution, and a memory manager for contextual reasoning. While this modular decomposition aligns with established design patterns, our core contribution lies in a systematic compute-allocation analysis, revealing that planning is the dominant factor influencing task performance. Execution and memory management require significantly less compute and model capacity to achieve competitive results. Building on these insights, we introduce a planner-centric reinforcement learning approach, which exclusively optimizes the planner using trajectory-level rewards from a VLM-as-judge, while freezing the other components. Extensive experiments on benchmarks spanning web navigation, OS control, and tool use demonstrate that concentrating model capacity and learning on high-level planning yields robust and compute-efficient improvements in long-horizon agent automation. Our code is publicly released.

### [163] [Retrieval and Multi-Hop Reasoning in 1M-Token Context Windows: Evaluating LLMs on Classical Chinese Text](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02173)
*Eric H. C. Chow*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well five leading 1M-token context LLMs actually retrieve and reason over very long inputs using a classical Chinese corpus, finding that simple long-range retrieval is solved but multi-hop reasoning over long context degrades in distinct ways across models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vendors advertise 1M-token context windows, but it is unclear whether models can effectively use such long contexts for both direct retrieval and more complex multi-hop reasoning, especially in non-Western, domain-specific corpora. The paper aims to systematically test what these long contexts are really good for and where they break down.

Method: They design two tests on a classical Chinese corpus. Test 1: single-needle retrieval at 1M tokens, planting three biographical facts (needles) at different depths and pairing each with real vs altered variants to distinguish true in-context retrieval from reliance on training priors. Test 2: a three-hop chain-traversal task across large contexts (256K, 512K, 1M tokens) to see how performance changes when answering requires intermediate reasoning across multiple pieces of information. They evaluate five frontier LLMs with claimed 1M-token contexts: Gemini 3.1 Pro, Claude Opus 4.7, GPT-5.5, Qwen3.6-plus, and DeepSeek V4 Pro.

Result: For Test 1, the strongest models (Gemini 3.1 Pro, Claude Opus 4.7, GPT-5.5) achieve 100% accuracy on single-needle retrieval at 1M tokens, indicating that direct long-range retrieval is effectively solved for them. In Test 2, multi-hop reasoning reveals three distinct degradation patterns as context length increases: (1) Stable regime: Gemini Pro and Claude maintain >80% accuracy up to 512K tokens with only modest drop at 1M; (2) Late-cliff regime: GPT-5.5 and Qwen3.6-plus perform reasonably up to 512K but collapse sharply between 512K and 1M; (3) Smooth-decline regime: DeepSeek V4 Pro shows a gradual decline in accuracy from 256K through 1M tokens.

Conclusion: Nominal context window size alone is a poor indicator of practical long-context, multi-hop reasoning ability. Although headline 1M-token windows enable perfect single-needle retrieval for top models, their usable reasoning performance over long contexts diverges, with the transition from 512K to 1M tokens emerging as the key differentiator among current 1M-context flagship LLMs.

Abstract: We evaluate the long-context retrieval and reasoning capabilities of five frontier large language models with advertised 1M-token context windows on a classical Chinese corpus. Two complementary studies are reported. Test 1 measures single-needle retrieval at 1M tokens of input, with three biographical needles planted at three depths and pairs of real (training-prior-consistent) and altered (training-prior-contradicting) variants to separate genuine in-context retrieval from reliance on memorised training data. Test 2, a follow-up designed to probe whether long-context capability degrades when retrieval requires intermediate reasoning, measures three-hop chain traversal across three context tiers (256K, 512K, and 1M tokens). We find that single-needle retrieval at 1M is essentially solved for the strongest models - Gemini 3.1 Pro, Claude Opus 4.7, and GPT-5.5 each achieve 100% - but that multi-hop performance reveals three distinct decay signatures: a stable regime (Gemini Pro, Claude) maintaining greater than 80% accuracy through 512K with modest degradation at 1M; a late-cliff regime (GPT-5.5, Qwen3.6-plus) collapsing sharply between 512K and 1M; and a smooth-decline regime (DeepSeek V4 Pro) decaying gradually across the entire range. The findings suggest that nominal context-window length is a poor proxy for usable long-context multi-hop capability, and that the sharpest discriminator between current 1M-context flagships is the 512K-to-1M transition.

### [164] [Intervention Complexity as a Canonical Reward and a Measure of Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02175)
*Brendan McCane*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They extend the Legg–Hutter intelligence framework by replacing externally-given rewards with a canonical, environment-derived reward based on a new notion called intervention complexity, and use this to define a two-dimensional measure of intelligence that separates competence from learning efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The Legg–Hutter universal intelligence measure depends on an externally specified reward function, which is arbitrary and requires extra normative input. The authors want a notion of reward that is canonical, derived from the environment and computational resources, so that intelligence can be measured without hand-designed rewards and with clearer foundations for comparing agents and studying superintelligence.

Method: They introduce a new quantity, intervention complexity (IC), defined relative to a resource function rho that encodes an inductive bias (e.g., program length, runtime, or energy). They axiomatize IC via five properties—environment-derivedness, universality, minimality, sensitivity, and achievement preference—and show that rho-IC defines a universal, canonical reward for any such resource bias. They then define intelligence in two dimensions: agent competence (performance vs. a hypothetical oracle-optimal agent) and learning efficiency (rate of improvement of competence with experience). Using complexity-theoretic and information-theoretic arguments, they analyze how different choices of resource function affect the computability of IC and the gap between having oracle access and not.

Result: They obtain a family of canonical reward functions, one for each resource bias rho, that complete the Legg–Hutter framework without needing external rewards. They prove a separation theorem: when rho counts actions, the corresponding IC is computable in polynomial time, but when rho is program length and no oracle is available, IC becomes uncomputable. The difference between oracle-based IC and non-oracle IC is shown to match the information-theoretic content of learning. This yields a precise way to discuss and compare competence and learning efficiency across agents.

Conclusion: By grounding rewards in intervention complexity tied to resource biases, they turn the Legg–Hutter intelligence measure into a family of canonical, environment-derived measures. Their two-dimensional view separates how good an agent can be (competence) from how quickly it gets there (learning efficiency), and their computability and separation results clarify how inductive biases and resource assumptions shape what forms of general intelligence are measurable and learnable. They suggest these ideas can inform discussions of superintelligence and strategies for pre-training broadly capable agents.

Abstract: The Legg--Hutter universal intelligence measure provides a rigorous scalar assessment of general intelligence as expected reward across all computable environments, weighted by simplicity. However, the measure presupposes an externally specified reward function, raising the question of whether the reward primitive is inherently arbitrary or whether a canonical choice exists. We propose a new measure, called intervention complexity, that has five natural properties: environment-derivedness, universality, minimality, sensitivity, and achievement preference. Given a resource function rho encoding an inductive bias (such as program length, execution time, or energy), rho-intervention complexity is a universal reward. The result yields a family of canonical rewards indexed by resource bias, providing a principled completion of the Legg--Hutter framework that does not require external normative input. We further propose a two-dimensional characterisation of intelligence: agent competence (how well the agent performs relative to the oracle optimum) and learning efficiency (how quickly this competence improves with experience). A separation theorem establishes that the choice of resource bias determines the computability of the resulting measure: action-count IC is computable in polynomial time, while program-length IC without oracle access is uncomputable, with the gap between oracle and bare IC precisely quantifying the information-theoretic content of learning. We discuss implications for superintelligence and for pre-training universal agents.

*Haixin Wang,Hejie Cui,Chenwei Zhang,Xin Liu,Shuowei Jin,Shijie Geng,Xinyang Zhang,Nasser Zalmout,Zhenyu Shi,Yizhou Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose T^2PO, a token- and turn-level uncertainty-aware exploration control method that stabilizes multi-turn RL training for reasoning LLMs and improves performance on interactive tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-turn RL for reasoning LLMs suffers from training instability and collapses, even with existing stabilization methods. The authors argue that a key cause is inefficient exploration: policies repeatedly choose low-information actions that neither reduce uncertainty nor advance task progress in complex interactive environments. There is a need for a principled way to control exploration more finely in multi-turn settings.

Method: They introduce Token- and Turn-level Policy Optimization (T^2PO), an RL framework that is explicitly uncertainty-aware and operates at two granularities. At the token level, the method monitors changes in uncertainty during generation and triggers a thinking intervention (e.g., more deliberate reasoning or planning) once the marginal change in uncertainty drops below a threshold. At the turn level, it tracks exploration progress across interactions and identifies turns with negligible exploratory value, then dynamically resamples such turns to avoid wasting rollouts, thus reallocating training effort to more informative experiences.

Result: Across multiple interactive benchmarks—WebShop, ALFWorld, and Search QA—T^2PO yields more stable RL training dynamics and avoids typical collapse behaviors, while also improving task performance metrics. It achieves these gains with higher exploration efficiency, meaning fewer rollouts are wasted on low-information actions or turns.

Conclusion: Fine-grained, uncertainty-aware control of exploration at both the token and turn levels is effective for stabilizing multi-turn RL training for reasoning LLMs. T^2PO reduces inefficient exploration, improves training stability, and boosts performance across diverse interactive tasks, suggesting that more structured exploration control is a key ingredient for robust RL-based reasoning LLMs.

Abstract: Recent progress in multi-turn reinforcement learning (RL) has significantly improved reasoning LLMs' performances on complex interactive tasks. Despite advances in stabilization techniques such as fine-grained credit assignment and trajectory filtering, instability remains pervasive and often leads to training collapse. We argue that this instability stems from inefficient exploration in multi-turn settings, where policies continue to generate low-information actions that neither reduce uncertainty nor advance task progress. To address this issue, we propose Token- and Turn-level Policy Optimization (T$^2$PO), an uncertainty-aware framework that explicitly controls exploration at fine-grained levels. At the token level, T$^2$PO monitors uncertainty dynamics and triggers a thinking intervention once the marginal uncertainty change falls below a threshold. At the turn level, T$^2$PO identifies interactions with negligible exploration progress and dynamically resamples such turns to avoid wasted rollouts. We evaluate T$^2$PO in diverse environments, including WebShop, ALFWorld, and Search QA, demonstrating substantial gains in training stability and performance improvements with better exploration efficiency. Code is available at: https://github.com/WillDreamer/T2PO.

However, as their applications become increasingly widespread, recent studies have revealed that VLMs are vulnerable to backdoor attacks. Existing backdoor attacks on VLMs primarily rely on data poisoning by adding visual triggers and modifying text labels, where the induced image-text mismatch makes poisoned samples easy to detect. To address this limitation, we propose the Clean-Label Backdoor Attack on VLMs via Diffusion Models (CBV), which leverages diffusion models to generate natural poisoned examples via score matching. Specifically, CBV modifies the score during the reverse generation process of the diffusion model to guide the generation of poisoned samples that contain triggered image features. To further enhance the effectiveness of the attack, we incorporate the textual information of the triggered images as multimodal guidance during generation. Moreover, to enhance stealthiness, we introduce a GradCAM-guided Mask (GM) that restricts modifications to only the most semantically important regions, rather than the entire image. ### [168] [Submodular Benchmark Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02209)
*Alexander Smola*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a principled way to pick a small, informative subset of benchmarks for evaluating large language models by modeling benchmark scores as a multivariate Gaussian and using submodular maximization objectives like entropy and mutual information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating large language models on many benchmarks is computationally and financially expensive, and many benchmarks are redundant because their results are highly correlated. The authors aim to systematically reduce the number of benchmarks needed while preserving as much information as possible about model performance, instead of choosing subsets heuristically.

Method: They model benchmark scores as a multivariate Gaussian and formalize benchmark subset selection as a submodular maximization problem. They consider two objectives: (1) entropy of the selected subset, which is the log-determinant of its covariance matrix and corresponds to pivoted Cholesky factorization with known spectral residual guarantees; and (2) mutual information between the selected and remaining benchmarks, which is submodular but generally non-monotone. Despite non-monotonicity, they find it empirically monotone for small subset sizes and thus apply a greedy selection algorithm.

Result: On three covariance matrices derived from ten public LLM leaderboards, they evaluate how well different selection strategies allow imputing (predicting) the unselected benchmark scores. The mutual-information-based selection consistently yields better imputation performance than entropy-based selection when only small subsets of benchmarks are chosen.

Conclusion: Submodular objectives based on a Gaussian model provide a principled method for selecting informative benchmark subsets for LLM evaluation. While entropy-based (pivoted Cholesky) selection has strong theoretical guarantees, mutual-information-based selection is empirically superior for small subset sizes in the examined leaderboard data, making it a promising practical strategy for reducing evaluation cost without sacrificing much information.

Abstract: Evaluating large language models across many benchmarks is expensive, yet many benchmarks are highly correlated. We formalize the selection of a small, informative subset as submodular maximization under a multivariate Gaussian model. Entropy (log-determinant covariance) and mutual information between selected and remaining benchmarks arise as natural objectives. Both are submodular; entropy selection coincides with pivoted Cholesky and has spectral residual bounds, while mutual information is non-monotone in general but empirically monotone for small subsets, so we optimize it greedily. Experiments on three matrices from ten public leaderboards show that mutual information selection outperforms entropy for imputation at small subsets.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large vision-language models are powerful but too computationally and memory intensive for direct deployment on mobile devices. Device–edge co-inference with speculative decoding is a promising solution, where a smaller on-device model drafts outputs and a larger edge model verifies them. However, naively extending speculative decoding to VLMs is inefficient due to costly visual-token processing and large communication overhead between device and edge. The paper aims to enable practical, efficient VLM inference in such settings without sacrificing accuracy.

Method: The authors propose CoVSpec, a collaborative speculative decoding framework tailored for VLMs in a device–edge setting. (1) They introduce a training-free visual token reduction method on the mobile device that prunes redundant visual tokens, using criteria based on query relevance, token activity, and low-rank dependency. This reduces the visual computation burden. (2) They design an adaptive drafting strategy that dynamically tunes both the frequency with which drafts are verified by the edge model and the length of each draft, balancing speed and accuracy. (3) They develop a parallel branching mechanism with decoupled verification and correction so that the draft model can continue useful computation while the edge model verifies, and to minimize the amount of data transmitted for corrections. These components are integrated into a speculative decoding pipeline for VLMs.

Result: On multiple benchmarks, CoVSpec significantly improves system-level efficiency compared to standard approaches. It achieves up to 2.21× higher throughput than running only the large target model on the edge (target-only inference) and cuts communication overhead by more than 96% relative to existing baselines. Importantly, these gains are obtained without degrading task accuracy, indicating that the pruning and adaptive strategies do not harm model performance.

Conclusion: CoVSpec demonstrates that carefully designed speculative decoding tailored to VLM characteristics can make device–edge collaborative inference both efficient and accurate. By reducing redundant visual tokens, adapting the drafting process, and parallelizing verification and correction, it addresses the main bottlenecks of naïve VLM speculative decoding—compute and communication. This suggests a practical path for deploying powerful VLM capabilities on resource-constrained mobile devices through efficient cooperation with edge servers.

Abstract: Vision-language models (VLMs) have demonstrated strong capabilities in multimodal perception and reasoning. However, deploying large VLMs on mobile devices remains challenging due to their substantial computational and memory demands. A practical alternative is device-edge co-inference, where a lightweight draft VLM on the mobile device collaborates with a larger target VLM on the edge server via speculative decoding. Nevertheless, directly extending speculative decoding to VLMs suffers from severe inefficiency due to excessive visual-token computation and high communication overhead. To address these challenges, we propose CoVSpec, an efficient collaborative speculative decoding framework for VLM inference. Specifically, we first develop a training-free visual token reduction framework that prunes redundant visual tokens on the mobile device by jointly considering query relevance, token activity, and low-rank dependency. Moreover, we design an adaptive drafting strategy that dynamically adjusts both the verification frequency and the draft length. In addition, we introduce a parallel branching mechanism with decoupled verification-correction to improve draft-side utilization during target-side verification and reduce correction-related transmission overhead. Experiments on multiple benchmarks show that CoVSpec achieves up to 2.21x higher throughput than target-only inference and reduces communication overhead by more than 96% compared with baselines, without compromising task accuracy.
