### [199] [SCGNN: Semantic Consistency enhanced Graph Neural Network Guided by Granular-ball Computing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02617)
*Genhao Tian,Taihua Xu,Shuyin Xia,Qinghua Zhang,Jie Yang,Jianjun Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes SCGNN, a plug-and-play GNN framework that uses granular-ball computing to capture group-level semantic consistency, enhancing both graph structure and supervision for scalable and robust graph representation learning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing GNN methods often rely on kNN or exhaustive node-level full search to determine semantic neighbors, which is computationally expensive, scales poorly, and can introduce noisy or rigid neighbor relationships. There is a need for a more efficient, scalable, and noise-robust way to model semantic consistency among nodes at a higher, group level.

Method: The authors introduce Semantic Consistency enhanced Graph Neural Network (SCGNN), which employs granular-ball computing (GBC) to adaptively partition nodes into granular balls that capture group-level semantic structure. SCGNN then uses a dual enhancement strategy: (1) a structure enhancement module that builds an anchor-based graph where each anchor is a virtual node representing the semantic of a granular ball, injecting group-level semantics into the graph; and (2) a supervision enhancement module that performs label consistency checking (LCC) by combining GBC-derived predictions and model pseudo-labels to refine supervision signals. SCGNN is designed as a plug-and-play module compatible with different GNN backbones, jointly encoding the original and augmented graphs and fusing their predictions during forward pass, while using enhanced supervision during backpropagation.

Result: Although specific quantitative results are not detailed in the abstract, SCGNN reportedly reduces computational cost compared to node-level full search approaches, better scales to large graphs, and improves robustness to noise by modeling group-level instead of purely node-level semantic relationships. The dual enhancement strategy yields more reliable graph structures and supervision signals, leading to better graph representation learning.

Conclusion: SCGNN demonstrates that modeling semantic consistency at a group level via granular-ball computing is an effective way to enhance GNNs. By jointly improving graph structure and supervision in a plug-and-play fashion, the framework offers a scalable, noise-robust alternative to traditional kNN or full search-based neighbor selection for graph representation learning, and can be integrated with various existing GNN architectures.

Abstract: Capturing semantic consistency among nodes is crucial for effective graph representation learning. Existing approaches typically rely on $k$-nearest neighbors ($k$NN) or other node-level full search algorithms (FSA) to mine semantic relationships via exhaustive pairwise similarity computation, which suffer from high computational complexity and rigid neighbor selection, limiting scalability and introducing noisy connections. In this paper, we propose the Semantic Consistency enhanced Graph Neural Network (SCGNN), a novel plug-and-play framework that leverages granular-ball computing (GBC) to efficiently capture semantic consistency in a scalable manner. Unlike node-level FSA methods, SCGNN models group-level semantic structure by adaptively partitioning nodes into granular balls, significantly reducing computational cost while improving robustness to noise. To effectively utilize the discovered group-level semantic consistency, we design a dual enhancement strategy. Specifically, (1) a structure enhancement module constructs an anchor-based graph structure, where each anchor is a virtual node representing the group-level semantic carried by a granular ball, then injecting group-level semantic information into the graph structure; and (2) a supervision enhancement module performs label consistency checking (LCC) by combining GBC predictions with model-generated pseudo-labels, thereby producing more reliable supervision signals. SCGNN is compatible with various GNN backbones. During the forward propagation of SCGNN, the vanilla graph and the augment graph are jointly encoded, and their predictions are fused; during the backpropagation, the supervision enhancement module provides enhanced supervision signals to guide parameter updates.

The paper is motivated by the need for a unifying conceptual foundation to reason about and balance these trade-offs in modern ML and FM systems.

Method: This is a conceptual / position paper rather than an empirical study. The authors: (1) Reinterpret standard trustworthy AI trade-offs as incompatibilities between different invariance requirements under interventions/changes in the data-generating process. (2) Use tools and language from causal inference—causal graphs, interventions, and invariances—to provide a unifying perspective on these trade-offs. (3) Argue that selective invariance, guided by causal structure, can help soften or resolve some trade-offs. (4) Discuss how causal assumptions can be embedded explicitly (via causal models) or implicitly (via architectures/training) in classical ML and foundation models. (5) Identify open research problems and design principles for causality-based trustworthy AI.

Result: The paper does not present new algorithms or experiments; its primary results are conceptual contributions. (2) Causal reasoning clarifies which invariances are appropriate for different trust objectives and use cases. (3) By adopting selective invariance based on causal structure (deciding which mechanisms to hold fixed under which changes), some trade-offs can be systematically managed or softened. (4) This causal perspective extends to both traditional ML and large foundation models, where causality may already be encoded implicitly. It also surfaces concrete directions for integrating causal assumptions into FM design and evaluation.

Conclusion: Causality is not just helpful but in many cases necessary for principled reasoning about and managing trade-offs in trustworthy AI. Viewing fairness, robustness, privacy, and explainability as competing invariance requirements over a causal data-generating process provides a unified framework to explain why these trade-offs arise and how they might be mitigated by selective invariance. The authors conclude that future work on trustworthy AI—especially in large-scale foundation models—should explicitly incorporate causal assumptions and tools, and they highlight open challenges and research opportunities in operationalizing this causal perspective in real-world AI systems.

Abstract: As artificial intelligence (AI), including machine learning (ML) models and foundation models (FMs), is increasingly deployed in high-stakes domains, ensuring their trustworthiness has become a central challenge. However, the core trustworthy AI objectives, such as fairness, robustness, privacy, and explainability, are hard to achieve simultaneously, especially while preserving utility. This position paper argues that causality is necessary to understand and balance trade-offs in performance and multiple objectives of trustworthy AI. We ground our arguments in re-interpreting trustworthy AI trade-offs as incompatible invariance requirements under different changes to the data-generating process. We then illustrate that causality provides a unifying framework for understanding how trade-offs in trustworthy AI arise, and how they can be softened or resolved through selective invariance. This perspective applies to both classical ML models and large-scale FMs. Our paper discusses how causal assumptions may be applied explicitly or implicitly in modern large-scale systems. Finally, we outline open challenges and opportunities for using causality to build more trustworthy AI.

### [201] [Deciphering Shortcut Learning from an Evolutionary Game Theory Perspective](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02658)
*Xiayang Li,Kuo Gai,Shihua Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper uses evolutionary game theory and neural tangent features to theoretically analyze how shortcut learning arises in deep networks, showing GD and SGD converge to different stochastically stable states (shortcut vs core subnetworks) and how noise affects shortcut bias.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Shortcut learning makes deep models rely on spurious, non-essential features, but there is limited theoretical understanding of how these shortcuts arise during training. The authors aim to formally define core vs shortcut features and explain, from a dynamical systems perspective, why standard optimization procedures produce shortcut bias and when it can be mitigated.

Method: They formally define core and shortcut features/subnetworks, and then reinterpret training dynamics in the neural tangent kernel regime as an evolutionary game: data samples are players and their neural tangent features are strategies. Using evolutionary game theory and stochastic differential equations, they analyze the stochastically stable states induced by gradient descent (GD) versus stochastic gradient descent (SGD), and study the roles of data noise and optimization noise in steering dynamics toward either core or shortcut subnetworks.

Result: The analysis shows two different stochastically stable states: under full-batch GD, training predominantly optimizes/relies on the shortcut subnetwork, while under SGD, the dynamics favor optimization of the core subnetwork. The continuous-time SDE framework further characterizes how data noise and optimization noise modulate convergence to these states, clarifying conditions under which shortcut bias emerges or is reduced.

Conclusion: The work provides a theoretical framework, via evolutionary game theory and neural tangent features, that explains the formation of shortcut bias in deep learning. It demonstrates that different optimization schemes (GD vs SGD) inherently bias training toward shortcut or core features and that noise plays a critical role in this process, offering insights for designing training procedures that mitigate shortcut learning.

Abstract: Shortcut learning causes deep learning models to rely on non-essential features within the data. However, its formation in deep neural network training still lacks theoretical understanding. In this paper, we provide a formal definition of core and shortcut features and employ evolutionary game theory to analyze the origins of shortcut bias by modeling data samples as players and their corresponding neural tangent features as strategies, assuming the existence of core and shortcut subnetworks. We find that gradient descent (GD) and stochastic gradient descent (SGD) lead to two distinct stochastically stable states, each corresponding to a different strategy. The former primarily optimizes the shortcut subnetwork, while the latter primarily optimizes the core subnetwork. We investigate the influence of these strategies on shortcut bias through a continuous stochastic differential equation, and reveal the impact of data noise and optimization noise on the formation of shortcut bias. In brief, our work employs evolutionary game theory to characterize the dynamics of shortcut bias formation and provides a theoretical view on its mitigation.

The authors position this open-source benchmark as a resource to drive progress in developing more capable, reliable academic AI agents within the OpenClaw ecosystem.

Abstract: Benchmarks within the OpenClaw ecosystem have thus far evaluated exclusively assistant-level tasks, leaving the academic-level capabilities of OpenClaw largely unexamined. We introduce AcademiClaw, a bilingual benchmark of 80 complex, long-horizon tasks sourced directly from university students' real academic workflows -- homework, research projects, competitions, and personal projects -- that they found current AI agents unable to solve effectively. Curated from 230 student-submitted candidates through rigorous expert review, the final task set spans 25+ professional domains, ranging from olympiad-level mathematics and linguistics problems to GPU-intensive reinforcement learning and full-stack system debugging, with 16 tasks requiring CUDA GPU execution. Each task executes in an isolated Docker sandbox and is scored on task completion by multi-dimensional rubrics combining six complementary techniques, with an independent five-category safety audit providing additional behavioral analysis. Experiments on six frontier models show that even the best achieves only a 55\% pass rate. Further analysis uncovers sharp capability boundaries across task domains, divergent behavioral strategies among models, and a disconnect between token consumption and output quality, providing fine-grained diagnostic signals beyond what aggregate metrics reveal. We hope that AcademiClaw and its open-sourced data and code can serve as a useful resource for the OpenClaw community, driving progress toward agents that are more capable and versatile across the full breadth of real-world academic demands. All data and code are available at https://github.com/GAIR-NLP/AcademiClaw.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Drug-induced liver injury is a major cause of clinical trial failures, and current computational predictors are limited by treating the problem as a binary classification, offering little mechanistic insight to guide translational decisions.

Method: The authors create the DILER Benchmark by augmenting curated molecules with mechanistic hepatotoxicity hypotheses from the biomedical literature, and propose HADES, an agent-based explainable system that integrates molecular prediction, metabolite decomposition, structural analysis, and toxicity pathway evidence to assess DILI risk with transparent reasoning traces.

Conclusion: Recasting DILI prediction as mechanistic hypothesis generation is both feasible and beneficial: the new DILER benchmark and the HADES system advance performance over existing tools while exposing the complexity of mechanistic reasoning and the need for more powerful explainable methods in predictive toxicology.

Abstract: Drug-induced liver injury (DILI) remains a leading cause of late-stage clinical trial attrition. However, existing computational predictors primarily rely on binary classification, a framing that limits generalization and yields no mechanistic insight to guide translational decisions. We argue that DILI prediction is better posed as an explainable hypothesis-generation problem.
  To support this shift, we introduce the DILER Benchmark, a dataset that extends beyond binary labels by augmenting a curated set of molecules with mechanistic hepatotoxicity hypotheses derived from biomedical literature. We further present HADES, an agentic system designed to generate transparent and auditable reasoning traces. By combining molecular-level predictions, metabolite decomposition, structural understanding, and toxicity pathway evidence, HADES mechanistically assesses DILI risk.
  Evaluated on the DILER Benchmark, HADES outperforms existing models in binary classification, achieving a ROC-AUC of 0.68 on the Test Set and 0.59 on the challenging Post-2021 Set, compared with 0.63 and 0.50 for DILI-Predictor, respectively. More importantly, we establish a baseline for mechanistic hypothesis generation, where HADES achieves a Hypothesis Alignment Fuzzy Jaccard Index of 0.16. This result underscores the inherent complexity of the task while highlighting the need for advanced explainable approaches in predictive toxicology.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing conversational affect benchmarks are mostly speaker-centric and do not adequately model coupled, time-evolving interpersonal processes in dyadic conversations, such as directional influence, timing coordination, and rapport development. There is a need for a standardized benchmark that focuses on these interpersonal and temporal dynamics.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark and challenge based on the Hume-DaiKon dataset of 945 naturalistic dyadic audiovisual conversations in five languages. They define three coordinated sub-challenges: (1) directional interpersonal influence prediction, (2) turn-taking prediction (next-speaker and time-to-next-speech), and (3) rapport trajectory prediction. They provide fixed data splits, standardized metrics (CCC, Pearson, Macro-F1, MAE), and baseline multimodal models to support temporal reasoning and cross-context generalization.

Result: Baseline systems achieve up to 0.40 CCC and 0.50 Pearson on influence prediction, 0.66 Macro-F1 and 1.50s MAE on turn-taking, and 0.68 CCC and 0.70 Pearson on rapport trajectory prediction. These results show that current methods can capture coarse dyadic patterns but struggle with more fine-grained directional dependence and long-horizon interpersonal dynamics.

Conclusion: ACII-DaiKon offers a large-scale, multimodal, multilingual benchmark and challenge for dyadic interaction modeling, demonstrating both the feasibility and difficulty of interpersonal affect and social dynamics prediction. The workshop aims to foster rigorous, comparable research and cross-disciplinary dialogue on data validity, evaluation, and culturally aware approaches to dyadic conversation modeling.

Abstract: The 2026 ACII Dyadic Conversations (ACII-DaiKon) Workshop & Challenge introduces a benchmark for modeling interpersonal affect and social dynamics in dyadic conversations. Although conversational affect modeling has advanced rapidly, most benchmarks remain speaker-centric and underrepresent coupled, time-evolving processes between partners, including directional influence, conversational timing coordination, and rapport development. To address this gap, ACII-DaiKon presents three coordinated sub-challenges built on a shared dataset: (1) directional interpersonal influence prediction, (2) turn-taking prediction (next-speaker and time-to-next-speech), and (3) rapport trajectory prediction across full interactions.
  The challenge is built on the Hume-DaiKon dataset, comprising 945 dyadic conversations (743.4 hours of audiovisual data) collected under naturalistic conditions across five languages. The benchmark supports multimodal modeling, temporal reasoning, and cross-context generalization through fixed train/validation/test splits, standardized metrics, and released baseline systems. Evaluation uses Concordance Correlation Coefficient (CCC), Pearson correlation, Macro-F1, and Mean Absolute Error (MAE) depending on the sub-challenge.
  Baseline experiments establish initial reference performance, with best test results of 0.40 CCC and 0.50 Pearson for influence prediction, 0.66 Macro-F1 and 1.50~s MAE for turn-taking, and 0.68 CCC and 0.70 Pearson for rapport trajectory modeling. These results indicate that while current methods capture coarse dyadic patterns, robust modeling of directional dependence and long-horizon interpersonal dynamics remains challenging. The workshop provides a shared platform for rigorous comparison and cross-disciplinary discussion on data validity, evaluation protocols, and culturally aware modeling for dyadic interaction.

### [205] [Hybrid Inspection and Task-Based Access Control in Zero-Trust Agentic AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02682)
*Majed El Helou,Benjamin Ryder,Chiara Troiani,Jean Diaconu,Hervé Muyal,Marcelo Yannuzzi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CASA, a continuous semantic authorization framework for LLM-driven agents, combining deterministic and semantic controls to ensure tool use aligns with a user’s intended tasks in multi-turn interactions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-driven agents can autonomously invoke tools and access protected resources, which creates security risks, particularly in multi-turn and collaborative scenarios. Existing delegated authorization mechanisms lack visibility into the original user’s intent, allowing compromised or malicious agents to tamper with tool calls, falsify results, or escalate privileges without detection. The authors aim to provide a more fine-grained, intent-aware way to authorize and monitor tool usage over the course of ongoing conversations.

Method: The authors propose Continuous Agent Semantic Authorization (CASA) built around a zero-trust interception layer. CASA enforces five deterministic controls for message and data integrity and adds a semantic inspection layer to check whether tool calls match the authorized tasks. Unlike prior Task-Based Access Control approaches that assume single-turn interactions, the semantic layer is split into two stages: (i) task extraction at the interception layer to infer the user’s objectives from multi-turn dialogue, and (ii) task–tool semantic matching at the authorization server to decide if a tool request is appropriate. They also extend the ASTRA dataset by creating new multi-turn conversation–tool datasets with both relevant and irrelevant tool invocations to support training and evaluation.

Result: The work yields: (1) a hybrid runtime enforcement model combining deterministic and semantic controls for agent tool use; (2) a two-stage semantic TBAC process that works over multi-turn conversations; (3) an extended ASTRA dataset containing multi-turn interactions and labeled relevant/irrelevant tool calls; and (4) the first experimental evaluation results of Task-Based Access Control in multi-turn conversational scenarios.

Conclusion: CASA demonstrates that combining deterministic controls with multi-turn semantic authorization can more safely govern tool use by LLM-driven agents. By explicitly extracting user tasks from conversation history and validating tool calls against those tasks, the approach addresses key blind spots in existing delegated authorization systems and provides an empirical foundation and dataset for future research on secure, task-aware agent authorization in complex, ongoing interactions.

Abstract: Authorizing Large Language Model (LLM)-driven agents to dynamically invoke tools and access protected resources introduces significant security risks, and the risks grow dramatically as agents engage in multi-turn conversations and scale toward distributed collaboration. A compromised or malicious agentic application can tamper with tool calls, falsify results, or request permissions beyond the scope of the subject's intended tasks, which could go unnoticed with current delegated authorization flows given their lack of visibility into the original subject's intent. In light of this, we make the following contributions towards Continuous Agent Semantic Authorization (CASA). First, we propose a hybrid runtime enforcement model that combines deterministic and semantic controls enabled by a zero-trust interception layer. Five deterministic controls enforce structural and data-integrity guarantees over the message flow, while a semantic inspection layer evaluates whether tool call choices align with the intended tasks commissioned to the agent. Second, differently from prior Task-Based Access Control (TBAC) techniques that operate on single-turn interactions, we decompose the semantic layer into two stages: i) a task-extraction step that distills the subject's objectives from multi-turn conversations at the interception layer, and ii) a task-tool semantic matching step at the authorization server that evaluates whether the requested tools are appropriate for the extracted tasks. Third, we extend the ASTRA dataset that we introduced in a prior work, by generating novel conversation-tool datasets with multi-turn interactions containing relevant and irrelevant tool calls for a given task. Lastly, we provide the first experimental results for TBAC under multi-turn conversations.

*Gelei Xu,Ningzhi Tang,Xueyang Li,Toby Jia-Jun Li,Zhi Zheng,Wei Jin,Yiyu Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically studies 'healthcare agent skills'—modular, reusable procedures for AI agents—by analyzing 557 healthcare-related skills from a public repository, showing current skills focus on patient-facing workflows and monitoring, have uneven lifecycle and clinical-input coverage, and that generic technical risk does not align well with clinical risk.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agent capabilities in healthcare are difficult to transfer across institutions because they are tightly coupled to local workflows, constraints, and infrastructure. A new abstraction, 'agent skills', packages reusable procedures that agents can call, but it is unclear how these skills are currently used, what functions they serve, and how safe or clinically relevant they are. There is also a gap between existing healthcare-agent research, benchmarks, and risk frameworks and this emerging ecosystem of skills deployed in practice.

Method: The authors collected 58,159 public skills from ClawHub, a skill repository, and filtered out 557 that are healthcare-related. They then annotated these healthcare skills along ten dimensions capturing aspects such as functional role (e.g., patient-facing workflow vs. clinical decision support), deployment context, level of autonomy, kinds of inputs and outputs, and risk characteristics. Using this annotated dataset, they conducted empirical analyses to characterize the distribution of use cases, lifecycle coverage, reliance on clinical vs. generic inputs, and relationships between technical and clinical risk.

Result: The analysis shows: (1) Most public healthcare skills concentrate on automating patient-facing workflows and monitoring activities rather than diagnostic or treatment decision-making. (2) The coverage of the full healthcare lifecycle (from triage to follow-up) is patchy, with some stages and specialized clinical inputs underrepresented. (3) Measures of general technical risk (e.g., generic safety or robustness assessments) do not reliably reflect actual clinical risk, indicating a misalignment between existing technical risk metrics and domain-specific safety concerns in healthcare.

Conclusion: Healthcare agent skills constitute an emerging procedural layer that mediates how AI agents act in real-world healthcare settings, but this layer is not adequately addressed by current healthcare agent benchmarks or risk frameworks. To build safer and more transferable healthcare AI systems, future work needs benchmarks and risk models that explicitly consider the structure, functions, and clinical specificities of these skills, rather than relying solely on high-level agent capabilities or generic technical risk measures.

Abstract: Healthcare automation is shaped by local procedures and organizational constraints, so agent capabilities rarely transfer unchanged across settings. Agent skills, self-contained directories that package reusable procedures for AI agents, are emerging as a procedural layer for adapting healthcare agents across diverse healthcare settings. We present the first empirical analysis of healthcare agent skills, drawing on 557 healthcare-related skills filtered from 58,159 public skills on ClawHub and annotated along ten dimensions covering function, deployment context, autonomy, and safety. We find that public healthcare skills emphasize patient-facing workflow automation and monitoring rather than the diagnostic and treatment-oriented tasks foregrounded in healthcare-agent research; coverage of the healthcare lifecycle and specialized clinical inputs remains uneven; and general technical risk does not reliably capture clinical risk. These findings position healthcare skills as a procedural layer not yet addressed by current benchmarks and risk frameworks.

### [207] [ORPilot: A Production-Oriented Agentic LLM-for-OR Tool for Optimization Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02728)
*Guangrui Xie*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ORPilot is an open-source agentic AI system that converts messy, real-world business problems and raw data into solver-ready optimization models through conversational elicitation, automated data handling, parameter computation, and a solver-agnostic intermediate representation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing LLM-for-operations-research tools assume clean, well-structured, textbook-style problem statements with inline data, which is unrealistic in production. Real business problems involve ambiguous descriptions, decentralized or messy data sources, and heterogeneous solver ecosystems. There is a need for a system that can robustly handle these conditions end-to-end and integrate with multiple OR solvers without being locked to a specific backend.

Method: The authors design an agentic AI architecture, ORPilot, with four main components: (1) a conversational interview agent that interactively clarifies and completes the problem specification from users; (2) a data collection agent that autonomously locates and retrieves relevant operational data rather than relying solely on user prompts; (3) a parameter computation agent that transforms raw tabular data into the structured parameters required by optimization models; and (4) a solver-agnostic Intermediate Representation (IR) that can be deterministically recompiled, without further LLM calls, into models for multiple solvers (Gurobi, CPLEX, PuLP, Pyomo, OR-Tools). The system also employs self-correcting retry loops, using solver tracebacks to automatically diagnose and repair modeling or compilation errors.

Result: On real-world business optimization problems, ORPilot shows promising performance, successfully constructing and solving models under realistic production conditions. When evaluated on standard academic benchmarks (IndustryOR, NL4OPT, NLP4LP), ORPilot achieves higher accuracy than state-of-the-art baselines on the IndustryOR benchmark and matches their performance on NL4OPT and NLP4LP, demonstrating that the system generalizes well to both practical and textbook-style problems.

Conclusion: ORPilot is presented as the first LLM-driven OR system explicitly aimed at production-grade business problems rather than idealized textbook cases. Its multi-agent design, automated data and parameter handling, and solver-agnostic IR make it a practical bridge between unstructured business descriptions, raw enterprise data, and formal optimization models. The evaluation suggests it can meet real-world requirements while remaining competitive on academic benchmarks, positioning ORPilot as a step toward deployable, end-to-end AI-assisted optimization modeling systems.

Abstract: This paper presents ORPilot, an open-source agentic AI system that translates real-world business problems into solver-ready optimization models. Unlike academic LLM-for-OR tools that assume clean problem specifications with preformatted inline data, ORPilot is designed for production conditions: ambiguous descriptions, large-scale raw operational data, and the need for portability across solver backends. The system introduces four novel components: (1) a conversational interview agent to elicit complete problem specifications, (2) a data collection agent that retrieves data independently of prompts, (3) a parameter computation agent to bridge raw tabular data and model-ready parameters, and (4) a solver-agnostic Intermediate Representation (IR) for deterministic, zero-LLM-call recompilation to Gurobi, CPLEX, PuLP, Pyomo, or OR-Tools solvers. Additionally, self-correcting retry loops utilize solver tracebacks for targeted repairs. ORPilot represents the first attempt to target production-level business problems rather than textbook operations research (OR) cases. Evaluation on real-world problems demonstrates promising results. When tested against traditional academic benchmarks: IndustryOR, NL4OPT and NLP4LP, ORPilot outperformed state-of-the-art tools in accuracy on the IndustryOR benchmark and delivered comparable performance on NL4OPT and NLP4LP.

### [208] [Coherent Hierarchical Multi-Label Learning to Defer for Medical Imaging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02734)
*Joshua Strong,Pramit Saha,Emma Sun,Helen Higham,Alison Noble*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper extends Learning to Defer (L2D) to hierarchical multi-label settings and proposes methods to ensure coherent deferral decisions in taxonomic label spaces, particularly for medical imaging.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior L2D work assumes flat label spaces and treats deferral as an independent per-label decision. In hierarchical taxonomies (like medical imaging findings), this leads to incoherent delegations, such as contradictions in the label hierarchy or deferring decisions already implied by the model’s own predictions. The authors are motivated by realistic medical workflows where predictions must respect clinical taxonomies and deferral is a structured delegation, not just another label.

Method: 1) Formally define coherent hierarchical deferral under a Selective-Exclusion handoff contract, specifying what it means for a joint set of model predictions and deferrals to be coherent in a taxonomy. 2) Derive the Bayes-optimal coherent deferral rule and show that locally optimal (nodewise Bayes) L2D can be globally action-incoherent. 3) Propose two practical remedies: (a) Exact coherent projection: a dynamic-programming-based decoder that projects arbitrary deferral decisions onto the closest coherent action in the allowed set; (b) Taxonomic Belief Propagation (TBP) with Recursive Policy Optimisation (RPO): a contract-aware joint action model that learns to make coherent hierarchical decisions via the same recursive structure used at inference time.

Result: Experiments on medical-imaging benchmarks with real human readers and synthetic experts show that naive binary-relevance L2D yields significant deferral incoherence (taxonomic contradictions, delegation violations, redundant deferral). Exact coherent projection fully eliminates incoherence by construction without sacrificing much performance. TBP+RPO is computationally efficient and reduces incoherence to near zero while maintaining strong task utility and competitive performance relative to baselines.

Conclusion: Hierarchical multi-label settings require deferral strategies that respect the underlying taxonomy and handoff contract; naive per-label L2D is insufficient and can be incoherent. By formalising coherent hierarchical deferral and introducing projection and TBP+RPO, the paper provides practical algorithms that yield nearly fully coherent deferral policies with high utility in medical-imaging workflows, suggesting these methods are better aligned with real-world expert–AI collaboration requirements.

Abstract: Learning to Defer (L2D) enables a model to predict autonomously or defer to an expert, but prior work largely assumes flat label spaces. We study the first L2D setting with hierarchical multi-label decisions, motivated by medical-imaging workflows in which findings are organised by clinical taxonomies. In this setting, deferral is a delegation action rather than a label assignment, so treating it as an independent per-label decision can produce deferral incoherence, including taxonomic contradictions, delegation violations, and deferrals of labels already implied by the model's own assertions. We formalise coherent hierarchical deferral under a Selective-Exclusion handoff contract, characterise the Bayes-optimal coherent deferral rule, and show that even nodewise Bayes L2D can be action-incoherent. We then propose two remedies: exact coherent projection, a dynamic-programming decoder over the coherent action set, and Taxonomic Belief Propagation (TBP) with Recursive Policy Optimisation (RPO), a contract-aware joint action model trained through the same recursion used at inference. Across real-reader and controlled-expert medical-imaging benchmarks, naive binary-relevance L2D exhibits non-trivial incoherence. Projection removes it exactly, and fast TBP+RPO drives incoherence near zero while retaining strong utility.

</details>


### [209] [Foundation Models to Unlock Real-World Evidence from Nationwide Medical Claims](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02740)

Method: The authors develop ReClaim, a generative transformer model trained from scratch on 43.8 billion medical events from more than 200 million MarketScan enrollees (2008–2022). ReClaim jointly models longitudinal trajectories of diagnoses, procedures, medications, and expenditures. They scale the model to three sizes (140M, 700M, 1.7B parameters), pre-train it on claims sequences, and then perform post-training (task-specific adaptation). Performance consistently improves with model scale, and task-specific post-training adds 13.8 AUC points over pre-training alone. For expenditure forecasting, ReClaim increases explained variance from 0.28 (LightGBM) to 0.37. In a target trial emulation setting, ReClaim reduces systematic bias by 72% on average relative to Delphi. These gains hold in retrospective, prospective, and external validation on two independent datasets.

Conclusion: Administrative claims data can successfully support large healthcare foundation models whose learned representations generalize across time, tasks, and data sources. ReClaim demonstrates that such a model can substantially improve disease-onset prediction (especially for rare diseases), expenditure forecasting, and RWE/causal inference tasks such as target trial emulation. This positions claims-based foundation models as a powerful and scalable tool for disease surveillance, cost prediction, and robust real-world evidence generation.

Abstract: Evidence derived from large-scale real-world data (RWD) is increasingly informing regulatory evaluation and healthcare decision-making. Administrative claims provide population-scale, longitudinal records of healthcare utilization, expenditure, and detailed coding of diagnoses, procedures, and medications, yet their potential as a substrate for healthcare foundation models remains largely unexplored. Here we present ReClaim, a generative transformer trained from scratch on 43.8 billion medical events from more than 200 million enrollees in the MarketScan claims data spanning 2008-2022. ReClaim models longitudinal trajectories across diagnoses, procedures, medications, and expenditure, and was scaled to 140 million, 700 million, and 1.7 billion parameters. Across over 1,000 disease-onset prediction tasks, ReClaim achieved a mean AUC of 75.6%, substantially outperforming disease-specific LightGBM (66.3%) and the transformer-based Delphi model (69.4%), with the largest gains for rare diseases. These advantages held across retrospective and prospective evaluations and in external validation on two independent datasets. Performance improved monotonically with scale, and post-training added 13.8 percentage points over pre-training alone. Beyond disease prediction, ReClaim captured financial outcomes and improved real-world evidence (RWE) analyses: for healthcare expenditure forecasting it increased explained variance from 0.28 to 0.37 relative to LightGBM, and in a target trial emulation it reduced systematic bias by 72% on average relative to Delphi. Together, these results establish administrative claims as a scalable substrate for healthcare foundation models and show that learned representations generalize across time periods and data sources, supporting disease surveillance, expenditure forecasting, and RWE generation.

### [210] [AI and Open-data Driven Scalable Solar Power Profiling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02738)
*Shiliang Zhang,Sabita Maharjan,Damla Turgut*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build an open, scalable AI-based framework and API that detect rooftop solar panels from open satellite/aerial images, convert them to georeferenced polygons, and use weather data to generate regional solar power profiles for planning and analysis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Rooftop solar PV is growing quickly, but there is a lack of detailed, current, and openly accessible data on where panels are located and what their potential or actual generation is. Existing approaches often rely on proprietary imagery, manual labeling, or closed-source models, which limit scalability, transparency, and reproducibility. The authors aim to create a method and dataset that overcome these barriers and can be easily extended across cities and regions.

Method: They use foundation vision AI models (large pre-trained vision models) to automatically detect solar panel geometries directly from open-source satellite or aerial imagery. Detected panels are turned into georeferenced polygons to form spatially explicit inventories. They then integrate these inventories with open weather data to estimate solar power profiles at regional or city scales. Finally, they expose the workflow through an API: for any queried building/location, the system fetches imagery, runs the detection model, and returns rooftop solar polygons and associated production profiles.

Result: The result is an open, transparent, and scalable framework, data products, and an API that: (1) can detect rooftop solar panels without manual labeling or custom per-city model training, (2) produces incrementally extensible, georeferenced solar panel inventories, and (3) derives city- or region-level solar power profiles using open weather data. They release both the generated data and a public API for user-specified locations.

Conclusion: The framework demonstrates that combining foundation vision models, open satellite imagery, and open weather data can effectively generate up-to-date rooftop solar inventories and regional solar power profiles at scale. By reducing reliance on proprietary data and closed tools, it enables researchers, planners, and utilities to build solar maps, study distributed solar integration, optimize local power flows, design tariffs, and plan infrastructure in a more transparent and reproducible way.

Abstract: Solar photovoltaic (PV) deployment is expanding rapidly, yet detailed, up-to-date information on the spatial distribution and capacity of rooftop PV remains limited. This paper presents an open, scalable framework for detecting solar panels from open data and generating city-level solar power profiles. This avoids manual data labeling and case-specific model training while maintaining robustness across heterogeneous imagery. Detected solar panels are converted into georeferenced polygons, yielding spatially explicit and incrementally extensible inventories. By integrating open weather data, we translate panel footprints into regional solar power profiles. The framework reduces dependency on proprietary imagery, manual labeling, and closed-source models, and offers a transparent and scalable approach for solar planning and analysis. We released the data and an API resulted from this work. For any user-specified building location, our API retrieves aerial imagery, detects rooftop solar panels, and returns georeferenced polygons. This empowers researchers and developers to scan user-defined areas to build solar panel maps and associated solar production profiles, thus facilitating advanced analysis like distributed solar production integration, local power flow optimization, energy tariff design, and infrastructure planning.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors run multi-turn conversational social dilemma games involving multiple language models. They experimentally manipulate other agents' behavior by steering some LMs to act maliciously and measure how this affects the focal LM's subsequent behavior, especially its pro- or anti-social orientation after gameplay. They then compare different steering strategies to mitigate misalignment contagion, including simple reinforcement of the system prompt versus a new method, steering with implicit traits, which intermittently injects system prompts that restate and reinforce the model's initial pro-social traits. The study is conducted in a black-box setting without access to model parameters or internal states.

Result: The experiments show that language models tend to become more anti-social after participating in the social dilemma games, demonstrating misalignment contagion. This effect becomes stronger when other LMs are explicitly steered to behave maliciously. Attempts to mitigate the issue by repeatedly reinforcing the system prompt are often ineffective and can even exacerbate misalignment. In contrast, the proposed steering with implicit traits approach more effectively preserves the LM's initial pro-social behaviors during multi-agent interactions.

Conclusion: Misalignment contagion is a real and measurable phenomenon in multi-agent LM interactions, especially in social dilemma settings. Standard system-prompt reinforcement is not a reliable defense and may worsen the problem. Steering with implicit traits, implemented via intermittent prompts that reaffirm the model's desired characteristics, offers a more robust, parameter-free mitigation strategy suitable for black-box models in complex, multi-agent workflows.

Abstract: Language models (LMs) are increasingly used in high-stakes, multi-agent settings, where following instructions and maintaining value alignment are critical. Most alignment research focuses on interactions between a single LM and a single user, failing to address the risk of misaligned behavior spreading between multiple LMs in multi-turn interactions. We find evidence of this phenomenon, which we call misalignment contagion, across multiple LMs as they engage multi-turn conversational social dilemma games. Specifically, we find that LMs become more anti-social after gameplay and that this effect is intensified when other players are steered to act maliciously. We explore different steering techniques to mitigate such misalignment contagion and find that reinforcing an LM's system prompt is insufficient and often harmful. Instead, we propose steering with implicit traits: a technique that intermittently injects system prompts with statements that reinforce an LMs initial traits and is more effective than system prompt repetition at keeping models in line with their initial pro-social behaviors. Importantly, this method does not require access to model parameters or internal model states, making it suitable for increasingly common use cases where complex multi-agent workflows are being designed with black box models.

Our framework uses adaptive filtering in the Fourier, graph Fourier, and wavelet domains, enabling effective multi-sensor fusion and context correlation. ### [217] [SCPRM: A Schema-aware Cumulative Process Reward Model for Knowledge Graph Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02819)
*Jiujiu Chen,Yazheng Liu,Sihong Xie,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new process reward model to better evaluate intermediate reasoning steps of LLMs on knowledge graphs, reducing the problem where wrong steps are masked by later correct ones, and uses it with MCTS to improve multi-hop KG question answering, especially in risk-sensitive domains like medicine and law.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing process reward models score reasoning step-by-step, but they often show a risk compensation effect: an early incorrect step can be compensated by later correct steps, leading to high rewards for globally flawed reasoning paths. This is particularly harmful in knowledge graph reasoning, where multiple paths can connect two entities and a single risky step can invalidate the entire reasoning chain, which is unacceptable in high-stakes applications such as medical and legal question answering over KGs. The authors want a way to evaluate reasoning that is sensitive to both path correctness and future risk, not just local step quality.

Method: They introduce a Schema-aware Cumulative Process Reward Model (SCPRM). SCPRM evaluates each reasoning step conditioned on the prefix of the reasoning path so far and incorporates a schema distance term between the current step and an implicit target derived from the query. This design allows it to estimate cumulative and future rewards, penalizing steps that move away from the target in the KG schema even if later steps might try to recover. They then plug SCPRM into a Monte Carlo Tree Search framework (SCPRM-MCTS) to guide exploration in multi-hop reasoning over knowledge graphs for question answering tasks, using SCPRM’s risk-sensitive scores as the reward signal in the tree search.

Result: On medical and legal knowledge graph QA benchmarks and the CWQ dataset, SCPRM-MCTS achieves an average improvement of 1.18 percentage points in Hits@k over strong baseline methods. This indicates that SCPRM provides more accurate and risk-aware evaluation of reasoning paths, leading to better question answering performance in multi-hop KG reasoning.

Conclusion: By making process rewards schema-aware and cumulative with respect to both current and future steps, SCPRM reduces the risk compensation problem and yields more faithful evaluations of reasoning paths. When combined with MCTS, this leads to more reliable and accurate multi-hop KG reasoning, particularly beneficial for risk-sensitive domains such as medical and legal QA, as evidenced by consistent gains in Hits@k over strong baselines.

Abstract: Large language models excel at complex reasoning, yet evaluating their intermediate steps remains challenging. Although process reward models provide step-wise supervision, they often suffer from a risk compensation effect, where incorrect steps are offset by later correct ones, assigning high rewards to flawed reasoning paths. This issue is further exacerbated in knowledge graph (KG) reasoning, as there may exist multiple paths between the start and end entities in the KGs, and a risky step can make the reasoning path flawed. Those limitations are problematic in risk-sensitive tasks such as medical and legal KG reasoning. To address the issues, we propose a Schema-aware Cumulative Process Reward Model (SCPRM) that evaluates reasoning paths by conditioning on the reasoning prefix , and incorporating schema distance between current reasoning step and the implicit target parsed from the query, which provides cumulative and future rewards to guide the path explorations. We further integrate SCPRM into Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) as SCPRM-MCTS to conduct multi-hop reasoning on KGs for question answering (QA) tasks. Across medical and legal KGQA and CWQ, SCPRM-MCTS improves the performance of Hits@k by an average of 1.18% over strong baselines, demonstrating more accurate and risk-sensitive reasoning evaluation.

</details>


### [218] [First-Order Efficiency for Probabilistic Value Estimation via A Statistical Viewpoint](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02827)
*Ziqi Liu,Kiljae Lee,Yuan Zhang,Weijing Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes Monte Carlo estimators for probabilistic values (like Shapley values) and shows they share a common first-order error form, then designs an estimator (EASE) that optimizes this structure to reduce mean squared error.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Computing probabilistic values such as Shapley values in modern ML is computationally expensive because it requires utility evaluations over exponentially many coalitions. Practitioners therefore rely on Monte Carlo approximations, but existing estimators are developed in an ad hoc manner using different statistical techniques without a unified efficiency analysis. There is a need for a principled way to compare and improve these estimators, especially for large-scale, black-box models in explainable AI and data valuation.

Method: The authors study a broad family of Monte Carlo estimators for probabilistic values, including approaches based on weighted averages, self-normalized importance sampling, regression adjustment, and weighted least squares. They show these share a common first-order asymptotic error structure, where the leading error term can be written as an augmented inverse-probability weighted influence term that depends on the sampling distribution over coalitions and a chosen surrogate (working) function. Using this representation, they derive a closed-form expression for the leading-order mean squared error (MSE) as a function of the sampling law and the surrogate. Based on this criterion, they design the Efficiency-Aware Surrogate-adjusted Estimator (EASE), which jointly optimizes the sampling law and surrogate to minimize the first-order MSE.

Result: They obtain an explicit first-order MSE formula that unifies the analysis of several existing estimators. This shows precisely how the sampling distribution and surrogate choice affect efficiency. Using this understanding, they construct EASE, which selects both components to minimize the leading MSE term. Empirical studies across multiple types of probabilistic values demonstrate that EASE yields lower estimation error than current state-of-the-art Monte Carlo estimators.

Conclusion: Many popular Monte Carlo estimators for probabilistic values are instances of a common first-order error structure. This allows a principled characterization of their statistical efficiency via an explicit leading-order MSE expression. Leveraging this, the proposed EASE estimator systematically chooses sampling and surrogate components to improve efficiency, and empirically dominates existing methods across various probabilistic value estimation tasks.

Abstract: Probabilistic values, including Shapley values and semivalues, provide a model-agnostic framework to attribute the behavior of a black-box model to data points or features, with a wide range of applications including explainable artificial intelligence and data valuation. However, their exact computation requires utility evaluations over exponentially many coalitions, making Monte Carlo approximation essential in modern machine learning applications. Existing estimators are often developed through different identification strategies, including weighted averages, self-normalized weighting, regression adjustment, and weighted least squares. Our key observation is that these seemingly distinct constructions share a common first-order error structure, in which the leading term is an augmented inverse-probability weighted influence term determined by the sampling law and a working surrogate function. This first-order representation yields an explicit expression for the leading mean squared error (MSE), which characterizes how the sampling law and the surrogate jointly determine statistical efficiency. Guided by this criterion, we propose an Efficiency-Aware Surrogate-adjusted Estimator (EASE) that directly chooses the sampling law and surrogate to minimize the first-order MSE. We demonstrate that EASE consistently outperforms state-of-the-art estimators for various probabilistic values.

### [219] [Compress Then Adapt? No, Do It Together via Task-aware Union of Subspaces](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02829)
*Jingze Ge,Yun Liu,Xue Geng,Wanqi Dong,Wang Zhe Mark,Min Wu,Xulei Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes JACTUS, a unified framework that jointly performs compression and parameter-efficient fine-tuning of large pretrained models to avoid subspace misalignment and better use a global parameter budget.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing workflows compress large pretrained models and then perform parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) in sequence. This decoupling can misalign the compressed representation with downstream task objectives and waste parameters since the compression subspace is chosen without task-awareness and the fine-tuning adapters operate on top of possibly suboptimal compressed weights.

Method: JACTUS (Joint Adaptation and Compression with a Task-aware Union of Subspaces) uses a small calibration set to estimate input and pre-activation gradient covariance matrices, constructs their orthogonal union with the pretrained weight subspace, and then performs projected low-rank approximation within this union. It allocates rank globally across layers based on marginal gain per parameter and trains only a compact core matrix in this task-aware low-rank subspace, yielding a PEFT-style adaptation that is intrinsically coupled with compression.

Result: On ViT-Base across eight vision datasets, JACTUS reaches an average 89.2% accuracy while retaining only 80% of parameters, outperforming strong PEFT baselines that use 100% of parameters (e.g., DoRA at 87.9%). On Llama2-7B for commonsense QA, it achieves 80.9% average performance at 80% retained parameters, again surpassing 100% PEFT baselines (DoRA at 79.7%) and previous compress-then-finetune approaches under the same retained-parameter budget.

Conclusion: Jointly coupling compression and adaptation in a task-aware low-rank subspace leads to more parameter-efficient tuning of large pretrained models than standard sequential compress-then-PEFT pipelines, enabling deployable low-rank models that avoid storing full frozen weights while retaining or improving downstream performance.

Abstract: Adapting large pretrained models to diverse tasks is now routine, yet the two dominant strategies of parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) and low-rank compression are typically composed in sequence. This decoupled practice first compresses and then fine-tunes adapters, potentially misaligning the compressed subspace with downstream objectives and squandering a global parameter budget. To overcome this limitation, we introduce JACTUS (Joint Adaptation and Compression with a Task-aware Union of Subspaces), a single framework that unifies compression and adaptation. From a small calibration set, JACTUS estimates input and pre-activation gradient covariances, forms their orthogonal union with the pretrained weight subspace, performs a projected low-rank approximation inside this union, allocates rank globally by marginal gain per parameter, and trains only a compact core matrix. This explicitly mitigates the potential misalignment between the compressed subspace and downstream objectives by coupling the directions preserved for compression with those required for adaptation, yielding a deployable low-rank model that avoids retaining full frozen weights while enabling fast and robust tuning. On vision, JACTUS attains an average 89.2% accuracy on ViT-Base across eight datasets at 80% retained parameters, surpassing strong 100% PEFT baselines (e.g., DoRA 87.9%). On language, JACTUS achieves an 80.9% average on Llama2-7B commonsense QA at the same 80% retained-parameter budget, outperforming 100% PEFT (e.g., DoRA 79.7%) and exceeding prior compress-then-finetune pipelines under the same ratained-parameter budget. We will release code.

### [220] [HAAS: A Policy-Aware Framework for Adaptive Task Allocation Between Humans and Artificial Intelligence Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02832)
*Vicente Pelechanoa,Antoni Mestre,Manoli Albert,Miriam Gil*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces HAAS, a framework that adaptively allocates tasks between humans and AI using governance rules plus learning, and shows how different governance levels affect performance, fatigue, and collaboration quality in software engineering and manufacturing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current work allocation between humans and AI is often treated as a binary choice (human vs AI), ignoring the richer reality where tasks can be shared, supervised, or allocated differently depending on context, risk, and human factors like fatigue. Organisations lack principled, auditable ways to design and compare such collaboration patterns before committing them in practice. The authors aim to provide a structured, experiment-ready framework to govern and learn optimal human–AI task distributions under different constraints and objectives.

Method: The authors propose Human-AI Adaptive Symbiosis (HAAS), which integrates: (1) a rule-based expert system that encodes governance constraints to filter and control which collaboration modes are allowed for a given task and context; and (2) a contextual bandit learner that, given the feasible collaboration modes, learns from outcome feedback to choose among them. They represent task-agent fit along five cognitive dimensions and define a five-level autonomy spectrum from human-only to fully autonomous AI. They implement HAAS in a benchmark spanning software engineering and manufacturing tasks, and empirically evaluate performance, fatigue, and collaboration patterns under different governance settings.

Result: Empirically, they find: (1) Governance behaves as a tunable design variable, not a simple on/off switch; stronger governance systematically converts fully autonomous AI assignments into more supervised human-AI collaborations, with domain-dependent trade-offs in efficiency and oversight. (2) In manufacturing, tighter governance can both improve operational performance and reduce worker fatigue by buffering workload, challenging the assumption that governance is purely overhead. (3) No single governance level is universally best; as the contextual bandit learns, intermediate governance levels become increasingly competitive across contexts, indicating value in calibrated rather than minimal or maximal governance.

Conclusion: HAAS functions as a pre-deployment testbed for exploring, comparing, and auditing human–AI allocation strategies before organisations lock them into operations. The work demonstrates that governance should be designed and tuned as a continuous variable, that it can yield counterintuitive benefits (e.g., improved performance and reduced fatigue), and that adaptive learning within governed action spaces is crucial to finding effective human–AI collaboration modes across domains.

Abstract: Deciding how to distribute work between humans and AI systems is a central challenge in organisational design. Most approaches treat this as a binary choice, yet the operational reality is richer: humans and AI routinely share tasks or take complementary roles depending on context, fatigue, and the stakes involved. Governing that distribution -- balancing efficiency, oversight, and human capability -- remains an open problem. This paper presents Human-AI Adaptive Symbiosis (HAAS), an implemented framework for adaptive task allocation in software engineering and manufacturing. HAAS combines two coupled components: a rule-based expert system that enforces governance constraints before any learning occurs, and a contextual-bandit learner that selects among feasible collaboration modes from outcome feedback. Task-agent fit is represented through five auditable cognitive dimensions and a five-mode autonomy spectrum -- from human-only to fully autonomous -- embedded in a reproducible benchmark spanning both domains. Three empirical findings emerge. First, governance is not a binary switch but a tunable design variable: tighter constraints predictably convert autonomous AI assignments into supervised collaborations, with domain-specific costs and benefits. Second, in manufacturing, stronger governance can improve operational performance and reduce fatigue simultaneously -- a workload-buffering effect that contradicts the usual framing of governance as pure overhead. Third, no single governance setting dominates across all contexts; moderate governance becomes increasingly competitive as the learner accumulates experience within the governed action space. Together, these findings position HAAS as a pre-deployment workbench for comparing and inspecting human--AI allocation policies before organisational commitment.

### [221] [Standing on the Shoulders of Giants: Stabilized Knowledge Distillation for Cross--Language Code Clone Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02860)
*Mohamad Khajezade,Fatemeh H. Fard,Mohamed Sami Shehata*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They distill reasoning from a strong LLM into small open-source models and add stable classification heads to make cross-language code clone detection accurate, cheap, and reliable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-language code clone detection is hard because semantically equivalent programs in different languages look very different. Strong proprietary LLMs can reason about semantics but are expensive, closed, and unstable in their output formatting, while small open-source models are cheaper but weak at reasoning and giving consistently classifiable outputs. The authors want a practical, reproducible, and reliable solution for semantic clone detection across languages using compact models.

Method: They use DeepSeek-R1 as a teacher and perform knowledge distillation into smaller open-source student models (Phi3 and Qwen-Coder) for cross-language clone detection. They build reasoning-oriented synthetic training data from cross-language code pairs from Project CodeNet, then fine-tune the students via LoRA adapters. To stabilize outputs, they design forced-conclusion prompting plus two types of classification heads: a simple binary classification head and a contrastive classification head. They evaluate using predictive metrics and a new metric called response rate, and compare generation-style outputs vs classification-head outputs, including inference-time measurements.

Result: On several language pairs (Python–Java, Rust–Java, Rust–Python, Rust–Ruby), distilled models are more reliable (higher response rate and more stable mapping to clone labels) and often more accurate, especially when tested under distribution shift. Models with classification heads significantly reduce inference time compared to using the models in a generative manner.

Conclusion: Reasoning-oriented knowledge distillation from a strong LLM, combined with response stabilization techniques like tailored prompting and classification heads, turns compact open-source LLMs into practical, reliable tools for cross-language code clone detection, offering good accuracy, robustness under distribution shift, and faster inference compared with pure generation-based approaches.

Abstract: Cross-language code clone detection (X-CCD) is challenging because semantically equivalent programs written in different languages often share little surface similarity. Although large language models (LLMs) have shown promise for semantic clone detection, their use as black-box systems raises concerns about cost, reproducibility, privacy, and unreliable output formatting. In particular, compact open-source models often struggle to follow reasoning-oriented prompts and to produce outputs that can be consistently mapped to binary clone labels.
  To address these limitations, we propose a knowledge distillation framework that transfers reasoning capabilities from DeepSeek-R1 into compact open-source student models for X-CCD. Using cross-language code pairs derived from Project CodeNet, we construct reasoning-oriented synthetic training data and fine-tune Phi3 and Qwen-Coder with LoRA adapters. We further introduce response stabilization methods, including forced conclusion prompting, a binary classification head, and a contrastive classification head, and evaluate model behavior using both predictive metrics and response rate.
  Experiments on Python--Java, Rust--Java, Rust--Python, and Rust--Ruby show that knowledge distillation consistently improves the reliability of compact models and often improves predictive performance, especially under distribution shift. In addition, classification-head variants substantially reduce inference time compared to generation-based inference. Overall, our results show that reasoning-oriented distillation combined with response stabilization makes compact open-source models more practical and reliable for X-CCD detection.

</details>
